The General Possible Proposition: Propositional Status and Modality
Author argues whether al-mumkina al-amma is a proposition and discusses two interpretations of imkan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given modal proposition qualifies as a proposition (actual or potential) and identify whether it contains one judgment or two.

Core Idea
The general possible proposition is not a simple proposition; it is a proposition only potentially, but it bears modality actually.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial point in modal logic: does the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-amma) count as a genuine proposition? The author navigates two interpretations of possibility and shows that while it lacks a positive judgment on the agreeing side, it still qualifies as a proposition due to its components. More importantly, the modality of possibility is actually present, because negating necessity is a real quality of the relation. Understanding this helps avoid misclassifying propositions in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What exactly is being discussed? The 'general possible proposition' (al-mumkina al-amma). Identify its definition: it negates necessity from the opposite side, but does not include a positive judgment on the agreeing side.
The passage assumes the reader knows the basic definition.
2
Extract the two interpretations of imkan
Some interpret imkan as 'negation of impossibility from the agreeing side' (option A). Others interpret it as 'negation of necessity from the opposite side' (option B). The author shows they are equivalent: each entails the other.
Both interpretations reduce to the same meaning.
3
Analyze the objection and the author's response
An objector claims: choose option A, then the proposition includes a judgment on the agreeing side (negation of impossibility), so it is a simple proposition. The author responds: this is not a judgment of occurrence, only a statement of non-impossibility. Therefore it does not create a second judgment.
The objection is refuted by clarifying the nature of the judgment.
4
Assess the propositional status
The author cites investigators who say the possible proposition is only potentially a proposition (qadiya bi-l-quwwa), not actually (bi-l-fi'l). However, since logicians define proposition as subject, predicate, and relation, the possible proposition qualifies by having these components, even if the judgment is only potential.
The potentiality is based on suitability for a modality.
5
Determine the modality status
The author argues: if the possible proposition is a proposition potentially, then it actually has a modality, because the negation of necessity is a real quality that attaches to the relation. This is analogous to the absolute general proposition which also has a modality actually.
The modality is not potential; it is real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ظهر من ذلك ان الممكنة العامة غير مشتملة على الحكم في الجانب الموافق كما صرح به بعضهم فح لا يصح عدها قضية فضلاً عن كونها بسيطة أو موجهة وبالجملة ان قيل بأن الممكنة العامة مشتملة على الحكم في الجانب الموافق لم يصح عدها من البسائط لاشتمالها على حكمين مختلفين وان قيل بانها غير مشتملة عليه، لم يصح عدها قضية اعم من كونها بسيطة أو موجهة. لا يقال انا نختار الشق الأول ونقول : ان معنى الامكان سلب الامتناع عن الطرف الموافق كما فسره بذلك قوم، فح يصح عدها من البسائط لاشتمالها على الحكم في الجانب الموافق دون المخالف ولا يرد شيء لانانقول: لانسلم ان الامكان بذلك المعنى يدل على الحكم في الجانب الموافق، غاية الامرانه يدل على ان تلك النسبة المذكورة غير ممتعة و ليس هذا حكماً بوقوعها مع ان التحقيق: ان الامكان بهذا المعنى عين الامكان بذلك المعنى في المعنى فان سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف يلزمه سلب الامتناع عن الطرف الموافق وبالعكس. قال بعض المحققين ان الممكنة وان لم يكن فيها فى الطرف الموافق حكم اصلاً حتى يحتمل ان يكون واقعاً و ان لا يكون، بل انما حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة عن الطرف المتخالف فقط، فالمطلقة العامة هي القضية بالفعل والموجهة بالقوة والممكنة ليست قضية الا بالقوة فضلاً عن كونها موجهة بالفعل لكن لما كان مرادهم بالقضية اعم من ان تكون بالفعل أو بالقوة وقد صرحوا بان الموضوع و المحمول والنسبة بينهما قضية، الا ترى انهم عدوا المختطلات فى القضايا ولا حكم فيها بالفعل صح عدهم اياها في عداد القضايا بل عدهم اياها من الموجهات ايضاً انها بملاحظة صلاحيتها للجهة بالقوة وان لم تكن لهاجهة بالفعل كما عدوا المطلقة العامة منها بهذه الملاحظة أيضاً. و اقول : اذا ثبت كونها قضية بهذه الملاحظة أو لغيرها مما قيل امكن لنا ان نقول باشتمالها على الجهة بالفعل، ضرورة ان سلب الضرورة عن الطرف المخالف كيفية حاصلة للنسبة فانها قبل تقييدها بالامكان كانت محتملة لذلك و غيره حتى يمكن ان يكون طرفها المخالف ضرورياً واذا قيدت به علم ان ذلك الطرف ليس بضرورى ولاشك في كونها كيفية مخالفة لاصل الحكم وهذا نظير ما قلنا في المطلقة العامة، فافهم وتأمل
It has become clear from this that the general possible proposition does not include a judgment on the agreeing side, as some have stated, so it is not valid to count it as a proposition, let alone as a simple or modal proposition. In summary, if it is said that the general possible includes a judgment on the agreeing side, then it is not valid to count it among the simple propositions because it includes two different judgments; and if it is said that it does not include it, then it is not valid to count it as a proposition at all, whether simple or modal. It cannot be said: we choose the first alternative and say that the meaning of possibility is the negation of impossibility from the agreeing side, as some have interpreted it, so it would be valid to count it among the simple propositions because it includes a judgment on the agreeing side without the opposing side, and no objection arises. Because we say: we do not accept that possibility in that sense indicates a judgment on the agreeing side; at most it indicates that that mentioned relation is not impossible, and this is not a judgment of its occurrence. Rather, the verified truth is that possibility in this sense is identical to possibility in that sense in meaning, for the negation of necessity from the opposing side entails the negation of impossibility from the agreeing side, and vice versa. Some investigators have said that the possible proposition, even if it has no judgment at all on the agreeing side such that it might be actual or not, rather it only judges the negation of necessity from the opposite side, so the absolute general proposition is a proposition actually, and the modal proposition is potentially, and the possible proposition is not a proposition except potentially, let alone being modal actually. But since their intention with 'proposition' is more general than being actual or potential, and they have stated that the subject, predicate, and the relation between them constitute a proposition, do you not see that they counted the mixed propositions among the propositions even though there is no actual judgment in them? So it is valid to count them among the propositions, and indeed they counted them among the modals as well, by virtue of their suitability for the modality potentially, even if they do not have a modality actually, just as they counted the absolute general among them by this consideration as well. And I say: if it is established that it is a proposition by this consideration or by other reasons mentioned, we can say that it includes the modality actually, necessarily because the negation of necessity from the opposite side is a quality that attaches to the relation; for before it was restricted by possibility, that relation was open to that and other possibilities, so that its opposite side could be necessary. When it is restricted by it, it becomes known that that side is not necessary. There is no doubt that this quality is contrary to the original judgment. This is analogous to what we said about the absolute general. So understand and reflect.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion about the nature of the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-amma). The author first presents the problem: if the general possible proposition includes a judgment on the agreeing side (that the relation is not impossible), then it contains two judgments (the original relation and its negation of impossibility), so it would not be a simple proposition. If it does not include such a judgment, then it is not a proposition at all. The author then dialectically refutes an objection that the possible proposition could be considered simple by interpreting possibility as negation of impossibility from the agreeing side. The author responds that this interpretation does not actually give a positive judgment; it only states that the relation is not impossible. Then the author cites some investigators who clarify that the possible proposition is only a proposition potentially, not actually, because it lacks a real judgment. However, since the logicians define a proposition as subject, predicate, and relation, the possible proposition qualifies by having these components, even if the judgment is only potential. This is similar to how they treat mixed propositions (mukhtalitat) that have no actual judgment. Finally, the author argues that if the possible proposition is a proposition by this potential, then it actually has a modality, because the negation of necessity is a real quality attached to the relation. This is analogous to the absolute general proposition. The discussion shows the subtlety of classifying modal propositions.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الامكان
القضية
البسيطة

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is al-mumkina al-amma not a simple proposition if it includes a judgment on the agreeing side?
A) Because it has no judgment at all.
B) Because it contains two different judgments.
C) Because it is a modal proposition.
D) Because it is a general proposition.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition is not a simple proposition; it is only a proposition potentially, but it actually bears a modal quality (negation of necessity) that makes it a genuine modal proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The world is created by Allah.' Is this a general possible proposition? Explain your answer using the two interpretations of imkan discussed in the lesson.
?
In a fiqh context, the proposition 'Intoxicating drinks are forbidden' is a categorical proposition. If we negate the necessity of permissibility (i.e., it is not necessary that they are permissible), does this yield a general possible proposition? Justify.

The Eight Basic Modal Propositions: A Classification Tree
Author enumerates the eight basic modal propositions and explains their classification based on restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the classification tree of the eight basic modal propositions and name each one based on its modal restriction.

Core Idea
Eight modal propositions arise from two root restrictions: necessity and non-necessity.

Modal propositions add a restriction (jiha) to the relation between subject and predicate. The logicians have classified these restrictions into a tree that yields eight fundamental types. Understanding this tree is essential for working with modal syllogisms, which are widely used in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh to evaluate the certainty of claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main branches
Start from the modal restriction: either necessity (darura) or other-than-necessity. This is the first division.
2
Subdivide necessity
If the restriction is necessity, it either applies at a specific time or not (i.e., it holds for all times the subject exists).
3
Refine time-specific necessity
If it is time-specific, the time is either determined (absolute temporal) or undetermined (absolute diffusive).
4
Refine non-time-specific necessity
If not time-specific, the necessity holds either as long as the essence exists (absolute necessary) or as long as the description exists (general conditional).
5
Subdivide other-than-necessity
If the restriction is not necessity, it is either permanence (dawam) or something else (actuality or possibility). Permanence then divides into as-long-as-essence (absolute permanent) or as-long-as-description (general customary). The remaining two are absolute general (actuality) and general possible (possibility).
The classification ends with eight leaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : «القضاياء الثمانية المذكورة) وطريق انحصارها بها ان النسب المذكورة في القضية الموجهة اما ان تكون مقيدة بالضرورة ام بغيرها فان كانت مقيدة بها فاما ان تكون خاصة بوقت ام لا و على الأول اما ان يكون الوقت معيناً فهى الوقتية المطلقة او غير معين فهي المنتشرة المطلقة وعلى الثانى اما ان تكون مادام الذات فهى الضرورية المطلقة أو مادام الوصف فهى المشروطة العامة و ان كانت مقيدة بغير الضرورة فاما ان تكون بالدوام أو بغيره وعلى الأول اما ان يكون مادام الذات فدائمة مطلقة او مادام الموصف فعرفية عامة وعلى الثانى اما ان تكون مقيدة بالفعل فهى المطلقة العامة او بالامكان فهى الممكنة العامة. فهذه ثمانية. ولا يخفى ان حصر القيود اولاً بالاربعة غير عقلى وهكذا حصر اقسام الضرورة بالأربعة و الدوام بالاثنين و حصر كل واحد من الفعلية والامكان بواحد، فان الاقسام الأربعة المذكورة للضرورة تجرى في جمعيها بل يجرى فيها اقسام اخر ليس هنا موضع ذكرها لكن جرى عادة المنطقيين بالبحث عن احوال البسابط الثمانية المذكورة والمركبات السبعة التي يجىء ذكرها بل لا يبحث في مباحث التناقض و العكوس و الاقيسة من الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة ايضاً مع كونها من البسائط الثمانية المذكورة و لهذا تركها بعض المحققين من المنطقيين هنا ايضاً فتأمل.
His statement: 'The eight aforementioned propositions' and the way they are confined is that the relations mentioned in the modal proposition are either restricted by necessity or by something else. If they are restricted by necessity, then either they are specific to a time or not. In the first case, either the time is determined, so it is the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa), or it is undetermined, so it is the absolute diffusive (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa). In the second case, either it is as long as the essence exists, so it is the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa), or as long as the description exists, so it is the general conditional (al-mashruta al-amma). And if they are restricted by other than necessity, then either by permanence (dawam) or by other than that. In the first case, either as long as the essence exists, so it is the absolute permanent (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa), or as long as the description exists, so it is the general customary (al-'urfiyya al-amma). In the second case, either it is restricted by actuality, so it is the absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-amma), or by possibility, so it is the general possible (al-mumkina al-amma). So these are eight. And it is not hidden that the classification of restrictions firstly into four is not rational, and likewise the division of necessity into four, permanence into two, and each of actuality and possibility into one. For the four mentioned divisions of necessity run through all of them, and there are other divisions not to be mentioned here. But it is customary for logicians to investigate the states of the eight simple propositions and the seven compound propositions that will be mentioned, and they do not investigate in the topics of contradiction, conversion, and syllogisms the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive, even though they are among the eight simple propositions. For this reason, some verifying logicians omitted them here as well. So reflect.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage provides a complete enumeration of the eight basic modal propositions (al-basa'it al-thamaniya) that form the foundation of modal logic. The author explains the classification tree: The relation (nisba) in a modal proposition can be restricted by necessity or by something else. If by necessity, then either the necessity is time-specific (with determined or undetermined time, yielding al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa and al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) or not time-specific (as long as the essence exists, giving al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa, or as long as the description exists, giving al-mashruta al-amma). If restricted by something other than necessity, then either by permanence (dawam) – again either as long as the essence (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa) or as long as the description (al-'urfiyya al-amma) – or by actuality (al-mutlaqa al-amma) or possibility (al-mumkina al-amma). The author notes that this division is not rational in the sense that it is arbitrary, and that logicians typically only discuss these eight and the seven compound propositions (probably referring to mixed moods). They also note that two of these eight – the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive – are often ignored in later topics due to their limited use. The passage is a classic memorization anchor for students of logic.

Key Terms
القضاياء الثمانية
الضرورة
الدوام
المطلقة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Absolute temporal and absolute diffusive
C) General conditional and general customary
D) Absolute general and general possible

Key Takeaway
The eight basic modal propositions are a conventional classification; logicians often ignore the absolute temporal and diffusive in practice.

Condition for Compound Propositions and Refutation of a Misconception
Commentator explains condition for compound propositions and answers a misconception about labeling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that defines a compound proposition (qadiyya murakkaba) and explain why the labeling as affirmative or negative is based on the explicit part.

Core Idea
A compound proposition is one in meaning, not necessarily in wording, and its second part must not be independently expressed.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key condition for what makes a proposition compound (murakkaba): it must convey two rulings (affirmation and negation) without stating them as two independent propositions. He also addresses a potential misconception: if a compound proposition contains both affirmation and negation, how can we call it affirmative or negative? The answer, foreshadowed here, is that the labeling is based on the explicit part. This clarification is essential for correctly classifying propositions in modal logic, especially the special possibility proposition (al-mumkina al-khassa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
Locate the phrase 'بشرط أن لا يكون الجزء الثاني مذكوراً فيها بعبارة مستقلة'. This states that the second part (the negative or positive qualification) must not be presented as an independent expression. If it were, you would have two separate propositions, not one compound.
2
Distinguish meaning from wording
The commentator clarifies that the definition of a compound proposition applies to its meaning (the combination of two rulings) rather than its wording. This is to include al-mumkina al-khassa (special possibility), whose wording is not overtly composed of affirmation and negation but whose meaning is compound.
The special possibility proposition affirms possibility while implicitly negating necessity, making it compound in meaning.
3
Analyze the misconception
Someone might object: if a compound proposition contains both affirmation and negation, how can we label it as purely affirmative or negative? The comment foreshadows the answer: the criterion (al-ibra) is affirmation and negation with respect to the explicit first part of the proposition.
This anticipates the rule that the proposition is named after its explicit ruling.
4
Trace the response
The commentator references his later remark: 'والعبرة بالإيجاب والسلب...' meaning the labeling depends on the explicit part. Thus, a compound proposition like al-mumkina al-khassa is called affirmative because its explicit part is affirmative, even though its meaning includes negation.
This resolves the misconception by distinguishing between the surface form and the full meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٩٨) أي معناها وإنما قيل كذا ولم يقل لفظها، ليكون شاملاً للممكنة الخاصة، فإن لفظها غير مركب من الإيجاب والسلب كما سيصرح به. (محمد علي)
(١١) أي من الموجب والمسلوب. (عبد الرحيم)
(١٠٠) قوله: (بشرط أن لا يكون الجزء الثاني مذكوراً فيها بعبارة مستقلة): وذلك لأنه لو كان مذكوراً فيها بعبارة مستقلة يكون هناك قضيتان مستقلتان لا قضية واحدة مركبة. ثم ربما يتوهم أن القضية المركبة إذا اشتملت على الإيجاب والسلب فلا معنى لتخصيص بعضها بالموجبة وبعضها الأخرى بالسالبة. والجواب: ما أشار إليه المحشي فيما بعد بقوله: «والعبرة بالإيجاب والسلب...» (شيخ عبد الرحيم)
(98) i.e., its meaning, and it is said thus, and not its wording, so that it includes al-mumkina al-khassa, for its wording is not composed of affirmation and negation as will be clarified. (Muhammad Ali) (11) i.e., from the affirmative and negative. (Abd al-Rahim) (100) His saying: 'on condition that the second part is not mentioned in it with an independent expression': because if it were mentioned in it with an independent expression, there would be two independent propositions, not one compound proposition. Then perhaps one might imagine that if the compound proposition includes affirmation and negation, then there is no meaning to specifying some of them as affirmative and others as negative. And the answer: that which the commentator indicated later with his saying: 'And the criterion is affirmation and negation...' (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on a previous text defining compound propositions. The commentator begins by clarifying that the definition refers to the meaning (the combination of rulings) rather than the wording, because the wording of the special possibility proposition (al-mumkina al-khassa) is not overtly composed of affirmation and negation—yet it still qualifies as a compound proposition in meaning. The gloss then quotes the condition for a compound proposition: the second part must not be stated independently. If it were independent, you would have two separate propositions, not a single compound one. A hypothetical objection is raised: if a compound proposition already contains both affirmation and negation, why would we label it as affirmative or negative? The answer, which the commentator promises to give later, is that the labeling is based on the explicit part (the first part) of the proposition, not the implicit part. This anticipates the rule discussed in subsequent lessons.

Key Takeaway
A compound proposition is defined by its meaning (two rulings) not its wording, and its second part must be dependent on the first to remain a single proposition.

Two Types of Composition in Compound Propositions: Wording vs. Meaning
Author distinguishes composition in wording from composition in meaning, giving examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between composition in wording and composition in meaning in compound propositions, and identify each type in examples from modal logic in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A single qualifier can hide a compound meaning—special possibility is actually two negations in one.

In the previous lesson, you learned that a compound proposition has two implications (affirmation and negation). But how can this compound structure appear? The author explains that sometimes the composition is explicit in the wording, like the phrase 'not always', and other times it is hidden in a single qualifier that resolves into two restrictions, like 'with special possibility'. Let's see how to tell them apart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of composition
Recognize that composition in wording (tarkib lafzi) uses explicit separate phrases, while composition in meaning (tarkib ma'nawi) uses a single qualifier that implies multiple restrictions.
2
Analyze the wording example
Examine: 'Every human is laughing potentially not always'. The phrase 'not always' is an explicit addition, creating a compound of affirmation and negation in the wording itself.
3
Analyze the meaning example
Examine: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility'. The single qualifier 'with special possibility' actually negates necessity from both the affirmative and negative sides, making it compound in meaning.
4
Contrast the two types
Compare: in wording, the compound is overt (e.g., 'not always'); in meaning, the compound is implicit and requires logical analysis to uncover the two restrictions.

Source Text

(١٠١) قوله: (سواء كان في اللفظ): أي في لفظ القضية التي لها مفادان سلب وإيجاب تركيب كقولنا: "كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً" فقولنا "لا دائماً" وبه جاء التركيب لقضية الأصل إشارة إلى حكم سلبي، أي لأن الأصل موجب، أي لا شيء من الإنسان ضاحك بالفعل؛ لأنه سيجيء أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة. أو لم يكن في اللفظ تركيب بل كان مقيداً بلون من القيود فيدل إلى لون آخر كقولنا: "كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص" فهذا قيد واحد لا أنه قيد بعد قيد كما سبق في المثال السابق. ولكن هذا القيد الموجود المنحل إلى قيدين كان مكانه قيد بسيط وهو سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف، فلما أريد سلب الضرورة عن جانبي القضية الموافق والمخالف بدل هذا القيد البسيط وجيء بقيد يعطي سلب ضرورتي الجانبين الموافق والمخالف. فإن مفاد "كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام" أن سلب الكتابة عنه ليس ضرورياً، ولكن مفاد "كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص" أن سلب الكتابة عنه وهكذا إثباتها له جميعاً ليسا ضروريين.
(101) His saying: (whether in wording): i.e., in the wording of the proposition that has two implications, negation and affirmation, composed, like our saying: 'Every human is laughing potentially not always', so our saying 'not always' and with it came the composition of the original proposition indicating a negative ruling, i.e., because the original is affirmative, i.e., no human is laughing potentially; because it will come that 'non-permanence' indicates a general absolute. Or if there is no composition in the wording, but it is qualified by a type of restriction and indicates another type, like our saying: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility'. So this is a single restriction, not a restriction after restriction as in the previous example. But this existing restriction, which resolves into two restrictions, had in its place a simple restriction, which is the negation of necessity from the opposite side. When the negation of necessity from both sides of the proposition, the affirmative and negative, was desired, this simple restriction was replaced and a restriction was brought that gives the negation of the necessity of both sides, the affirmative and negative. For the import of 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' is that the negation of writing from him is not necessary, but the import of 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' is that the negation of writing from him and likewise its affirmation to him are all not necessary.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage expands on the earlier definition of a compound proposition by distinguishing two ways composition can occur. First, composition can be in the wording itself, as in the example 'Every human is laughing potentially not always'. Here the phrase 'not always' is explicitly stated, and it combines with the original affirmative proposition to yield a compound that includes both a positive ruling (laughing potentially) and a negative one (not always). The commentator notes that 'non-permanence' (la-dawam) refers to a general absolute proposition. Second, composition can be in the meaning without being overt in the wording, as in 'Every human is a writer with special possibility'. At first glance this seems to be a single qualification ('with special possibility'), but it actually resolves into two restrictions: the negation of necessity from the opposite side (the negative) and from the affirmative side as well. The simple restriction of 'special possibility' replaces a more complex one and serves to negate necessity from both sides. The difference between general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) and special possibility (al-imkan al-khass) is explained: in general possibility, only the negation of writing is not necessary; in special possibility, both the negation and the affirmation are not necessary. Thus, the single qualifier 'with special possibility' implies a compound of two negations of necessity, making it a compound proposition in meaning.

Key Terms
تركيب لفظي
تركيب معنوي
إمكان عام
إمكان خاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes composition in wording from composition in meaning in compound propositions?
A) Only composition in wording requires a second part.
B) Composition in wording uses explicit separate phrases, while composition in meaning uses a single qualifier that resolves into two restrictions.
C) Composition in meaning always uses the phrase 'not always'.
D) Composition in wording does not involve negation.

Key Takeaway
A compound proposition can have its composition either in explicit wording or in a single qualifier that implicitly contains multiple restrictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine the proposition 'Every adult Muslim is obligated to pray five times a day with general possibility.' Is the composition in wording or in meaning? Explain your answer by referring to the passage's analysis of general possibility.

Labeling Compound Propositions: The Rule of the Explicit Part
Author states the rule for naming compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly label any compound proposition as affirmative or negative by identifying its explicit first part.

Core Idea
A compound proposition takes its name from its explicit part, not its implicit qualification.

You may have wondered: if a compound proposition contains both an affirmation and a negation, how can we call it simply 'affirmative' or 'negative'? The classical logicians resolved this by pointing to the explicit first part (al-juz' al-sarih) as the deciding criterion. This lesson will equip you with that rule, which is essential for classifying propositions in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition
A compound proposition has two parts: the first explicit statement and the second qualification (the implicit part).
2
Locate the explicit part (al-juz' al-sarih)
The explicit part is the first part that directly affirms or negates the predicate. Disregard the qualification for now.
For example, in 'Every human is a writer unless they are illiterate', the explicit part is 'Every human is a writer'.
3
Check the quality of the explicit part
Determine whether that first part is affirmative or negative. Does it state that the subject has the predicate or does it deny it?
4
Label the compound accordingly
If the explicit part is affirmative, the whole compound is called affirmative. If it is negative, the compound is called negative. The implicit part (the qualification) does not affect the label.
The second part will have the opposite quality but the same quantity as the first part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و شعار القضية المركبة من ناحية الإيجاب والسلب بأن يقال سالبة أو موجبة، ملحوظ في جزئها الصريح وهو الأول، لا في جزئها الضمني وهو مفاد القيد، فإن كان الجزء الصريح موجباً سميت المركبة موجبة وإن كان سالباً سميت سالبة. (التقريب ص ٤٦-٤٧)
(١٠٢) فإن كان الجزء الأول موجباً كانت القضية موجبة وإن كان سالباً فسالبة، والجزء الثاني مخالف له في الكيف وموافق له في الكم. (شرح الشمسية ص ٩٥)
And the sign of the compound proposition with respect to affirmation and negation, in that it is called negative or affirmative, is considered in its explicit part, which is the first, not in its implicit part, which is the import of the qualification. So if the explicit part is affirmative, the compound is called affirmative, and if it is negative, it is called negative. (al-Taqrib, p. 46-47) (102) For if the first part is affirmative, the proposition is affirmative, and if it is negative, then negative, and the second part is contrary to it in quality and agreeing in quantity. (Sharh al-Shamsiyya, p. 95)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage provides the key rule for naming compound propositions. It states that the 'shiar' (sign or criterion) for determining whether a compound proposition is called affirmative or negative is based solely on its explicit first part (al-juz' al-sarih), not on the implicit part (al-juz' al-dimni) which is the meaning conveyed by the qualification. For example, if the first part says 'Every human is a writer...' (affirmative), the compound is affirmative even if the qualification implies a negation. The second part (the qualification) is always contrary in quality (if the first is affirmative, the second is negative, and vice versa) but agrees in quantity (both have the same subject/predicate terms). This rule resolves the earlier misconception: even though the compound contains both affirmation and negation, it takes its name from the explicit first part. The source quotes from both al-Taqrib and Sharh al-Shamsiyya, indicating this is a standard doctrine in logic texts.

Key Terms
شعار القضية
جزء صريح
جزء ضمني
كيف
كم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Based on the overall meaning combining both parts.
B) Based on the implicit part (the qualification).
C) Based on the explicit first part.
D) It depends on whether the second part is contrary in quantity.

Key Takeaway
The labeling of compound propositions is determined solely by the explicit first part, regardless of the qualification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Objection: Special Possibility is Compound in Meaning
Author refutes an objection that special possibility propositions are not compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the compound nature of al-mumkina al-khassa and defend it using the distinction between wording and meaning qualification.

Core Idea
A proposition can be compound in meaning even without compound wording.

Some commentators argued that al-mumkina al-khassa (special possibility) is not a compound proposition because it lacks explicit words like 'not always' or 'not necessary'. The author dismantles this objection by clarifying that qualification (takyid) covers both wording and meaning. Special possibility includes the meaning of negation of necessity, making it compound in meaning. This lesson walks through the author's two-pronged refutation and shows how this logical tool works in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the objection from 'some verifying commentators' that the earlier rule on compound propositions 'does not apply to special possibility'. What exactly are they denying?
They deny that special possibility has any qualification.
2
Author's First Argument: Qualification is General
The author argues that 'takyid' (qualification) applies to both wording and meaning, citing the commentator's own earlier statement: 'whether in wording there is composition or not'. This refutes the claim of no qualification at all.
Focus on the phrase 'المراد من التقييد أعم'.
3
Author's Second Argument: Special Possession Implicitly Contains Negation of Necessity
The author equates special possibility with general possibility plus negation of necessity (la-darura). For example, 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' means 'by general possibility, not by necessity'. This hidden qualification makes it compound.
Note that the negation of necessity is a qualification in meaning.
4
Apply the Principle to an Islamic Science Example
Now apply this distinction to a real juristic or theological example. For instance, in usul al-fiqh, a prohibition might be derived from the meaning of an 'illah (effective cause) even if the wording is general. The tool of 'qualification in meaning' is used to extend rulings.
Think of how the prohibition of wine is extended to all intoxicants based on the 'illah of intoxication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٠٣) قوله: واعلم أيضاً: أن القضية ...». قال بعض المحققين من المحشين (ره): لا يخفى أنه لا يصدق على الممكنة الخاصة.
وأقول: إن أراد أنه ليس فيها تقييد مطلقاً كما هو الظاهر، فهو غير مسلم، لأن المراد من التقييد أعم من أن يكون في اللفظ أم في المعنى كما يرشدك إليه قوله قبيل هذا: سواء كان في اللفظ تركيب ... أو لم يكن في اللفظ تركيب. وإن أراد أن التقييد فيها ليس بقيد اللادوام واللاضرورة، فهو أيضاً ممنوع، فإن الممكنة الخاصة كما سيجيء هي الممكنة العامة المقيدة بعدم ضرورة جانبها الموافق أيضاً، وهو معنى اللاضرورة. فإن قولنا "كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص" في معنى قولنا "كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام لا بالضرورة"، إلا أنهم قالوا: بالإمكان الخاص وأرادوا ذلك، مع أنا نقول: إن مراد المحشي أعم من أن يكون التقييد بلفظي اللادوام واللاضرورة وما يفيد مؤداهما. فتأمل. (محمد علي)
(103) His saying: 'And know also: that the proposition...' Some of the verifying commentators said: 'It is not hidden that it does not apply to al-mumkina al-khassa.' And I say: if he intends that there is no qualification in it absolutely, as is apparent, then it is not accepted, because the meaning of qualification is general, whether it is in the wording or in the meaning, as his saying shortly before guides you: 'whether in wording there is composition ... or not in wording there is composition'. And if he intends that the qualification in it is not by the expression of non-permanence (la-dawam) or non-necessity (la-darura), then that is also prevented, because al-mumkina al-khassa, as will come, is al-mumkina al-‘amma qualified by the negation of necessity of its affirmative side as well, and that is the meaning of la-darura. For our saying 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' is in the meaning of our saying 'Every human is a writer with general possibility not by necessity', except that they said: 'with special possibility' and intended that, although we say: that the intention of the commentator is general, whether the qualification is by the wording of la-dawam and la-darura or what conveys their meaning. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Muhammad Ali (the hashiya author) responding to an objection raised by some verifying commentators (al-muhakkikin). They claimed that the earlier rule about compound propositions does not apply to al-mumkina al-khassa (special possibility). The objection seems to be that special possibility propositions have no explicit qualification (takyid) in wording, so they are not compound. The author responds by dismantling this objection with two arguments. First, if the objector means that there is no qualification at all (absolutely none), that is false because 'qualification' (al-takyid) is general and includes qualification in meaning as well as in wording, as the commentator himself stated earlier ('whether in wording there is composition or not'). Second, if the objector means that the qualification is not by the specific expressions 'la-dawam' or 'la-darura', that is also false, because special possibility is essentially general possibility qualified by the negation of necessity from the affirmative side—which is exactly the meaning of 'la-darura' (non-necessity). For example, 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' is equivalent in meaning to 'Every human is a writer with general possibility, not by necessity'. The author concludes that the original commentator's intention was general, covering both explicit wording like 'not always' or 'not necessary' and any phrase that conveys the same meaning. The call to 'reflect' (tأمل) invites the reader to consider this nuanced position.

Key Terms
ممكنة خاصة (mumkina khassa): Special possibility proposition: equivalent to general possibility plus negation of necessity from both sides. [Note: Here it is argued to be compound in meaning; earlier traditions sometimes treated it as simple.]
تقييد (takyid): Qualification, whether in wording or in meaning. [Note: No significant shift.]
لاضرورة (la-darura): Negation of necessity; a modal qualifier indicating non-necessity. [Note: Standard term.]
لادوام (la-dawam): Negation of permanence; a temporal qualifier indicating non-permanence. [Note: Standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it uses the phrase 'not always' implicitly.
B) Because the qualification can be in meaning, not just in wording.
C) Because it is equivalent to general possibility.
D) Because it is not possible to be non-compound.

Key Takeaway
Special possibility propositions are compound because they contain a qualification in meaning, even if not in explicit wording.

Compound Proposition: Definition and Quality Rule
Author defines compound proposition and explains the rule for its quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound proposition and determine whether it is affirmative or negative based on its explicit part.

Core Idea
The quality of a compound proposition is judged by its first, explicit part, not the implicit qualifier.

Compound propositions (qadiyya murakkaba) are used in Islamic logic to express qualifications like 'not always' or 'not necessarily'. A common confusion is how to classify them as affirmative or negative when they contain both. This passage from the hashiya clarifies that the criterion is the explicit part, making it straightforward to evaluate such propositions in debates and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for a compound proposition
Find the condition that the second part must not be independently stated; otherwise it's two separate propositions.
Key phrase: 'على أن لا يكون الجزء الثاني مذكوراً فيها بعبارة مستقلة'
2
Distinguish explicit vs implicit composition
Look for two types: explicit qualifiers like 'not always' (تركيب في اللفظ) and implicit ones like 'with possibility particular' (تقييد بلون من القيود).
Implicit qualifiers resolve into a single restriction that implies negation of necessity on both sides.
3
Apply the quality rule
Check the sign (شعار) of the compound: if the explicit first part (الجزء الصريح) is affirmative, the compound is affirmative; if negative, it is negative. Ignore the implicit part (الجزء الضمني).
Example: 'كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً' – the first part 'كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل' is affirmative, so the compound is affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(100) قوله: (بشرط ان لا يكون الجزء الثانى مذكوراً فيها بعبارة مستقلة»: وذلك لانه لوكان مذكوراً فيها بعبارة مستقلة يكون هناك قضيتان مستقلتان لاقضية واحدة مركبة. ثم ربما يتوهم ان القضية المركبة اذا اشتملت على الايجاب والسلب فلامعنى لتخصيص بعضها بالموجبة وبعضها الأخرى بالسالبة. والجواب: ما اشار اليه المحشى فيما بعد بقوله: «والعبرة بالايجاب و السلب...» (101) قوله سواء كان في اللفظ» اى فى لفظ القضية التي لها مفادان سلب و ایجاب تركيب كقولنا كل انسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً فقولنا لا دائماً » و به جاء التركيب لقضية الاصل اشارة الى حكم سلبى اى لان الأصل موجب ) اى لا شيء من الانسان يضاحك بالفعل لانه سيجيء ان اللادوام اشارة الى مطلقة عامة « او لم يكن في اللفظ تركيب بل كان مقيداً بلون من القيود فيدل الى لون آخر كقولنا كل انسان کاتب بالامكان الخاص فهذا قيد واحد لا انه قيد بعد قيد كما سبق فى المثال السابق ولكن هذا القيد الموجود المنحل الى قيدين كان مكانه قيد بسيط وهو سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف فلما اريد سلب الضرورة عن جانبي القضية الموافق والمخالف بدل هذا القيد البسيط و جيء بقيد يعطى سلب ضرورتى الجانبين الموافق والمخالف، فان مفاد كل انسان كاتب بالامكان العام، ان سلب الكتابة عنه ليس ضرورياً ولكن مفاد كل انسان كاتب بالامكان الخاص، ان سلب الكتابة عنه وهكذا اثباتها له جميعا ليسا ضروريين. و شعار القضية المركبة من ناحية الايجاب والسلب بان يقال سالبة أو موجبة، ملحوظ في جزئها الصريح وهو الأول، لافى جزئها الضمنى و هو مفاد القيد فان كان الجزء الصريح موجباً سميت المركبة موجبة و ان كان سالباً سميت سالبة.
His saying: 'on condition that the second part not be mentioned in it with an independent expression' That is because if it were mentioned in it with an independent expression, there would be two independent propositions, not one compound proposition. Then perhaps it is imagined that the compound proposition, if it includes affirmation and negation, there is no meaning to classifying some of them as affirmative and others as negative. And the answer: what the commentator indicated later by his saying: 'The criterion is affirmation and negation...' His saying: 'whether there is in the expression' i.e., in the expression of the proposition that has two meanings, negation and affirmation, a composition, like our saying: 'Every human is actually laughing, not always.' So our saying 'not always' is the composition brought for the original proposition, an indication of a negative judgment, i.e., because the original is affirmative, i.e., no human is actually laughing, because it will be mentioned that 'non-permanence' indicates a general absolute. Or there is no composition in the expression, but it is restricted by some kind of restriction, so it indicates another kind, like our saying: 'Every human is a writer with possibility particular.' So this is one restriction, not a restriction after a restriction as in the previous example. But this existing restriction that resolves into two restrictions took the place of a simple restriction, which is the negation of necessity from the opposite side. So when it was desired to negate necessity from both sides of the proposition, the affirmative and the opposite, this simple restriction was replaced and a restriction was brought that gives negation of the two necessities from both sides, the affirmative and the opposite. For the meaning of 'Every human is a writer with possibility general' is that the negation of writing from him is not necessary. But the meaning of 'Every human is a writer with possibility particular' is that the negation of writing from him and likewise its affirmation for him are both not necessary. And the sign of the compound proposition with respect to affirmation and negation, by which it is called negative or affirmative, is considered in its explicit part, which is the first, not in its implicit part, which is the meaning of the restriction. So if the explicit part is affirmative, the compound is called affirmative; and if it is negative, it is called negative.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage defines the compound proposition and explains how its quality is determined. The author first states the condition that the second part must not be independently stated; otherwise it would be two propositions. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding: if a compound proposition contains both affirmation and negation, how can it be simply called affirmative or negative? The answer is that the criterion is based on the first part. He then explains the two types of compound propositions: those with explicit composition (like 'not always') and those with implicit composition (like 'with possibility particular'). In the latter, the single qualifier resolves into two implications: negation of necessity on both sides. Finally, he states the rule: the sign of the compound's quality is its explicit first part; if that is affirmative, the compound is affirmative; if negative, it is negative.

Key Terms
قضية مركبة
جزء صريح
جزء ضمني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By the explicit first part
B) By the implicit qualifier
C) By both parts together
D) By the presence of negation

Key Takeaway
The quality (affirmative/negative) of a compound proposition is always judged by its explicit first part, regardless of the implicit meaning of the qualifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether 'كل إنسان كاتب بالامكان الخاص' is a compound proposition, then classify it as affirmative or negative according to the rule.

The Possible Particular as an Implicit Compound
Author defends that the possible particular is a compound proposition despite not having an explicit two-part phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit restriction in the possible particular and demonstrate why it qualifies as a compound proposition despite its single-qualifier wording.

Core Idea
The possible particular is compound because it contains an implicit negation of necessity on both sides.

Some commentators doubted that the possible particular (ممكنة خاصة) is a compound proposition, since it seems to have only one qualifier. The passage shows that 'restriction' includes both explicit and implicit components, and that the possible particular is actually the possible general further restricted by the non-necessity of the affirmative side. Understanding this exposes the compound nature hidden in a simple phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Find the commentator's claim: he says the definition of 'compound proposition' does not apply to the possible particular because it lacks explicit restriction.
This doubt arises from a narrow view of 'restriction' as only explicit wording.
2
Expand the meaning of 'restriction'
The author argues that restriction can be either in the expression (لفظ) or in the meaning (معنى). The possible general is the base, and the possible particular adds the meaning of 'not necessary' on the affirmative side, even if not written separately.
This mirrors earlier discussion where composition may be verbal or semantic.
3
Break down the possible particular
The possible particular is defined as the possible general (affirming possibility on the negative side) plus the negation of necessity on the affirmative side. So it implicitly contains two negations: one for the negative side (from the possible general) and one for the affirmative side (the added qualifier).
Example: 'Every human is a writer with possibility particular' = 'Every human is a writer with possibility general, not by necessity'.
4
Recognize the implicit compound
Because the qualifier 'with possibility particular' implies a double negation of necessity (neither side is necessary), the proposition is logically compound — it combines two modal restrictions into one expression.
Thus the possible particular satisfies the broader definition of compound proposition.
5
Apply the principle to other cases
Generalize: whenever a single modal term implicitly contains multiple conditions, the proposition should be classified as compound. This principle helps classify modal propositions correctly in logic.
Example: 'necessarily impossible' or 'contingently possible' may also be compound.

Commentary
This passage defends the classification of the possible particular (ممكنة خاصة) as a compound proposition. The possible particular is actually the possible general (ممكنة عامة) further restricted by the negation of necessity on the affirmative side. So the qualifier 'with possibility particular' implicitly contains two negations of necessity. Therefore it qualifies as a compound proposition even if the wording is not obviously composite.

Understanding the Indication of 'Not Always'
Author explains that 'not always' indicates a general absolute proposition opposite in quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general absolute proposition indicated by the qualifier 'not always' (la dawam dhati) in a given proposition.

Core Idea
'Not always' signals a general absolute of opposite quality to the original.

This lesson examines the logical function of the temporal qualifier 'la dawam' (not always). The passage explains that this qualifier does not directly state a general absolute proposition but points to one as a necessary consequence. Understanding this relationship is crucial for correctly interpreting propositions in logic, especially when moving from necessary to absolute statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's quality and quantity
Note whether the original is affirmative or negative, universal or particular. This determines the indicated proposition.
For universal propositions, the indicated general absolute is also universal.
2
Recognize the function of 'not always'
The phrase 'la dawam' indicates a general absolute proposition of opposite quality as a necessary consequence, not an explicit assertion.
This is a semantic indication, not a direct statement.
3
Derive the indicated proposition
Change the quality (affirmative to negative or vice versa) while keeping the quantity the same.
For particular originals, the indicated remains particular.
4
Verify with the author's examples
Check that 'Every writer is a finger-mover while writing, not always' indicates 'No writer is a finger-mover in actuality.'
The examples illustrate the logical transformation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So if the original proposition is affirmative universal, then non-permanence indicates a negative universal general absolute, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not always,' i.e., no writer is a finger-mover in actuality. And if the original is negative universal, then non-permanence indicates an affirmative universal general absolute, like our saying: 'Necessarily, nothing of the writer is finger-still as long as he is a writer, not always,' i.e., every writer is finger-still in actuality. And similarly the matter in the particular. And the clarification of that will come from the commentator.

Commentary
This passage explains that the temporal qualifier 'not always' (لادوام) does not directly mean a general absolute proposition; rather, it indicates (اشارة) one. The author clarifies that the phrase signals a general absolute proposition that is opposite in quality to the original. For example, if the original is an affirmative universal, the indication is a negative universal. He provides examples: from an affirmative original 'Every writer is a finger-mover while writing, not always,' the indicated is 'No writer is a finger-mover in actuality.' Conversely, from a negative original, the indicated is affirmative. This shows that the qualifier's meaning is not the general absolute itself but entails it as a necessary consequence.

Essential Non-Permanence and Universal Absolute Propositions
Author explains that essential non-permanence indicates a universal absolute proposition with opposite quality and same quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the universal absolute proposition indicated by a given lā-dawām phrase, and explain why it is a necessary consequence rather than the explicit meaning.

Core Idea
Lā-dawām indicates a universal absolute proposition, not directly but as a logical entailment.

In classical Islamic logic, modal propositions like 'necessarily' and 'always' have specific negations. The term lā-dawām (non-permanence) appears in these contexts. This lesson unpacks how lā-dawām dhātī relates to universal absolute propositions, clarifying a subtle distinction between the explicit concept and its logical consequence. Understanding this mapping is crucial for translating modal statements into absolute ones, especially when evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences such as kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define lā-dawām dhātī and mutlaqa ʿāmma
Identify the two key terms: lā-dawām dhātī is the negation of permanence of a judgment as long as the subject's essence exists (e.g., 'not always'). Mutlaqa ʿāmma is a universal absolute proposition without modality, simply true.
2
Examine the mapping: from modal to absolute
The passage shows that if the original proposition is universal affirmative, lā-dawām indicates a universal negative absolute. If universal negative, it indicates universal affirmative. The quality flips, quantity stays same.
Example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always' becomes 'Nothing is a writer moving fingers actually'.
3
Distinguish explicit concept from necessary consequence
The explicit meaning of lā-dawām is merely the negation of permanence. The universal absolute proposition is a lāzim (necessary consequence) that follows from that negation. The author says 'indication' (ishāra) rather than 'meaning' (maʿnā) to emphasize this.
The passage notes: 'its explicit concept is the negation of permanence... and this is not the meaning of the universal absolute, but it entails it.'
4
Apply the rule
Given any modal proposition with lā-dawām, derive the universal absolute proposition by reversing quality and keeping quantity.
Practice with a particular example: if original is 'Some A is B necessarily as long as it is A, not always', then the indicated proposition is 'Some A is not B actually'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «فيكون اشارة الى قضية. ... : اى فيكون اللادوام الذاتى اشارة الى قضية مطلقة عامة مخالفة للاصل فى الكيف اى فى الايجاب والسلب وموافقة فى الكم اى في الكلية و الجزئية، فاذا كان اصل القضية موجبة كلية كان اللادوام اشارة الى مطلقة عامة سالبة كلية كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً اى: لاشيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الاصابع بالفعل، وان كان الاصل سالبة كلية كان اللادوام اشارة الى موجبة كلية كقولنا : بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً اى كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل وهكذا الأمر في الجزئية و سيأتي بيان ذلك من المحشى. ثم انما قال: فيكون إشارة اليها ولم يقل معناه هي كما قال فى اللاضرورة ان مفادها هي الممكنة العامة كما سيأتي. لانها ليست مفهومه الصريحى، بل لازمة له فان مفهومه الصريح ما ذكره المحشى اولا اعنى سلب الدوام عن الموضوع مادام الذات وليس هذا معنى المطلقة العامة البته لكنه يستلزمه فانه اذا حكم بسلب دوام الايجاب مادام الذات يلزمه فعلية السلب واذا حكم بسلب دوام السلب يلزمه فعلية الايجاب وكانه اشار الى هذه الدقيقة حيث أمر بالفهم.
His statement: 'So it is an indication to a proposition...' i.e., the essential non-permanence is an indication to a universal absolute proposition that is contrary to the original in quality, i.e., in affirmation and negation, and agreeing in quantity, i.e., in universality and particularity. So if the original proposition is a universal affirmative, then non-permanence indicates a universal absolute universal negative, as when we say: 'By necessity, every writer is a mover of his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' i.e., 'Nothing of the writer is a mover of his fingers actually.' And if the original is a universal negative, then non-permanence indicates a universal affirmative, as when we say: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' i.e., 'Every writer is still of fingers actually.' The same applies to particulars, and the commentator will explain that later. Then he said: 'So it is an indication to it' and did not say 'its meaning is it' as he said for non-necessity that its purport is the universal possible, as will come. This is because it is not its explicit concept, but rather a necessary consequence of it. For its explicit concept is what the commentator mentioned first, i.e., the negation of permanence from the subject as long as the essence exists, and this is not the meaning of the universal absolute at all, but it entails it. Because if one judges the negation of the permanence of affirmation as long as the essence exists, it entails the actuality of negation; and if one judges the negation of the permanence of negation, it entails the actuality of affirmation. And he seems to have indicated this subtlety when he commanded understanding.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of how the phrase 'lā-dawām dhātī' (essential non-permanence) functions in logic. It indicates a universal absolute proposition (mutlaqa ʿāmma) which differs from the original proposition in quality (i.e., if original is affirmative, the indicated proposition is negative, and vice versa) but agrees in quantity (i.e., both universal or both particular). The commentator gives examples: for a universal affirmative like 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer', the non-permanence yields 'Nothing is a writer moving fingers actually' a universal negative absolute. For a universal negative, the opposite. He then explains why the author says 'indication to' rather than 'its meaning is' – because the explicit concept of lā-dawām is simply the negation of permanence of the predicate as long as the subject exists; the universal absolute proposition is a necessary consequence (lāzim) of that, not its direct meaning. This subtlety is important for understanding how modalities relate to absolute propositions in the classical logical tradition.

مطلقة عامة

Key Takeaway
Lā-dawām dhātī indicates a universal absolute proposition with opposite quality and same quantity, but the absolute is a necessary consequence, not the explicit meaning.

The Separable Description in Special Conditioned Propositions
Commentator explains necessity of separable description in special conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a description in a conditioned proposition is separable (mufāriq) and explain why separability is required for non-permanence.

Core Idea
A special conditioned proposition's description must be separable; otherwise non-permanence becomes impossible.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a crucial condition for the special conditioned proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa): the description qualifying the subject must be separable from the subject's essence. Why? Because if the description were essential and permanent, then the predicate would also be permanent, contradicting the 'lā-dawām' (non-permanence) element that defines this proposition type. This logical nuance ensures that temporal conditioning remains meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Find the phrase 'المشروطة الخاصة' and note that it is equated here with the general conditioned proposition. Understand that a conditioned proposition predicates something of a subject only as long as the subject has a certain description.
The special conditioned proposition is a modal proposition involving a condition of non-permanence.
2
Extract the key condition
The commentator states that the description (waṣf) of the subject must be 'mufāriq' (separable) from the subject's essence. Locate the phrase 'يجب ان يكون وصفاً مفارقاً لذات الموضوع'.
Separable means the description can change or cease without altering the essence.
3
Understand why separability is necessary
If the description were permanent (dā'im) for the subject, then the predicate, being tied to that description, would also be permanent for the subject's essence. This would conflict with the meaning of non-permanence (lā-dawām). Trace the commentator's reasoning from 'اذا كان دائماً له' to 'مناف لمعنى اللادوام'.
Non-permanence implies that the predicate holds only during the presence of the description.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the proposition: 'Every traveler shortens his prayer while traveling.' The description 'traveler' is separable; a person can be a traveler at one time and not at another. If the description were essential, e.g., 'human' in 'Every human speaks as long as he is human', the predicate would be permanent, violating non-permanence.
Use examples from fiqh or kalam to practice identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «المشروطة الخاصة هي المشروطة العامة ... لا يخفى ان وصف الموضوع هنا و في العرفية الخاصة كما صرح به بعض المحققين في شرح الرسالة، يجب ان يكون وصفاً مفارقاً لذات الموضوع و الا لم يصح التقييد باللادوام الذاتى ضرورة انه اذا كان دائماً له و وصف المحمول دائم بدوام وصف الموضوع كان وصف المحمول دائماً لذات الموضوع و هذا مناف لمعنى اللادوام. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'The special conditioned proposition is the general conditioned proposition...' It is not hidden that the description of the subject here and in the special conventional proposition, as some investigators have stated in the commentary on the epistle, must be a description that is separable from the subject's essence; otherwise the restriction by essential non-permanence would not be valid. Necessarily, if it is permanent for it (the subject), and the description of the predicate is permanent with the permanence of the description of the subject, then the description of the predicate would be permanent for the subject's essence, and this contradicts the meaning of non-permanence. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage discusses the 'mashrūṭa khāṣṣa' (special conditioned proposition), a type of modal proposition used in logic. The commentator stresses a crucial condition: the description (waṣf) that qualifies the subject must be 'mufāriq' (separable) from the essence of the subject. If the description were essential (e.g., 'human' as a subject with description 'rational'), then any judgment using non-permanence would be impossible because the description is always present. For example, in 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is writing', the description 'writer' is separable from the essence of the person; one can cease to be a writer. If it were inseparable, the proposition would be perpetually true, contradicting the 'lā-dawām' element. This ensures that temporal conditioning is meaningful.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
وصف مفارق

Key Takeaway
For a special conditioned proposition to involve non-permanence, the subject's description must be separable from its essence; otherwise the predicate would be permanently true.

Composition of Special Conditioned and Identity of Lā-Ḍarūra with General Possibility
Author shows the composite parts of the special conditioned proposition and equates lā-ḍarūra with universal possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two composite parts of the special conditioned proposition in both its affirmative and negative forms, and explain why lā-ḍarūra is equivalent to the general possible proposition (mumkina ʿāmma).

Core Idea
Lā-ḍarūra is not a mere indicator; it is identical to general possibility.

In Islamic modal logic, the special conditioned proposition (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) is a crucial building block. Understanding its composite structure and the precise meaning of lā-ḍarūra (non-necessity) is essential for correctly interpreting arguments about necessity and possibility. This lesson unpacks the two parts of this proposition and reveals that lā-ḍarūra directly conveys general possibility, not just as a consequence.

Guided Path
1
Separate the two cases
Note that the special conditioned proposition has two forms: affirmative and negative. The passage treats them separately. For the affirmative, the first part is a temporal absolute affirmative with a restriction, and the second part is a universal absolute negative.
2
Extract the parts for the negative case
For the negative form, the first part is a temporal absolute negative, and the second part is a universal absolute affirmative. Recognize these as the two constituents that together form the special conditioned proposition.
3
Understand lā-ḍarūra definition
Lā-ḍarūra dhātiyya means negation of necessity from a relation as long as the subject's essence exists. According to the commentators, this negation directly implies the possibility of the opposite, because possibility is defined as the negation of necessity of the opposite side.
4
Connect lā-ḍarūra to general possible
Since possibility (imkān) is the negation of necessity of the opposite, the meaning of lā-ḍarūra is exactly the same as the judgment in the general possible proposition (mumkina ʿāmma). The commentators stress that lā-ḍarūra is identical to mumkina ʿāmma, not merely indicative of it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهى ان كانت موجبة فتركيبها من موجبة وقيد مطلقة اول و سالبة مطلقة عامة هي الجزء الثانى و ان كانت سالبة فتركيبها من سالبة وقتية مطلقة هى الجزء الاول و موجبة مطلقة عامة هي الجزء الثانى (شرح) (۱۱۱) ای فاذا كان معنى اللاضرورة الذاتية سلب الضرورة عن النسبة المذكورة مادام الذات فيكون هذااى معنى اللاضرورة الذاتية حكماً بامكان نقيض تلك النسبة المذكورة لان الامكان كما سبق هو الضرورة عن الطرف المقابل للحكم فيكون امكان نقيضها هو سلب الضرورة عنها لانها الطرف المقابل للنقيض . (محمد على) (۱۱۲) قوله لان الامكان هو سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل»: تعليل لقوله: «فيكون هذا حكماً بامكان نقيضها». و محصله : ان معنى اللاضرورة في الموجبة مثلاً هو: ان الايجاب لم يكن ضرورياً و اذا لم يكن الايجاب ضرورياً لكان هناك سلب ضرورة الايجاب وسلب ضرورة الايجاب ممكن عام سالب لان الامكان هو سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل فكلما كان الاصل موجبة فاللاضرورة اشارة الى ممكنة عامة لانها هي التي حكم فيها بسلب ضرورة الايجاب وقس على هذا الحال في السالبة (شيخ عبد الرحيم) (۱۱۳) قد عرفت ان الممكنة العامة عين معنى اللاضرورة الذاتية ولذا قال ان مقاد اللاضرورة هي الممكنة العامة ولم يقل فيكون اشارة اليها كما قال فى اللادوام. (میرزا محمد علی)
And if it (the special conditioned proposition) is affirmative, its composition is from an affirmative first part restricted by a first absolute (قيد مطلقة اول) and a universal absolute negative as the second part; and if it is negative, its composition is from a temporal absolute negative as the first part and a universal absolute affirmative as the second part. (Sharh) (111) i.e., if the meaning of essential non-necessity is the negation of necessity from the mentioned relation as long as the essence exists, then this—i.e., the meaning of essential non-necessity—is a judgment of the possibility of the contrary of that mentioned relation, because possibility, as has preceded, is the negation of necessity from the opposite side of the judgment. So the possibility of its contrary is the negation of necessity from it because it is the opposite side of the contrary. (Muhammad Ali) (112) His statement 'because possibility is the negation of necessity of the opposite side' is a justification for his statement 'so this is a judgment of the possibility of its contrary.' Its summary: the meaning of non-necessity in an affirmative, for example, is that the affirmation was not necessary. And when the affirmation is not necessary, there is a negation of the necessity of affirmation, and the negation of the necessity of affirmation is a universal possible negative, because possibility is the negation of necessity of the opposite side. So whenever the original is affirmative, non-necessity indicates a general possible, because it is that in which the negation of necessity of affirmation is judged. And apply the same to the negative. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (113) You already know that the general possible is identical to the meaning of essential non-necessity, and therefore he said that the purport of non-necessity is the general possible, and he did not say 'it is an indication to it' as he said for non-permanence. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This combined passage explains two related points: the composition of the special conditioned proposition (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and the meaning of lā-ḍarūra (non-necessity). First, from the Sharh: if the special conditioned proposition is affirmative, it consists of an affirmative first part (a temporal absolute affirmative) and a universal absolute negative as the second part. If negative, it consists of a temporal absolute negative first part and a universal absolute affirmative as the second part. Then, multiple commentators show that lā-ḍarūra (non-necessity) is equivalent to al-mumkina al-ʿāmma (general possibility). The reason is that possibility is defined as negation of necessity of the opposite side. So if we say 'It is not necessary that A is B', this means that the opposite (A is not B) is possible. In fact, the negation of necessity of the affirmation is exactly a universal possible negative. Thus lā-ḍarūra directly gives the meaning of a possible proposition, unlike lā-dawām which only indicates it as a consequence. This is why the author said '(the meaning of) lā-ḍarūra is the general possible'.

Key Terms
اللاضرورة الذاتية
الممكنة العامة
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Lā-ḍarūra is a cause of the general possible.
B) Lā-ḍarūra is identical in meaning to the general possible.
C) Lā-ḍarūra is a sign that the general possible is true.
D) Lā-ḍarūra is a different type of proposition from the general possible.

Key Takeaway
In the special conditioned proposition, lā-ḍarūra directly expresses the meaning of general possibility, not just as a consequence but as an identity.

Explaining Actuality: Existence at Some Time as Apposition
Author explains apposition and clarifies actuality's temporal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical and logical function of an explanatory apposition in a logical definition and explain why 'actuality' requires only existence at some time.

Core Idea
Actuality means existence at some time, not a specific tense.

In this brief passage, the commentator clarifies a key phrase in the definition of a categorical proposition. He explains that the phrase 'wa-wujūduhā fī waqt min al-awqāt' (and its existence at some time) serves as an explanatory apposition to 'faʿliyyat al-nisba' (actuality of the relation). This grammatical connection reveals an important logical point: the actuality of a relation does not require it to be past, present, or future; it simply requires that the relation exists at some time. Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusion when analyzing the temporal scope of propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apposition
Find the two phrases in the Arabic: 'wa-wujūduhā fī waqt min al-awqāt' and 'faʿliyyat al-nisba'. The commentator states they are in explanatory apposition (عطف تفسير).
The apposition means the second phrase explains the first.
2
Analyze the grammatical relation (tanāzuʿ)
The phrase 'fī waqt min al-awqāt' is grammatically related to both 'wujūduhā' and 'faʿliyyat al-nisba' via mutual conflict (تنازع). This indicates that the temporal component is shared by both expressions.
This reinforces that actuality and existence at some time are conceptually linked.
3
Extract the logical implication
The passage indicates that 'actuality' (بالفعل) does not mean specifically past, present, or future. It means existence in some manner, whether in time or not. Time is mentioned because the existence of the relation (e.g., in a proposition) is constituted by time.
This prevents misunderstanding that actuality requires a specific tense.
4
Relate to the broader text
The second gloss notes that the author omitted discussion of agreement in quantity here, relying on earlier and later passages. This shows internal coherence: definitions are built cumulatively.
Recognize that definitions in logic textbooks often refer back and forth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «ووجودها في وقت من الاوقات»، عطف تفسير بقوله: «فعلية النسبة» وقوله : فى وقت من الاوقات»، متعلق لهما على سبيل التنازع وفي هذا الكلام اشارة الى ان معنى بالفعل ليس هو اما ماض او حال او استقبال بل ما يوجد في الجملة سواء كان في زمان او لا، و انما ذكر الوقت لان وجود النسبة متقوم بالزمان. (ميرزا محمد علی) (۱١٤) قوله وموافقة له فى الكم، ... و انما تركه في هذا المقام اعتماداً على ما سبق في معنى اللادوام و و اتكالاً على ما سيأتي من المصنف في آخر الكلام. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'And its existence at some time' is an explanatory apposition to his statement: 'actuality of the relation'. And his statement: 'at some time' is related to both on the basis of mutual conflict (tanāzuʿ). In this statement there is an indication that the meaning of 'actually' is not that it is past, present, or future, but rather that it exists in some way, whether in time or not. And he mentioned time only because the existence of the relation is constituted by time. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (114) His statement: 'And agreeing with it in quantity' ... and he only omitted it here relying on what preceded in the meaning of non-permanence and depending on what will come from the author at the end of the discourse. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage contains two brief glosses. The first (115) explains that the phrase 'wa-wujūduhā fī waqt min al-awqāt' (and its existence at some time) is an explanatory apposition to 'faʿliyyat al-nisba' (actuality of the relation). The commentator notes that the word 'at some time' grammatically relates to both phrases (tanāzuʿ). More importantly, this indicates that 'actually' (bi al-fiʿl) does not mean specifically past, present, or future; rather, it means existence in some manner, but time is mentioned because the relation (like writing or moving fingers) necessarily occurs in time. The second gloss (114) remarks that the author omitted mentioning agreement in quantity for non-necessity (as he did for non-permanence), relying on earlier discussions and later ones. This shows internal coherence of the text.

عطف تفسير

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'existence at some time' clarifies that actuality of the relation does not require a specific tense, only existence at some point.
