The Absolute Necessary Proposition: Invalid Modal Restrictions
Author explains invalid modal restrictions for absolute necessary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute necessary proposition and determine which modal qualifiers are incompatible with it, based on the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity cannot coexist with any qualifier that negates necessity.

In this passage, the author explains that the absolute necessary proposition — one that holds for the subject as long as the essence exists — cannot be qualified with essential non-necessity or descriptive non-necessity. This is because such qualifiers directly contradict the very nature of absolute necessity. Understanding this helps us see how modal consistency is maintained in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
From the passage, extract the definition of the absolute necessary proposition: it asserts the predicate holds necessarily for the subject whenever the subject exists.
This is the baseline for evaluating qualifiers.
2
List the invalid qualifiers
The author mentions four: essential non-necessity, descriptive non-necessity, essential non-permanence, and descriptive non-permanence. Note that all negate some aspect of necessity or permanence.
These are the modes that cannot be combined.
3
Understand the reasoning per qualifier
For each, explain why it contradicts: essential non-necessity contradicts the essence-based necessity; descriptive non-necessity fails because the times of attributes are within the times of the essence; non-permanence contradicts the permanence inherent in necessity.
The core principle: necessity implies impossibility of separation.
4
Apply the principle to a new example
Given the proposition 'Every human is necessarily an animal', determine why it cannot be qualified with essential non-necessity. Use the same reasoning: the predicate is necessary for the essence, so negating that necessity is contradictory.
This tests comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرورية المطلقة لا يصح تقييد الضرورية المطلقة باللاضرورة الذاتية لان قيدها المذكور يناقض الاصل المقيد به، فان الضرورة الذاتية واللاضرورة الذاتية تتمانعان وهكذا لا يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية، لان المحمول اذا ثبت انتسابه للموضوع بالضرورة فى بيع اوقات الذات، فهو ثابت له مع جميع أوصافه لان اوقات أوصافه من اوقات ذاته لا تبارحها و هكذا لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى والوصفى لان ضرورة الانتساب دوام مع استحالة انفكاك، فضرورة انتساب المحمول لذات الموضوع مادامت الذات موجودة ينافيها لادوام الانتساب بحسب الذات وبحسب الوصف ايضاً لان الوصف من شؤن الذات ووقته من اوقاتها. المشروطة العامة - لا يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية لتنافى الضرورة بحسب الوصف واللاضرورة بحسب الوصف، ولكن يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية فان الضرورة الوصفية تحكم باستحالة انفكاك المحمول عن الموضوع مادام الوصف و هذا الوصف قديكون وصفاً مفارقاً يزول و يثبت مكانه غيره وذلك كوصف الكتابة والقيام و القعود و نظائرها، واللاضرورة الذاتية تقول : ان انتساب المحمول المذكور ليس ضرورياً للموضوع مادامت ذاته و قد عرفت ان وقت الوصف بعض من وقت الذات، فاللاضرورة الذاتية تشير الى مازاد من اوقات الذات عن اوقات الوصف، و اذاصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية صح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى فان اللادوام الذاتى فيها معناه ان انتساب المحمول فيها للموضوع ليس دائماً مادامت ذات الموضوع موجودة، فان اوقات الذات تزيد على اوقات الوصف العنوانى المفارق كالكتابة ونحوها فالضرورة بحسب الوصف انما تتناول وقتاً محدوداً من اوقات الذات والأوصاف اللازمة للذات كالانسانية للانسان والحيوانية للحيوان ونظائر ذلك ، لا يقال لها: اوصاف عنوانية الابضرب من التكلف، وانما هي عناوين انتزاعية من مقام المعنون ولذلك تدوم بدوامه و تنهدم با نهدامه. ولكن لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الوصفى لانه يناقض الضرورة الوصفية بوضوح.
The absolute necessary proposition: It is not valid to qualify the absolute necessary with essential non-necessity because its mentioned condition contradicts the principle qualified by it, for essential necessity and essential non-necessity are mutually exclusive. Likewise, it is not valid to qualify it with descriptive non-necessity, because if the predicate's attribution to the subject is established by necessity in some times of the essence, it is established for it with all its attributes, because the times of its attributes are from the times of its essence, not leaving it. Likewise, it is not valid to qualify it with essential non-permanence and descriptive non-permanence, because the necessity of attribution is permanence with impossibility of separation. So the necessity of the predicate's attribution to the subject's essence as long as the essence exists contradicts non-permanence of attribution according to essence and also according to description, because the description is among the affairs of the essence and its time is from its times. The general conditioned proposition: It is not valid to qualify it with descriptive non-necessity because of the conflict between necessity according to description and non-necessity according to description. But it is valid to qualify it with essential non-necessity, for the descriptive necessity rules the impossibility of the predicate's separation from the subject as long as the description exists, and this description may be a separable description that departs and another replaces it, such as the description of writing, standing, sitting and their likes. Essential non-necessity says that the attribution of the mentioned predicate is not necessary for the subject as long as its essence exists, and you know that the time of the description is part of the time of the essence. So essential non-necessity points to the surplus of the times of the essence over the times of the description. And since qualifying it with essential non-necessity is valid, qualifying it with essential non-permanence is valid, because essential non-permanence in it means that the predicate's attribution in it is not permanent as long as the subject's essence exists. For the times of the essence exceed the times of the descriptive separable attribute like writing and the like. So necessity according to description only occupies a limited time of the times of the essence. And attributes necessary to the essence, like humanity for man and animality for animal and their likes, are not called descriptive attributes except with some effort; rather they are abstracted notions from the place of the qualified, and therefore they endure with its endurance and vanish with its vanishing. But it is not valid to qualify it with descriptive non-permanence because it clearly contradicts descriptive necessity.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the modal restrictions for two types of categorical propositions: the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) and the general conditional (al-mashruta al-‘amma). The author, in the style of a hashya, expands on why certain modal qualifiers are invalid. For the absolute necessary, the attribute is always true of the subject as long as the essence exists, so any qualifier that negates necessity or permanence would contradict the fundamental meaning. For the general conditional, the necessity is tied to a separable description (like writing). Here, essential non-necessity is allowed because the description may vanish, but descriptive non-necessity is not allowed because it would directly oppose the conditional necessity. The author uses examples from everyday attributes (writing, standing) to illustrate separable descriptions, and essential attributes (humanity, animality) to contrast with separable ones. The reasoning follows the principle that contradictions cannot coexist; thus, if a proposition is necessary in some way, it cannot be qualified with its negation in the same respect.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
اللاضرورة الذاتية
اللاضرورة الوصفية
اللادوام الذاتى
اللادوام الوصفى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it invalid to qualify the absolute necessary proposition with essential non-necessity?
A) Essential non-necessity is a different modal category.
B) Essential necessity and essential non-necessity are mutually exclusive.
C) The absolute necessary has no essence.
D) The qualifier is redundant.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition is defined by necessity for the subject's essence, so any qualifier that negates that necessity is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every human is necessarily rational' (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa). Explain why it cannot be qualified with 'non-necessarily rational by description' (لا ضرورة وصفية). Use the reasoning from the passage.

The General Conditional Proposition: Valid and Invalid Modal Restrictions
Author explains valid and invalid modal restrictions for general conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid modal qualifiers for the general conditional proposition, and explain why essential non-necessity is allowed but descriptive non-necessity is not.

Core Idea
General conditional necessity allows essential non-necessity but not descriptive non-necessity.

In this part of the passage, the author turns to the general conditional proposition, which asserts necessity as long as a certain description holds. He explains that it cannot be qualified with descriptive non-necessity, but it can with essential non-necessity. This is because the description may be separable, so the necessity is limited to the time of the description, allowing the possibility of non-necessity at other times of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Define the general conditional
From the passage, the general conditional (المشروطة العامة) is a proposition where the predicate holds necessarily for the subject as long as a certain descriptive attribute exists (e.g., 'writer moves fingers').
The description is separable, not essential.
2
Identify the invalid qualifier
Descriptive non-necessity is invalid because it directly contradicts the necessity that is tied to the description.
This would be a straightforward contradiction.
3
Identify the valid qualifier
Essential non-necessity is valid because the description's time is only part of the essence's time; the proposition is necessary only during the description's presence, so it is not necessary for the essence as a whole.
The author illustrates with separable attributes like writing, standing.
4
Extend to non-permanence qualifiers
Essential non-permanence is also valid because the necessity is not permanent over all times of the essence, while descriptive non-permanence is invalid as it negates the necessity during the description's time.
This follows from the same reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why is essential non-necessity (اللاضرورة الذاتية) valid for the general conditional but not for the absolute necessary?
A) The general conditional has a separable description, so its necessity is limited.
B) Essential non-necessity only applies to conditional propositions.
C) The absolute necessary lacks a description.
D) The general conditional is weaker in modality.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional's necessity is tied to a separable description, so essential non-necessity is valid but descriptive non-necessity is not.

Temporal and Spreading Propositions: Modal Qualifications
Author explains that absolute temporal and indefinite spreading propositions can be qualified with certain modal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify absolute temporal and spreading propositions and determine which modal qualifications (essential/descriptive non-necessity/non-permanence) apply to them.

Core Idea
Temporal and spreading propositions outlast their necessity, enabling essential and descriptive modal qualifications.

In classical Islamic logic, propositions are often qualified by modalities like necessity and permanence. This passage focuses on two types of absolute propositions: the temporal (specific time) and the spreading (indefinite time). You will learn that both can be restricted by essential and descriptive non-necessity and non-permanence, based on the relationship between the subject's essence and the predicate's time. This understanding is crucial for correctly applying modal logic in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is absolute temporal (specific, determined time) or absolute spreading (indefinite time within the subject's existence).
The source distinguishes 'al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa' and 'al-muntashira al-mutlaqa'.
2
Understand the time restriction
In both types, the predicate holds only at a particular time segment. However, the subject's essence (dhat) has a broader temporal existence: it also exists before and after that segment.
For example, 'the moon is eclipsed' only at the moment of opposition, but the moon exists at other times.
3
Apply essential modal qualifications
Because the essence outlasts the time of necessity, we can say that the proposition is not essentially necessary (la darura dhatiyya) and not essentially permanent (la dawam dhatiyy) – the predicate is not true for the essence at all times.
The author says 'the condition of essential non-necessity points to the surplus of times of the essence over that specific time'.
4
Apply descriptive modal qualifications
The description used (e.g., moonness) is an essential attribute (wasf lazim) that always accompanies the essence. Therefore, descriptive non-necessity and non-permanence reduce to essential ones – they are identical.
The description is abstracted from the essence, so its modal status mirrors the essence's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقتية المطلقة يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية لان الضرورة فيها في وقت معين و اوقات ذات الموضوع تزيد على هذه القطعة اعنى الوقت المعين، فقيد اللاضرورة الذاتية يشير الى مازاد من اوقات الذات عن ذلك الوقت المعين وهكذا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى بالملاك المذكور وهكذا باللاضرورة و اللادوام الوصفيين، لان الوصف الذى يراعى فى الوقتية المطلقة وصف لازم منتزع عن ذات الموضوع كالقمرية من القمر و الانسانية من الانسان و هذه الاوصاف كما قرأت باقية ببقاء الذات، فعنى لاضرورتها ولا دوامها هو عين معنى لا ضرورة الذات ولا دوامها بلا تفاوت اصلا. المنتشرة المطلقة - وهي كالوقتية المطلقة ولكن وقت الضرورة فيها مردد في جملة اوقات الذات فكل ما قيل هناك ، يقال هنا بلا أدنى تفاوت فتتقيد المنتشرة المطلقة باللاضرورتين الذاتية و الوصفية و باللادوامين الذاتي و الوصفى.
The absolute temporal proposition: It is valid to qualify it with essential non-necessity because the necessity in it is at a specific time, and the times of the subject's essence exceed this segment, that is, the specific time. So the condition of essential non-necessity points to the surplus of the times of the essence over that specific time. Likewise, it is valid to qualify it with essential non-permanence by the same measure, and likewise with descriptive non-necessity and descriptive non-permanence, because the description that is taken into account in the absolute temporal proposition is an essential description abstracted from the subject's essence, like moonness from the moon and humanity from man. And these descriptions, as you have read, remain with the remainder of the essence. So the meaning of its non-necessity and its non-permanence is precisely the meaning of essential non-necessity and non-permanence with no difference at all. The absolute spreading proposition: It is like the absolute temporal proposition, but the time of necessity in it is indefinite among the set of times of the essence. So everything said there is said here without the slightest difference. So the absolute spreading proposition is qualified by essential and descriptive non-necessity and by essential and descriptive non-permanence.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the modal restrictions for the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa) and the absolute spreading (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) propositions. The author shows that these propositions can be qualified with both essential and descriptive non-necessity and non-permanence. The reasoning is that in the temporal proposition, necessity is limited to a specific time, but the essence of the subject exists at other times, so essential non-necessity applies to those other times. The description involved (like moonness) is an essential attribute that always accompanies the essence, so descriptive non-necessity is essentially the same as essential non-necessity. The spreading proposition is treated identically because its indefinite time still allows the same reasoning: the essence's times extend beyond the indefinite time of necessity. The author uses examples from natural kinds (moon, man) to illustrate essential attributes.

Key Terms
الوقتية المطلقة
المنتشرة المطلقة
الوصف اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the essence's times are greater than the specific time of necessity.
B) Because temporal propositions have no essence.
C) Because the predicate is not necessary at any time.
D) Because essential non-necessity is the same as temporal non-necessity.

Key Takeaway
Absolute temporal and spreading propositions can be qualified by essential and descriptive non-necessity and non-permanence because the subject's essence outlasts the time of necessity.

Modal Restrictions on Perpetual and Customary Propositions
Author explains which modal qualifiers are valid for perpetual and customary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the absolute perpetual proposition from the general customary proposition and determine which modal qualifiers (non-necessity, non-permanence) are valid for each based on their time domains.

Core Idea
A perpetual proposition's time domain is the essence; a customary's domain is a description—this determines which modal restrictions apply.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by their time domains. The absolute perpetual proposition (al-da’ima al-mutlaqa) asserts the predicate holds at all times of the subject's existence, while the general customary proposition (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma) holds only as long as a descriptive attribute applies. These different bases—essence versus description—determine which modal qualifiers like non-necessity (lā ḍarūra) and non-permanence (lā dawām) can be attached without contradiction. This lesson unpacks the rules for each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
First, determine whether the proposition is perpetual (predicate holds at all times of the subject's existence) or customary (predicate holds only while a descriptive attribute exists).
The key is the time domain: essence for perpetual, description for customary.
2
Understand the time domain
For perpetual, the times are all moments of the essence. For customary, the times are only those when the descriptive attribute applies—hence fewer than the essence's times.
This is why customary propositions can accept essential qualifiers that refer to times outside the description.
3
Check compatibility of modal operators
Non-necessity (negation of impossibility of separation) is compatible with permanence because it only denies necessity, not constancy. Non-permanence directly contradicts permanence, so it is invalid for perpetual propositions.
The text: 'permanence does not prevent the negation of impossibility of the predicate's separation from the subject, which is the meaning of non-necessity.'
4
Apply the specific rules
For perpetual: only non-necessity (essential or descriptive) is valid; any form of non-permanence is invalid. For customary: essential non-necessity, essential non-permanence, and descriptive non-necessity are valid; but descriptive non-permanence is invalid because it directly contradicts the descriptive permanence.
Customary propositions allow essential non-permanence because the essence's times exceed the description's times, so the predicate might not hold at all essential times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدائمة المطلقة - لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتي، لانه نقيض صريح للدوام الذاتي و هكذا لا تقيد باللادوام الوصفى لان اوقات الوصف من جملة اوقات الذات وتقيد باللاضرورتين الذاتية والوصفية، فان الدوام لا يمانع سلب استحالة انفكاك المحمول عن الموضوع الذي هو مفاد اللاضرورة واللاضرورة الوصفية هنا قرينة اللاضرورة الذاتية لان وصفها لازم منتزع لامفارق عنوانى. العرفية العامة يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية واللادوام الذاتى لما عرفت من ان اوقات الذات اكثر من اوقات الوصف العنواني، فاللاضرورة واللادوام الذاتيان يشيران الى مازاد من اوقات عن اوقات الوصف، وهكذا يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية فان الدوام بحسب الوصف لا يمانع سلب استحالة انفكاك المحمول عن الموضوع باعتبار وصفه ولكن تقييدها باللادوام الوصفى ممنوع، لانه نقيض صريح للدوام الوصفى.
The absolute perpetual proposition: It is not valid to qualify it with essential non-permanence because it is a clear contradiction of essential permanence. Likewise, it is not qualified with descriptive non-permanence because the times of the description are part of the times of the essence. It is qualified with essential and descriptive non-necessity, for permanence does not prevent the negation of impossibility of the predicate's separation from the subject, which is the meaning of non-necessity. And descriptive non-necessity here is a clue for essential non-necessity because its description is an essential abstracted attribute, not a separable descriptive one. The general customary proposition: It is valid to qualify it with essential non-necessity and essential non-permanence because you know that the times of the essence are more than the times of the descriptive attribute. So essential non-necessity and non-permanence point to the surplus of times over the times of the description. Likewise, it is valid to qualify it with descriptive non-necessity, for permanence according to description does not prevent the negation of impossibility of the predicate's separation from the subject with respect to its description. But qualifying it with descriptive non-permanence is prohibited because it is a clear contradiction of descriptive permanence.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage explains the modal restrictions for the absolute perpetual (al-da’ima al-mutlaqa) and the general customary (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma) propositions. The perpetual proposition asserts that the predicate holds at all times of the subject's existence; therefore, any qualifier that negates permanence is invalid because it directly contradicts the meaning. However, non-necessity is allowed because it does not contradict permanence—non-necessity only negates necessity, not permanence. The customary proposition is based on a descriptive attribute, so its times are only those when the description applies. Since the essence exists beyond those times, essential non-necessity and non-permanence are valid. Descriptive non-necessity is also valid because it only denies necessity based on the description, not permanence. But descriptive non-permanence would directly contradict the permanence of the predicate during the description, so it is prohibited.

Key Terms
الدائمة المطلقة (al-da’ima al-mutlaqa): A proposition that asserts the predicate holds for the subject at all times of its existence.
العرفية العامة (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma): A proposition that asserts the predicate holds for the subject as long as a certain descriptive attribute exists, which is customary for that subject.
اللادوام الذاتي (la-dawam al-dhati): Non-permanence with respect to the essence; the predicate does not hold at all times of the subject's existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because permanence does not negate the possibility of non-necessity.
B) Because perpetual propositions have no essence.
C) Because non-necessity is stronger than permanence.
D) Because the predicate is not necessary in perpetual propositions.

Key Takeaway
The validity of modal qualifications depends on whether the time domain is essential (perpetual) or descriptive (customary); non-necessity is always compatible, but non-permanence is only compatible when the time domain is broader than the description.

Why General Absolute and Possible Propositions Accept All Four Modal Qualifiers
Author states that general absolute and general possible propositions can be qualified with all four modal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the general absolute and general possible propositions can be qualified with all four modal conditions, and distinguish between the two meanings of possibility.

Core Idea
General absolute and general possible propositions are flexible enough to accommodate all four modal qualifiers.

In modal logic, propositions can be qualified by necessity, non-necessity, permanence, and non-permanence. The author of this passage explains that two basic types – the general absolute and the general possible – each can take all four qualifiers, but for different reasons. Understanding this flexibility is crucial for constructing precise syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general absolute proposition
The general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma) merely asserts that the predicate applies to the subject at some indefinite time during the subject's existence. It does not specify necessity or permanence. Because the subject's essence has many temporal instances beyond that indefinite moment, the proposition can be qualified with any of the four modal conditions (e.g., necessary, possible, permanent, non-permanent).
The author notes that the subject's description is abstracted and necessary, so it behaves like the essence itself.
2
Understand the general possible proposition
The general possible (al-mumkina al-‘amma) asserts that the predicate is possible for the subject, i.e., neither necessary nor impossible. This meaning is compatible with necessity and non-necessity, and with permanence and non-permanence, both according to description and essence. Thus it can also take all four qualifiers.
But see next step for a nuance.
3
Examine the two meanings of possibility
Possibility can mean either (1) mere compatibility (i.e., not impossible) or (2) negation of necessity (i.e., not necessary). Under the second meaning, possibility is incompatible with necessity. So when qualifying a possible proposition with necessity, the intended meaning matters. The author warns that one must be careful which meaning is intended.
The text says: 'if we say that the meaning of possibility is the negation of necessity, then it is not compatible with necessity but does not prevent permanence.'
4
Apply the distinction to modal qualification
If possibility means mere compatibility, then a possible proposition can be qualified as necessary (e.g., 'it is necessarily possible'?) Actually, the text says the meaning of possibility as compatibility 'is compatible with necessity' meaning the proposition's being possible does not contradict its being necessary. But if possibility means negation of necessity, then you cannot also say it is necessary. However, you can still say it is permanent (always true) because that does not entail necessity.
This subtlety is important in theological arguments where propositions about God's attributes might be qualified as necessary or possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلقة العامة يصح تقييدها بالقيود الاربع، لانها انما تفيد ان انتساب المحمول للموضوع ثابت في وقت غير معين و اوقات الذات أكثر من هذه القطعة الزمنية المرددة ووصف موضوعها انتزاعی لازم، فحكمه حكم الذات كما عرفت ذلك مكرراً. الممكنة العامة - كذلك يصح تقييدها بالقيود الاربع، لانها انما تفيد ان انتساب المحمول للموضوع جائز و هذا المعنى يلائم الضرورة واللاضرورة و الدوام واللادوام بحسب الوصف و بحسب الذات و ان قلنا ان معنى الامكان هو سلب الضرورة، فهو لا يلائم الضرورة ولا يمانع الدوام وعليه فيلزم التفطن لمعنى الامكان و ان المراد به ای معنى من هذين المعنيين.
The general absolute proposition: It is valid to qualify it with the four conditions, because it only conveys that the predicate's attribution to the subject is established at an indefinite time, and the times of the essence are more than this indefinite temporal segment, and its subject's description is an abstracted necessary attribute, so its ruling is the ruling of the essence, as you have repeatedly known. The general possible proposition: Likewise, it is valid to qualify it with the four conditions, because it only conveys that the predicate's attribution to the subject is possible, and this meaning is compatible with necessity and non-necessity and permanence and non-permanence according to description and according to essence. And if we say that the meaning of possibility is the negation of necessity, then it is not compatible with necessity but does not prevent permanence. Accordingly, one must pay attention to the meaning of possibility and which of these two meanings is intended.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage handles the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma) and the general possible (al-mumkina al-‘amma) propositions. The author asserts that both can be qualified with all four modal conditions (essential/descriptive non-necessity and non-permanence). For the general absolute, the reason is that the predicate holds at some indefinite time, and the essence exists at more times than that, so there is room for all qualifiers. The description of the subject is necessary and abstracted, so it behaves like the essence. For the general possible, the possibility of the predicate is compatible with necessity (if possibility is defined as compatibility), but if possibility is defined as negation of necessity, then it is incompatible with necessity but still compatible with permanence. The author warns that one must be careful about which definition of possibility is being used, as this affects the compatibility with other qualifiers.

Key Takeaway
General absolute and general possible propositions are flexible enough to take all four modal qualifiers, but the possible proposition's compatibility with necessity hinges on whether possibility is taken as mere compatibility or as negation of necessity.

The 32 Combinations of Modal Propositions and Restrictions
Enumeration of 32 combinations and their classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight simple modal propositions, the four restrictions, and categorize each of the 32 combinations as invalid, valid and considered, or valid but not considered.

Core Idea
Out of 32 possible forms, only 7 are standard; the rest are either invalid or non-standard.

Modal logic in Islamic thought systematically classifies propositions by necessity and perpetuity. Here, the author refines the base text's reference to eight propositions and four restrictions by enumerating all 32 combinations and separating those that are logically impossible from those that are possible but less central. This lesson will guide you through the enumeration and the threefold classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight simple modal propositions
From the source, recall the eight: al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa, al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa, al-mashrūṭa al-'āmma, al-'urfiyya al-'āmma, al-muṭlaqa al-'āmma, al-mumkina al-'āmma, and two others? Actually the text mentions only six by name but says 'the eight considered in this book'. The subtopics list them. List all eight according to standard modal logic: necessary absolute, perpetual absolute, conditional general, conventional general, absolute general, possible general, and two more? There is also the 'time-related' ones. For this exercise, accept the given eight.
The exact identity of all eight is not fully enumerated here; rely on the key terms.
2
Identify the four restrictions
The restrictions are lā-ḍarūra dhātiyya, lā-ḍarūra waṣfiyya, lā-dawām dhātī, lā-dawām waṣfī.
3
Multiply 8 propositions by 4 restrictions to get 32 combinations
Each restriction can be attached to each proposition, yielding 32 forms. The author then classifies each form into three categories.
4
Distinguish the three categories: invalid, valid considered, valid not considered
9 forms are invalid (ghayr ṣaḥīḥa), 7 are valid and considered (ṣaḥīḥa mu'tabara, mentioned in the matn), and the remaining 16 are valid but not considered (ṣaḥīḥa ghayr mu'tabara).
The author lists the invalid ones explicitly; the considered ones are from the main text.

(121) The detail in this topic is that the considered simple propositions mentioned in this book are eight, and the mentioned restrictions are four. By considering each of the eight propositions with each of those four restrictions, the number reaches thirty-two forms: nine of them are invalid, which are: restricting the absolute necessary by the four restrictions, because necessity as long as the self contradicts non-necessity and non-permanence in both their divisions; and restricting the absolute perpetual by the self-non-permanence and attributive-non-permanence, because permanence as per the self contradicts non-permanence in both its branches; these are six, and the remaining three are: restricting the general conditional by the attributive non-permanence and non-necessity, and restricting the general conventional by the attributive non-permanence. And seven of them are valid and considered, and they are mentioned in the text. The remaining forms are valid but not considered. 'al-la ḍarūr' 'al-la ḍarūra' 'al-la dawām' 'al-lā dawām' — and we draw a table containing forty-five houses, and we place the four restrictions in the upper houses, the pictures: the occasional? self, attributive, self, attributive, following the first house, with precedence of non-necessity over non-permanence and the self over the attributive. The eight simple propositions are in the houses following it from the right, according to the order of their mention in the text. And you learn each one of those three sections by a sign, and we place them in the remaining houses at the intersection of the two table lines that we drew: one of them is one of the propositions and the other is one of the four restrictions. Thus the ruling of each composition is known by the sign placed at the intersection of its table. So the sign of the valid considered is 'ṣḥṣm' and the sign of the valid but not considered is 'ṣġm' and the sign of the invalid is 'ġṣ' and this is the table.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a summary classification of the possible combinations between the eight simple modal propositions (al-basā'iṭ al-thamāniyya) and the four restrictions (al-quyūd al-arba'a): lā-ḍarūra dhātiyya, lā-ḍarūra waṣfiyya, lā-dawām dhātī, and lā-dawām waṣfī. The author states that the considered propositions in this book are eight, and the restrictions are four. Considering each proposition with each restriction yields 32 possible forms. Of these, 9 are invalid (ghayr ṣaḥīḥa): the absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) cannot be combined with any of the four restrictions because necessity as long as the self contradicts both non-necessity and non-permanence in both their divisions; the absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa) cannot be combined with self-non-permanence or attributive non-permanence because permanence as per the self contradicts non-permanence in both branches — these account for 6 invalid forms. The remaining 3 invalid forms are: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-'āmma) restricted by attributive non-permanence and attributive non-necessity, and the general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'āmma) restricted by attributive non-permanence. Seven forms are valid and considered (ṣaḥīḥa mu'tabara), and they are the ones mentioned in the main text. The remaining forms are valid but not considered (ṣaḥīḥa ghayr mu'tabara). The author then describes a table with 45 houses (likely 8x4=32 plus headers, but the number 45 is unclear). The restrictions are placed in upper houses, and the propositions are placed in descending order. By looking at the intersection, one can determine the ruling: 'ṣḥṣm' for valid considered, 'ṣġm' for valid but not considered, and 'ġṣ' for invalid. The table is meant to systematically summarize the classification.

Invalid Combinations and the Classification Table
Explanation of invalid combinations and description of the table.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why each of the nine invalid combinations is contradictory and interpret the symbols used in the classification table.

Core Idea
Invalid combinations are rooted in contradictions between the modal force and the restriction, such as necessity conflicting with non-necessity.

The previous lesson enumerated the combinations; now we delve into the specific logical reasons for invalidity and the table that encodes the judgments. Understanding these contradictions prevents erroneous reasoning and highlights the sophistication of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
List the invalid combinations and their rationale
The passage gives two main groups: (1) The absolute necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa) with all four restrictions – because necessity as long as the self exists contradicts any non-necessity or non-permanence. (2) The absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa) with the two non-permanence restrictions – because perpetual as long as the self exists contradicts non-permanence. Those account for 6. (3) Three additional: the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) with attributive non-permanence and attributive non-necessity (2) and the general conventional ('urfiyya 'amma) with attributive non-permanence (1). Note: there might be others; the text says 'these are six and the remaining three'.
The passage may have OCR issues; accept the given nine.
2
Analyze the contradiction for the daruriyya mutlaqa
Daruriyya mutlaqa means the predicate holds necessarily as long as the subject exists. Any restriction that negates necessity or perpetuity contradicts this core meaning. Therefore, combining it with la-darura (any form) or la-dawam (any form) is impossible.
3
Analyze the contradiction for the da'ima mutlaqa
Da'ima mutlaqa means the predicate holds perpetually as long as the subject exists. It does not assert necessity, but it asserts continuous occurrence. Restrictions that deny perpetuity (la-dawam) directly contradict. It can take non-necessity restrictions because it does not claim necessity.
So the two invalid forms are with la-dawam dhati and la-dawam wasfi.
4
Understand the table symbols
The author describes a table with 45 houses (likely 4 restriction columns × 8 proposition rows plus headers). Symbols: 'ṣḥṣm' for valid considered, 'ṣġm' for valid not considered, 'ġṣ' for invalid. The table is meant to quickly determine the status of any combination.
The exact layout is uncertain due to OCR issues, but the concept is clear.

Why Eight Propositional Images Were Omitted and What 'Unconsidered' Means
Author explains why the commentator omitted eight images and defines 'unconsidered'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only 24 of 32 proposition images are standard in later logical topics and identify what makes a proposition 'unconsidered'.

Core Idea
Logical validity does not guarantee inclusion in the canon; some propositions are valid but deliberately excluded.

In the study of categorical propositions, logicians identified 32 possible images, but only 24 are treated in subsequent discussions of contradiction, conversion, and syllogism. This lesson explains why eight were omitted and introduces the technical term 'unconsidered' (غير معتبرة).

Guided Path
1
Identify the total and split of proposition images
The passage states that the commentator addressed only 24 images and left 8. The 8 omitted result from restricting the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual with each of the four constraints.
2
Determine the reason for omission
The commentator's goal was to explain what the main text (matn) indicated. Since the matn did not refer to those eight, they were omitted.
3
Distinguish validity from being 'considered'
Among the possibilities for absolute necessary and absolute perpetual, six are invalid and two are valid but 'unconsidered'. Validity alone does not make a proposition standard in the tradition.
4
Define 'unconsidered' (ghayr mu'tabara)
The author clarifies that 'unconsidered' means logicians did not address these propositions in later topics like contradiction, conversion, and syllogism, even though they are logically valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then know that the commentator (al-muhashshi) did not address from these images except twenty-four of them and left the remaining eight—which result from restricting each of the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual with each of the four constraints—because his purpose was to explain what was indicated in the text, and the text did not refer to those eight in any way. In other words, the intended purpose is to explain the possibilities of propositions of which one of their possibilities is necessarily valid and considered, and you have known that six of the possibilities of the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual are invalid, and two of them are valid but unconsidered, contrary to the remaining six propositions, so reflect. Then it is not hidden that what is meant by a proposition being unconsidered is one of two meanings that have been previously indicated, and what is said here is that its meaning is that the logicians did not consider them and did not address their status in the coming topics of contradiction, conversion, and syllogism, even though they are valid. So it seems to revert to one of the two meanings as well, as is not hidden to the intelligent.

Commentary
This passage is a critical comment explaining why the commentator (likely al-Muhashshi) only discussed 24 of the 32 proposition images and left out eight. The author states that the eight omitted images come from restricting the absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) and absolute perpetual (الدائمة المطلقة) with all four constraints. The commentator's goal was to explain only what the main text (المتن) indicated, and the main text did not mention those eight. The author then rephrases: the purpose is to show the possibilities (احتمالات) where at least one option is valid and considered. Of the possibilities for the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual, six are invalid and two are valid but unconsidered, unlike the other six propositions which include valid considered ones. Finally, the author clarifies that 'unconsidered' (غير معتبرة) means that logicians did not treat these propositions as standard in later discussions of contradiction (تناقض), conversion (عكس), and syllogism (قياس), even though they are logically valid. This distinction is important because validity alone does not guarantee that a proposition form is studied in the tradition. All examples here are from the Islamic logical tradition.

Constraints on Conditional and Conventional Propositions
Author specifies which constraints apply to general conditional and conventional propositions, and discusses the term 'تركيب'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which constraints (descriptive non-permanence, non-necessity, essential non-permanence) apply to general conditional and general conventional propositions, and explain why 'tarkib' must include simple propositions.

Core Idea
The scope of 'tarkib' extends to simples constrained by conditions.

This passage refines our understanding of how constraints like non-permanence and non-necessity apply to two important proposition types in Islamic modal logic: the general conditional and the general conventional. It also clarifies that the term 'tarkib' (composition) should be understood broadly to include simple propositions that have constraints attached, because they are not covered by a narrow definition. By mastering this, you gain precision in classifying propositions for theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions and their constraints
Read statements (122) and (123) by Abd al-Rahim. Note that the general conditional (المشروطة العامة) is restricted by both descriptive non-permanence and non-necessity, while the general conventional (العرفية العامة) is restricted only by descriptive non-permanence. Then (123) mentions four propositions (two general and two temporal) restricted by essential non-permanence.
Descriptive non-permanence (اللادوام الوصفي) relates to a temporary attribute; essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي) relates to the essence.
2
Analyse the debate on 'tarkib'
Shaykh Abd al-Rahim in (124) argues that 'tarkib' must be interpreted in a more general sense that includes simple propositions (البسائط). The reason given is that simples too are not confined to what was previously indicated (i.e., they also need constraints). Understand why this broad interpretation is necessary for completeness.
Earlier logicians sometimes restricted 'tarkib' only to compound propositions; this passage expands it.
3
Consider Muhammad Ali's interpretation
In (125), Muhammad Ali offers a possible reading: 'tarkib' might refer to the temporal non-perpetual and conventional non-perpetual propositions that appear later in conversion theory. He links this to future topics like contradiction. This shows how one term can connect multiple logical discussions.
This step requires seeing the passage as part of a larger logical system.
4
Apply the constraints to examples
Test your understanding: For a general conditional like 'Every body is composite as long as it is a body', the constraint is descriptive non-permanence and non-necessity (since bodyhood is not permanent or necessary). For a general conventional like 'Every writer is human as long as he is described as a writer', only descriptive non-permanence applies (since being a writer is a temporary description).
Ensure you distinguish between the two proposition types and their respective constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱۲۳) هي : القضاياء الاربع اعنى: العامتين والوقتيتين المطلقتين باللادوام الذاتي. (عبد الرحيم)
(۱۲۴) ينبغي ان يراد بالتركيب المعنى الاعم المشتمل على البسايط، لانها ايضاً لا ينحصر فيا اشير اليه . شيخ عبد الرحيم)

(122) They are: restricting the general conditional to descriptive non-permanence and non-necessity, and restricting the general conventional to descriptive non-permanence. (Abd al-Rahim)
(123) They are: the four propositions, namely the two general and the two temporal, restricted by essential non-permanence. (Abd al-Rahim)
(124) It is necessary that by 'composition' (al-tarkib) be meant the more general meaning including the simples, because they too are not confined to what was indicated. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim)
(125) It is possible that what is meant by it is what will come in the discussion of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi), namely the temporal non-perpetual and the conventional non-perpetual in some. The first is the absolute temporal restricted by non-permanence, and the second is the general conventional restricted by non-permanence in some. This is if 'composition' (al-tarkib) in his statement is interpreted as the technical composition, such that it includes the simples as well, as indicated by some of the verifying commentators. Thus it would be an indication of what will come in the topic of contradiction as well, namely the temporal possible and the temporal absolute, and the commentator will explain them, so reflect. Commentary
This passage consists of three consecutive statements clarifying specific combinations and the interpretation of 'composition' (التركيب). First, (122) by Abd al-Rahim identifies two particular restrictions: the general conditional (المشروطة العامة) is restricted by descriptive non-permanence and non-necessity, and the general conventional (العرفية العامة) is restricted by descriptive non-permanence alone. Second, (123) by Abd al-Rahim refers to four propositions: the two general (عامتان) and the two temporal (وقتيتان) restricted by essential non-permanence. Third, (124) by Shaykh Abd al-Rahim insists that the term 'composition' (التركيب) should be understood in a broad sense that includes simple propositions (البسائط), because simples are also not confined to what was indicated. Finally, (125) by Muhammad Ali offers a possible interpretation: 'composition' might refer to what will appear in the discussion of simple conversion (عكس مستوي), namely the temporal non-perpetual (الحينية اللادائمة) and the conventional non-perpetual (العرفية اللادائمة) in some cases. He elaborates that the first is the absolute temporal restricted by non-permanence, and the second is the general conventional restricted by non-permanence in some. This reading assumes 'composition' is technical and includes simples. This also points to future topics like contradiction (تناقض), specifically the temporal possible and temporal absolute, which the commentator will explain. The passage thus refines the understanding of how constraints apply to different proposition types. All examples are from Islamic modal logic.

العرفية العامة

اللاضرورة الوصفية

Decomposing Special Possibility into Two General Possibilities
Author argues that special possibility decomposes into two general possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to transform a special possibility proposition into two general possibility propositions using the decomposition taught in this passage.

Core Idea
Special possibility is a conjunction of two general possibilities.

Modal propositions in Islamic logic include necessity, impossibility, and possibility. Among these, special possibility (al-imkan al-khass) is often taken as a simple modal notion. However, this passage argues that it is actually a compound, resolving into two general possibility propositions. Understanding this decomposition is essential for properly analyzing modal claims in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify a special possibility proposition
Find a proposition of the form 'Every A is B with special possibility' or 'No A is B with special possibility'. The passage uses 'كل انسان كاتب بالامكان الخاص' (Every human is a writer with special possibility).
Special possibility means neither the predicate nor its negation is necessary for the subject.
2
Express its meaning as two negations of necessity
The special possibility means two things: (1) the affirmation of B is not necessary for A, and (2) the negation of B is not necessary for A. For the example, it means that it is not necessary for a human to be a writer, and it is not necessary for a human not to be a writer.
3
Translate each negation into a general possibility proposition
The first part (affirmation not necessary) is rendered by the negative general possibility: 'لا شيء من الانسان بكاتب بالامكان العام' (Nothing of humans is a writer with general possibility). The second part (negation not necessary) is rendered by the affirmative general possibility: 'كل انسان كاتب بالامكان العام' (Every human is a writer with general possibility).
The negative general possibility asserts that B is not necessary for A; the affirmative general possibility asserts that not-B is not necessary for A.
4
Combine to show equivalence
The special possibility proposition is equivalent to the conjunction of the two general possibilities. As the passage states, 'انما ينحل الامكان الخاص الى ممكنتين عامتين' (special possibility resolves into two general possibilities).
This shows that special possibility is a compound notion, not a primitive one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲۶) لا يخفى ما فى تمثيله لاحدى الوجوديتين بالموجبة ولاخربهما بالسالبة. (محمد على)
(۱۲۷) قوله «ضرورة ان سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف هو امكان الطرف الموافق : انما ينحل الامكان الخاص الى ممكنتين عامتين احديهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة مع ان الحكم في كل من الممكنتين العامتين بسلب الضرورة من الجانب المخالف والحكم في الممكنة الخاصة بسلب الضرورة عن الجانب الموافق و المتخالف معاً، لان مفاد الممكنة الخاصة يؤديه مفاد الممكنتين العامتين، وذلك ، لان المراد بقولنا : كل انسان كاتب بالامكان الخاص، هو ان ثبوت الكتابة ليس ضرورياً للانسان مادامت ذاته وكذا سلبها عنه ليس ضرورياً مادامت ذاته أيضاً، والمفاد الأول يؤديه مفاد الممكنة العامة السالبة التي هي لاشيء من الانسان بكاتب بالامكان العام الذى معناه ان ثبوت الكتابة ليس بضرورى للانسان، والمفاد الثانى يؤديه مفاد الممكنة العامة الموجبة التي هي كل انسان كاتب بالامكان العام الذي معناه ان سلب الكتابة عنه ليس بضرورى (التقريب ص ۵۰)

(126) It is not hidden what is in his representation of one of the two existence propositions as affirmative and the other as negative. (Muhammad Ali)
(127) His statement: 'Necessarily, the negation of necessity from the opposite side is the possibility of the agreeing side': Indeed, special possibility (al-imkan al-khas) resolves into two general possibilities (mumkinatayn 'ammatayn), one affirmative and the other negative, although the judgment in each of the two general possibilities is the negation of necessity from the opposite side, and the judgment in the special possibility is the negation of necessity from both the agreeing side and the opposite side together, because the import of the special possibility is rendered by the import of the two general possibilities. That is because what is meant by our statement: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' is that the affirmation of writing is not necessary for the human as long as his essence exists, and likewise its negation from him is not necessary as long as his essence exists. The first import is rendered by the import of the negative general possibility, which is 'Nothing of humans is a writer with general possibility,' whose meaning is that the affirmation of writing is not necessary for the human. The second import is rendered by the import of the affirmative general possibility, which is 'Every human is a writer with general possibility,' whose meaning is that the negation of writing from him is not necessary. (al-Taqrib p. 50)
(128) His statement: 'Thus the judgment in the proposition...' i.e., whether it is affirmative like the mentioned example or negative.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement analyzing the nature of special possibility (الإمكان الخاص). It begins with (126), a brief remark by Muhammad Ali noting a flaw in someone's representation of two existence propositions as one affirmative and one negative. Then (127) delves into the main argument: the author quotes a statement that 'the negation of necessity from the opposite side is the possibility of the agreeing side,' and then demonstrates that special possibility decomposes into two general possibilities (ممكنتین عامتین), one affirmative and one negative. In each general possibility, the judgment is the negation of necessity from the opposite side (i.e., it denies that the opposite is necessary). In special possibility, the judgment is the negation of necessity from both sides—both affirmation and negation are not necessary. The explanation uses the example: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility' means that it is not necessary for the human to be a writer (affirmation) nor is it necessary for him not to be a writer (negation). This is equivalent to the conjunction of two general possibilities: the negative general possibility ('Nothing of humans is a writer with general possibility') which asserts that affirmation is not necessary, and the affirmative general possibility ('Every human is a writer with general possibility') which asserts that negation is not necessary. Thus, the import of special possibility is fully captured by the two general possibilities. The final part (128) indicates that this analysis applies whether the original proposition is affirmative or negative. The example uses a proposition about humans and writing, which is a standard example in Islamic logic for contingency. All reasoning fits within the Islamic logical tradition.

Decomposing Specific Possibility into General Possibilities
Gloss explains the resolution of specific possibility into two general possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a specific possibility statement as a conjunction of two general possibility statements and verify their equivalence.

Core Idea
Specific possibility decomposes into two general possibilities: one positive, one negative.

In this gloss, Mirza Muhammad Ali unpacks the meaning of specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas) by showing that it is not a primitive modality but rather the conjunction of two general possibilities (al-imkan al-amm). This decomposition is crucial for understanding modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with statements about contingency and necessity. By breaking down specific possibility, we gain clarity on how modal judgments are constructed and how they relate to each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal terms
Locate the terms 'specific possibility' (al-imkan al-khas) and 'general possibility' (al-imkan al-amm) in the Arabic. Note that the gloss also uses 'necessity' (al-darura) and its negation.
2
Understand the definition of specific possibility
Specific possibility for a proposition like 'Every human is a writer' means that writing is neither necessary nor impossible for man. That is, both the predicate and its negation are not necessary.
This is the key: it negates necessity from both sides.
3
Recognize the two negations of necessity
The specific possibility 'Every human is a writer by specific possibility' asserts two things: (1) writing is not necessary for man, and (2) the negation of writing is not necessary for man. Each of these is a general possibility.
General possibility only negates necessity from one side.
4
Map each negation to a general possibility
The first negation (writing not necessary) corresponds to the negative general possibility: 'Nothing of man is a writer by general possibility'. The second negation (negation of writing not necessary) corresponds to the positive general possibility: 'Every human is a writer by general possibility'.
Note the quantifier shift: the negative general uses 'nothing', the positive uses 'every'.
5
Verify the equivalence
Conclude that the specific possibility statement is exactly equivalent to the conjunction of the two general possibility statements. The gloss confirms this by saying the content of specific possibility is conveyed by the two general possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «ضرورة ان سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف هو امكان الطرف الموافق : انما ينحل الامكان الخاص الى ممكنتين عامتين احديهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة مع ان الحكم في كل من الممكنتين العامتين بسلب الضرورة من الجانب المخالف والحكم في الممكنة الخاصة بسلب الضرورة عن الجانب الموافق و المتخالف معاً، لان مفاد الممكنة الخاصة يؤديه مفاد الممكنتين العامتين، وذلك ، لان المراد بقولنا : كل انسان كاتب بالامكان الخاص، هو ان ثبوت الكتابة ليس ضرورياً للانسان مادامت ذاته وكذا سلبها عنه ليس ضرورياً مادامت ذاتها أيضاً، والمفاد الأول يؤديه مفاد الممكنة العامة السالبة التي هي لاشيء من الانسان بكاتب بالامكان العام الذى معناه ان ثبوت الكتابة ليس بضرورى للانسان، والمفاد الثانى يؤديه مفاد الممكنة العامة الموجبة التي هي كل انسان كاتب بالامكان العام الذي معناه ان سلب الكتابة عنه ليس بضرورى (التقريب ص ۵۰)
His statement: 'Necessarily, the negation of necessity from the contradictory side is the possibility of the agreeing side.' Indeed, specific possibility resolves into two general possibilities, one positive and one negative, while the judgment in each of the two general possibilities is by negation of necessity from the contradictory side, and the judgment in specific possibility is by negation of necessity from both the agreeing side and the contradictory side together, because the content of specific possibility is conveyed by the content of the two general possibilities. That is because what is meant by our statement: 'Every human is a writer by specific possibility' is that writing is not necessary for man as long as his essence exists, and likewise its negation from him is not necessary as long as his essence exists. The first content is conveyed by the content of the negative general possibility, which is 'Nothing of man is a writer by general possibility', whose meaning is that writing is not necessary for man. And the second content is conveyed by the content of the positive general possibility, which is 'Every human is a writer by general possibility', whose meaning is that the negation of writing from him is not necessary.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This gloss by Mirza Muhammad Ali provides a detailed logical analysis of specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas). It begins by quoting the commentator's statement: 'Necessarily, the negation of necessity from the contradictory side is the possibility of the agreeing side.' The gloss then explains that specific possibility resolves into two general possibilities: one positive and one negative. In each general possibility, the judgment is the negation of necessity from the contradictory side, whereas in specific possibility, the judgment is the negation of necessity from both the agreeing side and the contradictory side. The reason is that the meaning of specific possibility (e.g., 'Every human is a writer by specific possibility') is that writing is not necessary for man as long as his essence exists, and similarly the negation of writing is not necessary. The first part ('writing is not necessary') corresponds to the negative general possibility: 'Nothing of man is a writer by general possibility', and the second part ('negation of writing is not necessary') corresponds to the positive general possibility: 'Every human is a writer by general possibility'. This resolves the specific into two general possibilities, showing their logical equivalence.

Positive and Negative in General Possibility: Same Meaning or Not?
Gloss on the semantic difference between affirmative and negative general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the semantic meanings of affirmative and negative general possibility propositions and identify the disagreement among logicians.

Core Idea
In general possibility, affirmative and negative forms are not semantically identical.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a debate about whether affirmative and negative forms of general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) mean the same thing. Some verifiers argue they are equivalent, but the author of the gloss insists there is a clear semantic difference. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the debate
Locate in the passage who claims no difference between affirmative and negative general possibility, and who disputes it. What is the basis for each view?
2
Understand affirmative general possibility
For the proposition 'Every human is a writer by general possibility', what exactly is being negated? The answer: the necessity of the negation of the predicate ('writing') from the subject.
The affirmative negates necessity of the opposite of the predicate.
3
Understand negative general possibility
For 'Nothing of man is a writer by general possibility', what is being negated? It is the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate ('writing') to the subject.
The negative negates necessity of the predicate itself.
4
Compare the two meanings
Why are the two forms different? In the affirmative, we say it is not necessary that writing be absent; in the negative, we say it is not necessary that writing be present. They are not the same.
5
Apply the distinction
Take a new predicate, e.g., 'learned' for 'human'. Write the affirmative general possibility ('Every human is learned by general possibility') and the negative general possibility ('Nothing of human is learned by general possibility'). Explain the difference in meaning.
Ensure the example is from an Islamic context, e.g., knowledge of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فيكون الحكم في القضية .... اى سواء كانت موجبة كالمثال المذكور او سالبة نحو: لاشيء من الانسان بكاتب بالامكان الخاص. فلا فرق بين الموجبة والسالبة الا في اللفظ ، معنی ان عبر بعبارة ايجابية كانت موجبة و ان عبر بعبارة سلبية كانت سالبة، هذا. و ذهب بعض المحققين الى عدم الفرق المعنوى فى الممكنة العامة أيضاً حيث قال: ان الموجبة والسائبة منها راجعة الى سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف فلا يظهر فيها فرق بين الموجبة والسالبة بحسب المعنى. واقول: الفرق بينهما ظاهر بحسبه ايضاً فان الحكم في الموجبة بسلب الضرورة عن سلب المحمول عن الموضوع و ثبوته له مطلق يجوز أن يكون ضرورياً أوغير ضرورى و فى السالبة بسلبها عن ثبوت المحمول للموضوع وسلبه عنه مطلق يجوزان يكون ضرورياً أو غير ضروري مثلاً قولنا : كل انسان كاتب بالامكان العام معناه: ان الكتابة للانسان غير ضرورى ولا حكم فيه بالنسبة الى الكتابة حتى انه يجوز ان تكون ضرورية او غير ضرورية، وقولنا : لاشيء من الانسان بكاتب بالامكان العام معناه بالعكس. عدم
His statement: 'Then the judgment in the proposition...' i.e., whether it is affirmative like the mentioned example or negative like: 'Nothing of man is a writer by specific possibility'. So there is no difference between affirmative and negative except in wording, meaning that if expressed by an affirmative phrase it is affirmative, and if expressed by a negative phrase it is negative. This. And some verifiers went to the absence of a difference in meaning in the general possibility as well, where they said: the affirmative and negative of it revert to the negation of necessity from the contradictory side, so there appears no difference between affirmative and negative in terms of meaning. But I say: the difference between them is apparent in terms of meaning as well. For the judgment in the affirmative is by the negation of necessity of the negation of the predicate from the subject, while its affirmation to him absolutely may be necessary or non-necessary. And in the negative, by its negation of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject and its negation from him absolutely may be necessary or non-necessary. For example, our statement: 'Every human is a writer by general possibility' means: writing for man is not necessary, and there is no judgment about writing, so it may be necessary or non-necessary. And our statement: 'Nothing of man is a writer by general possibility' means the opposite.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This gloss continues the analysis of specific and general possibility. It addresses the commentator's statement about the judgment in the proposition, clarifying that it applies alike to affirmative and negative forms of specific possibility. The difference between affirmative and negative is merely verbal: using an affirmative expression yields an affirmative proposition, and a negative expression yields a negative proposition. Then the gloss reports that some verifiers extend this to general possibility, claiming that the affirmative and negative forms revert to the same meaning (negation of necessity from the contradictory side), so there is no semantic difference. However, the author of the gloss (Muhammad Ali) disagrees, arguing that there is a clear semantic difference. In the affirmative general possibility, the judgment is the negation of necessity of the negation of the predicate; in the negative general possibility, it is the negation of necessity of the affirmation of the predicate. He illustrates with an example: 'Every human is a writer by general possibility' means that writing is not necessary for man, while 'Nothing of man is a writer by general possibility' means the opposite (i.e., that the negation of writing is not necessary). Thus the two are not semantically identical.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام (al-imkan al-'amm): General possibility: a modal where either the predicate is not necessary (in negative form) or the negation is not necessary (in positive form). [Note: Some scholars argue its positive and negative forms are semantically identical; this gloss disputes that.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The affirmative negates necessity of the predicate's negation; the negative negates necessity of the predicate's affirmation.
C) The affirmative affirms necessity; the negative denies it.
D) The affirmative is always true; the negative false.

Key Takeaway
Affirmative and negative general possibility differ semantically: the former negates necessity of the opposite of the predicate, the latter negates necessity of the predicate itself.

The Meaning Difference in Possible Propositions
Author refutes the claim that affirmative and negative possible propositions are identical in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the meaning of affirmative and negative general possible propositions and explain the semantic difference using the negation of necessity.

Core Idea
Affirmative and negative possible propositions differ in which necessity is negated.

A claim has been made that affirmative and negative possible propositions are identical in meaning, differing only in wording. The author refutes this by analyzing the general possible (mumkina ‘āmma). He shows that in the affirmative, necessity is negated from the predicate's existence, while in the negative, it is negated from its non-existence. This lesson will walk through his argument and the key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under criticism
Find the statement that some investigators say: the affirmative and negative possible propositions (both special and general) have no meaning difference, only a verbal one.
The claim is that both return to the negation of necessity from the opposite side.
2
Extract the author's counter-thesis
Look for 'wa-aqūlu' and read the author's assertion that a clear meaning difference exists between them in the general possible as well.
The author states that the difference is apparent in meaning.
3
Analyze the example of the affirmative general possible
Study the example: 'Every man is a writer with general possibility.' The author interprets this as: writing for man is not necessary, meaning the necessity of the predicate's existence is negated.
The affirmative removes necessity from the side of the predicate's existence.
4
Analyze the example of the negative general possible
Now look at its counterpart: 'Nothing of man is a writer with general possibility.' This means the opposite: the necessity of the predicate's non-existence is negated, so it may be necessary or non-necessary.
The negative removes necessity from the side of the predicate's non-existence.
5
Confirm the semantic distinction
Compare the two: in the affirmative, necessity from the predicate's existence is negated; in the negative, necessity from the predicate's non-existence is negated. Hence, they differ in which side's necessity is removed, demonstrating a real meaning difference.
This refutes the claim of identity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For example: 'Nothing of man is a writer with special possibility.' So there is no difference between affirmative and negative except in wording, meaning if expressed with an affirmative phrase it becomes affirmative, and if expressed with a negative phrase it becomes negative. This. And some investigators went to the lack of meaning difference in the general possible as well, saying: The affirmative and negative of it refer back to the negation of necessity from the opposite side, so no difference appears between affirmative and negative in meaning. And I say: The difference between them is apparent in meaning as well. For the ruling in the affirmative is the negation of necessity from the negation of the predicate from the subject and its absolute affirmation, which may be necessary or non-necessary. And in the negative, it is the negation of necessity from the affirmation of the predicate to the subject and its absolute negation, which may be necessary or non-necessary. For example, our saying: 'Every man is a writer with general possibility' means: writing for man is not necessary, and there is no ruling concerning writing, so it may be necessary or non-necessary. And our saying: 'Nothing of man is a writer with general possibility' means the opposite.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the view that affirmative and negative possible propositions are identical in meaning. The author first presents the claim regarding the special possible (mumkina khāṣṣa): there is no difference except in wording. For example, 'Nothing of man is a writer with special possibility' is equivalent to 'Every man is a non-writer with special possibility'? Then some investigators extend this to the general possible (mumkina ‘āmma), arguing that both affirmative and negative return to the negation of necessity from the opposite side. The author refutes this by showing that in the affirmative, the judgment is the negation of necessity from the negation of the predicate and its absolute affirmation, which could be necessary or contingent; in the negative, it is the negation of necessity from the affirmation of the predicate and its absolute negation. He illustrates with the example: 'Every man is a writer with general possibility' means writing is not necessary for man, so it may be necessary or contingent. Its negative means the opposite, i.e., the necessity of non-writing is removed, so it may be necessary or contingent. Thus, they differ in which side the necessity is removed from, reflecting a genuine meaning difference.

Key Terms
ممكنة خاصة (mumkina khāṣṣa): A proposition where possibility is affirmed with a special condition, e.g., 'man is possibly not writer' implies negation of necessity of both existence and non-existence.
ممكنة عامة (mumkina ‘āmma): A proposition where possibility is affirmed generally, negating the necessity of existence or non-existence.
سلب الضرورة (salb al-ḍarūra): The operation of removing necessity from a side; in this context, it is the key operation that distinguishes affirmative and negative possible propositions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the general possible proposition 'Every man is a writer with general possibility', what is negated?
A) The necessity of writing for man
B) The necessity of non-writing for man
C) Both the necessity of writing and non-writing
D) The possibility of writing

Key Takeaway
Affirmative and negative general possible propositions are semantically distinct: the affirmative negates necessity from the predicate's existence, the negative from its non-existence.

Criterion for Affirmation and Negation in Conditional Propositions
Commentators explain that truth of conditional depends on connection, not on quality of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a muttasila conditional is affirmative or negative based on the presence or absence of a causal connection, regardless of the quality of its parts.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional lies in the connection, not the parts.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle from the hashya: the criterion for a conditional's affirmation or negation is the connection (ittisal) itself, not whether the antecedent and consequent are positive or negative. Several examples illustrate this, and the text also clarifies that a negative conditional can arise from either no connection or a connection lacking causal basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
The passage states that the criterion (madaar) for affirmation in muttasila is the connection of the two relatives. Note that this applies regardless of the quality of the parts (both positive, both negative, or mixed).
2
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: 'If the sun is rising, then day exists' (both positive), 'If the sun is rising, then night does not exist' (mixed), and the negative example with Zayd (both negative). Verify that the affirmation/negation matches the connection, not the parts.
3
Distinguish the two kinds of negative
The text explains that a negative conditional can be due to either (a) no connection at all, or (b) a connection that is not based on causation ('alaqa). The example 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' illustrates the second kind.
4
Apply the criterion
Given a new muttasila statement, identify whether the connection is affirmed (positive) or denied (negative). Check if there is a causal link; if not, it is negative even if the connection may exist accidentally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني: فعلى ما ذكرنا يكون ذلك المثال متصلة موجبة مع كون النسبتين سلبيتين، لان مدار الايجاب في المتصلة على اتصال النسبتين سواء كانت النسبتان ثبوتيتين كقولنا: ان كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود او سلبيتين كما فى المثال الذي ذكره المحشى او مختلفتين كقولنا ان كانت الشمس طالعة فليس الليل موجوداً.
