(میرزا محمد علی) وذلك لان المركب كما ينتفى بانتفاء احد الاجزاء ، فقد ينتفي بانتفاء جميع الاجزاء فان السالبة ما حكم فيها بانه ليس هناك اتصال لعلاقة وذلك قديكون بان لا يكون هناك اتصال اصلاً كالمثال الذي ذكره المحشى للسالبة المتصلة وقد يكون بان يكون اتصال لا لعلاقة كقولنا : ليس البتة كلما كان الانسان ناطقاً كان الحمار ناهقاً بمعنى انه ليس ذلك الاتصال الحاصل بينها مستنداً الى علاقة . (ميرزا محمد على)
It means: Based on what we mentioned, that example is an affirmative muttasila despite both relatives being negative, because the criterion for affirmation in the muttasila is the connection of the two relatives, whether the two relatives are affirmative as in 'If the sun is rising then the day exists', or negative as in the example mentioned by the marginalist, or different as in 'If the sun is rising then the night is not existing'. (Abd al-Rahim) That is, the negative muttasila is that in which the connection of the two relatives is judged to be negated, whether they are affirmative as in the example mentioned by the marginalist, or negative as in 'It is not the case that whenever Zayd is not writing he is not walking', or different as in 'It is not the case that whenever the sun is not rising the day exists'. (Abd al-Rahim) It is a subject and predicate, i.e., the necessary muttasila is like the absolute muttasila in that the criterion for affirmation and negation is the connection of causation and the negation of that connection, and consideration is not given to the affirmation or negation of the two sides. So whether the two sides are affirmative or negative or different, the proposition is affirmative if the connection of the two relatives is judged based on causation, and negative if the negation of that connection is judged. (Muhammad Ali) (The pronoun 'the affirmative' is a subject and 'what is judged' is its predicate, and the sentence along with what is conjoined to it, namely 'and the negative is what is judged...' is in the position of detailing and branching off from the first sentence, namely 'and likewise the necessary one'. And it is not permissible for 'the affirmative' to be an adjective of 'the necessary one' as would be apparent, as is not hidden from those of sound taste. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) And that is because the compound, just as it ceases to exist by the non-existence of one part, may cease to exist by the non-existence of all parts. So the negative is that in which it is judged that there is no connection based on causation. That may be by there being no connection at all, as in the example given by the marginalist for the negative muttasila, or by there being a connection not based on causation, as in 'It is not the case that whenever man is rational the donkey is braying', meaning that that obtained connection is not attributable to causation. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the criterion for affirmative and negative conditional propositions (muttasila). The authors emphasize that the truth or falsity of a conditional does not depend on whether the individual parts (the antecedent and consequent) are positive or negative statements. Instead, it depends solely on whether there is a real connection (ittisal) between them, and whether that connection is based on a causal relationship ('alaqa). Several examples are given: 'If the sun is rising, then day exists' shows both parts positive; 'If the sun is rising, then night does not exist' shows a positive antecedent and negative consequent. Even if both parts are negative, as in an example involving Zayd not writing and not walking, the conditional can still be affirmative if the connection is affirmed. The negative muttasila is defined as one where the connection is denied. The text also clarifies a grammatical point about the syntactic structure of the definition, and finally discusses a subtlety: a negative conditional can arise either from a complete absence of connection or from a connection that lacks a causal basis. For instance, the statement 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' has no real causal link; it is merely an accidental coincidence. Thus, the key takeaway is that the judgment is about the connection itself, not about the quality of the parts.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional proposition composed of two implicative statements (muqaddam and tali) connected by a conjunction like 'إذا' or 'كلما'. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
اتصال النسبتين (ittisāl al-nisbatayn): The connection between the two relative propositions (the antecedent and consequent).
علاقة (ʿalāqa): A causal or necessary relation between the antecedent and consequent that grounds the connection. [Note: In some later texts, 'alaqa may be broadened to include any relation, not just causal.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether the antecedent and consequent are both affirmative or both negative.
B) Whether the connection between the two relatives is affirmed or denied.
C) Whether the antecedent is a cause of the consequent.
D) Whether the proposition uses 'كلما' or 'إذا'.

Key Takeaway
The criterion for affirmation in muttasila is the affirmation of the connection itself, not the quality of the parts; a negative conditional occurs when the connection is denied, either due to absence or lack of causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Consider the proposition: 'If a person is a Muslim, then he is generous.' Is there a causal connection? How would the hashya classify this? Compare with: 'If a person is a Muslim, then he is a believer.' Which one has a causal link?

Luzumiyya vs. Ittifaqiyya: Necessary vs. Accidental Conditionals
Commentators differentiate necessary from accidental conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya conditionals and identify the type of causal relation ('alaqa) in a given example.

Core Idea
A conditional is necessary if its connection has a causal basis; otherwise it is accidental.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions are not all alike. Some express a genuine causal link, while others only happen to coincide. The commentators of the Mantiq tradition, as seen in this hashya, draw a sharp distinction between luzumiyya (necessary) and ittifaqiyya (accidental) conditionals. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the definitions of luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya in the passage. Note that luzumiyya involves a causal relation ('alaqa) while ittifaqiyya involves mere connection without causation.
The passage also mentions an absolute muttasila where causation is not considered.
2
Understand the role of 'alaqa
Look for the description of 'alaqa as the thing that makes the connection necessary. It can be a cause, an effect, a common cause, or mutual dependence.
The commentator lists four ways 'alaqa can manifest.
3
Compare the examples
The passage gives examples: 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying' is accidental; 'If the day exists, the sun is rising' is necessary (causal).
Accidental conditionals lack a real causal link.
4
Classify types of 'alaqa
From the commentary, identify the four types: (1) antecedent causes consequent, (2) consequent causes antecedent, (3) both effects of a common cause, (4) mutual dependence (tadā'if).
This classification helps in analyzing real arguments.
5
Apply the tool
Given a conditional, decide if it is luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya. If luzumiyya, determine which type of 'alaqa is present.
Use Islamic science examples for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «و اما الاتفاقية فهى ما حكم فيها . .. عطف على محذوف مدلول عليه بالكلام السابق والتقدير ان اللزومية ما حكم فيها باتصال لعلاقة أو نفى ذلك الاتصال و اما الاتفاقية فهى ما حكم فيها بمجرد الاتصال او نفيه ولا يذهب عليك انه لا يجوز ان يكون معطوفاً على قوله: «و كذلك اللزومية» لان ذلك يستدعى أن لا يكون الاتفاقية موافقة لمطلق المتصلة فى كون الايجاب والسلب على الاتصال وسلبه كما ان اللزومية موافقة له فيه على ما هو الظاهر من قولنا زيد كالاسد واما عمرو فانه لا يجوز ذلك اذا كان ايضاً كالاسد والحال ان الاتفاقية ايضاً كمطلق المتصلة فى ذلك وكيف و هو قسم منه والمقسم جميع الاقسام و اما اذا كان معطوفاً على المقدر فلا يقتضى ذلك ، فان «اما» يقتضى مخالفة ما بعدها لما قبلها فيا اثبت له لا مطلقا وذلك متحقق كما ترى فان اللزومية حكم فيها باتصال لعلاقة أو نفيه والاتفاقية حكم فيها بمجرد الاتصال أو نفيه فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی) اعلم ان عد عدم استناده اليها اما لعدم تحققها في نفس الأمر أو لعدم اعتبارها والأول هو الاشهر والثاني تحقيق للمصنف حيث قال: والتحقيق ان المعية في الوجود امر ممكن لابد له من علة يقتضيه فذهب الى انقسام المتصلة إلى اللزومية والاتفاقية من جهة اعتبار العلاقة واعتبار عدمها. ثم ان حكم في القضية بسلب الاتصال او بسلبه و لم يعتبر شيء من العلاقة وعدمها فتسمى تلك القضية متصلة مطلقة (عبد الرحيم) فانه لا علاقة بين ناطقية الانسان و ناهقية الحمار حتى انه يجوز العقل تحقق كل منها دون الآخر لكن وقع الاتفاق بينهما في الصدق بحيث كلما كان الاول محققاً كان الثاني محققاً. (ميرزاعبد الرحيم) قوله وهى أمر بسببه يستصحب ... هذا الامر المسمى بالعلاقة قد يتحقق في ضمن العلة بان يكون المقدم علة للتالى كما ذكره المحشى او بالعكس كعلية طلوع الشمس لوجود النهار في قولنا: ان كان النها موجوداً، فكانت الشمس طالعة أو يكونا معلولى علة واحدة كقولنا: ان كان النهار موجوداً فالعالم مضىء فان وجود النهار واضائة العالم معلولان لطلوع الشمس وقد يتحقق في ضمن التضائف بان يكونا متضائفين أي يتوقف تعقل كل منهما على الآخر كقولنا ان كان زيد ابا عمرو فهو ابنه . (عبد الرحيم)
His saying: 'And as for the accidental (ittifaqiyya), it is that in which it is judged...' is a conjunction to an elided element indicated by the previous speech. The estimation is: The necessary (luzumiyya) is that in which the connection is judged to be based on causation or its negation, while the accidental is that in which mere connection or its negation is judged. And it should not escape you that it is not permissible for it to be conjoined to his saying 'and likewise the necessary', because that would require that the accidental does not accord with the absolute muttasila in that affirmation and negation are about the connection and its negation, just as the necessary accords with it in that, as is apparent from our saying 'Zayd is like a lion' and 'Amr, it is not permissible that he also be like a lion', while the accidental is also like the absolute muttasila in that; how could it be otherwise, since it is a part of it and the dividing class is all the parts. But if it is conjoined to the estimated [element], that does not require it, because 'amma' requires that what follows it differs from what precedes it in what is affirmed for it, not absolutely. And that is realized as you see: for the necessary, connection based on causation or its negation is judged; for the accidental, mere connection or its negation is judged. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) Know that the absence of its attribution to it is either because it is not actual in itself or because it is not considered. The first is the more famous, and the second is the verification of the author where he said: The verification is that coexistence in existence is a possible matter that necessarily requires a cause that necessitates it. So he went to the division of the muttasila into the necessary and the accidental from the perspective of considering the causation and considering its absence. Then if in the proposition the connection or its negation is judged and nothing of causation or its absence is considered, that proposition is called an absolute muttasila. (Abd al-Rahim) For there is no causal relation between man's rationality and the donkey's braying, to the extent that the intellect allows each to occur without the other, but they accidentally coincide in truth such that whenever the first is realized, the second is realized. (Mirza Abd al-Rahim) His saying: 'It is a thing by which...' This thing called 'relation' may be realized within the cause, such that the antecedent is a cause of the consequent as the marginalist mentioned, or the opposite, like the causality of sunrise for the existence of day in our saying 'If the day exists, then the sun is rising', or they are effects of a single cause like our saying 'If the day exists, then the world is illuminated', for the existence of day and the illumination of the world are effects of sunrise. And it may be realized within mutual dependence (tadā'if), such that they are mutually dependent, i.e., the conception of each depends on the other, like our saying 'If Zayd is the father of Amr, then he is his son'. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of muttasila conditionals: luzumiyya (necessary) and ittifaqiyya (accidental). The necessary conditional involves a causal connection ('alaqa) between the antecedent and consequent; the accidental conditional involves a mere connection without causation. The commentary clarifies that the division arises from considering whether a causal relation is present or not. An example of an accidental conditional is 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' – there is no real causal link, only accidental co-occurrence. The passage also discusses a grammatical point about the conjunction structure in the definition. Finally, it elaborates on the nature of 'alaqa (causal relation), explaining that it can be of three types: (1) the antecedent is the cause of the consequent (e.g., sunrise causing day), (2) the consequent is the cause of the antecedent (e.g., day indicating sunrise), (3) both are effects of a common cause (e.g., day and illumination both from sunrise), or (4) mutual dependence (tadā'if) like fatherhood and sonship. This detailed classification shows that 'alaqa is broader than simple causality.

Key Terms
لزومية
اتفاقية
علاقة
تضائف

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying'
C) 'If Zayd is not writing, then he is not walking'
D) 'If the day exists, then the world is illuminated'

Key Takeaway
The distinction between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya conditionals hinges on the presence or absence of a causal relation ('alaqa), with the former alone providing a genuine logical connection.

Understanding the True Disjunctive (Munfasila Haqīqiyya)
Commentators define disjunctive conditionals and distinguish the true from the false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the true disjunctive from other types by identifying whether mutual exclusion is in both truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
The true disjunctive is exclusive in both truth and falsehood.

In classical logic, disjunctive propositions are not always 'either-or' in the strongest sense. The commentators explain that the true disjunctive (munfasila haqīqiyya) requires mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood, making it the most rigorous form. Let's unpack this and see how it applies to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion for disjunctives
The passage says the criterion for truth/falsity in a disjunctive is the judgment of mutual exclusion (tanāfi) or its absence, analogous to the muttasila. Note that it doesn't matter if the parts are affirmative or negative; only the mutual exclusion matters.
This is foundational: tanāfi is the essence of disjunction.
2
Identify the three types of disjunctives
In logic, there are three types: manī'at al-jam' (cannot be true together), manī'at al-khuluw (cannot be false together), and the true exclusive (both). The passage compares the true disjunctive to the other two.
The other two are weaker forms.
3
Focus on the true disjunctive
The passage explains why it is called 'true': because mutual exclusion is in both truth and falsehood together, and this exclusion is stronger than in the other types. Therefore, it is most deserving of the name 'disjunctive'.
This is the core definition: complete mutual exclusion.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Given a proposition like 'Either Zayd is in the house or he is in the mosque', determine which type it is. If it is possible for him to be in both? Or neither? The true disjunctive requires that exactly one option is true.
Think about real fiqh classifications: 'The act is either wajib or haram' – can it be both? Neither?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني ان مدار الايجاب والسلب في المنفصلة على الحكم بالتنا في وعدمه على قياس المتصلة ولا يلاحظ كون النسبتين ثبوتيتين او سلبيتين او مختلفتين فافهم. (میرزا محمد علی) قوله : فالمنفصلة الحقيقية ... انما سميت حقيقية، لأن حقيقة الانفصال ان يكون التنافي : بين الجزئين فى الصدق والكذب معاً ولان التنافي بين جزئيها اشد و اقوى منه بين جزئي الاخيرين فهى احق بان تسمى منفصلة (محمد على)
He means that the criterion for affirmation and negation in the disjunctive (munfasila) is the judgment of mutual exclusion (tanāfi) or its absence, analogously to the muttasila, and consideration is not given to whether the two relatives are affirmative or negative or different. So understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) His saying: 'The true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-haqīqiyya)...' It is called true only because the reality of disjunction is that there is mutual exclusion between the two parts in truth and falsehood together, and because the mutual exclusion between its two parts is stronger and more powerful than that between the two parts of the other two [types], so it is more deserving of being called disjunctive. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage discusses the disjunctive conditional (munfasila), which is typically of the form 'Either A or B' or 'Either A or B or C'. The criterion for its truth (affirmation) or falsity (negation) is analogous to that of the muttasila: instead of connection, it is about mutual exclusion (tanāfi). Specifically, a true disjunctive proposition is one where the parts cannot be true together and cannot be false together (i.e., they are contradictory). The text explains why this type is called 'true disjunctive' (al-munfasila al-haqīqiyya): because the mutual exclusion between its parts is complete—both in truth and falsehood—and it is stronger than the exclusion in other types like manī'at al-jam' (mutual exclusion in truth only) or manī'at al-khuluw (mutual exclusion in falsehood only). Therefore, the true disjunctive is most deserving of the name 'disjunctive'.

Key Terms
منفصلة
تنافي
منفصلة حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It has only two parts.
B) Its parts cannot be true together but can be false together.
C) Its parts cannot be true together and cannot be false together.
D) Its parts can be true together but cannot be false together.

Key Takeaway
The true disjunctive requires mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood, making its parts logically contradictory.

Three Types of Disjunctive Conditionals
Author defines true disjunction, man'at al-jam', and man'at al-khuluw with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals (true, man'at al-jam', man'at al-khuluw) and identify them in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
A disjunction's logical force depends on what it excludes: conjunction, disjunction, or both.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive conditionals (al-munfaṣila) are not all alike. Some prevent both parts from being true together, others prevent both from being false together, and the true disjunction does both. This lesson unpacks these three types with concrete examples from the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the passage, find the names: true disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya), māni‘at al-jam‘, māni‘at al-khuluw. Note that the true disjunction is also called 'the real disjunction' because it excludes both possibilities.
The true disjunction is sometimes simply called 'al-munfaṣila' when unqualified.
2
Understand what each prevents
True disjunction prevents both parts being true together AND both being false together. Man'at al-jam' only prevents joint truth. Man'at al-khuluw only prevents joint falsehood.
Think of truth tables: p and q. True disjunction: not both true, not both false. Man'at al-jam': at most one true (falsehood allowed for both). Man'at al-khuluw: at least one true (truth allowed for both).
3
Analyze the examples given
The passage gives examples: 'either this is stone or tree' is true disjunction (cannot be both, cannot be neither). 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' is māni‘at al-jam‘ (cannot be both true, but both false is possible: Zayd not in sea and does drown). 'Either Zayd is not in the sea or he drowns' is māni‘at al-khuluw (cannot be both false, but both true is possible: Zayd in sea and drowns).
Check the logic: For māni‘at al-jam‘ example, the parts are p='Zayd is in sea' and q='Zayd does not drown'. They cannot both be true (if in sea, he drowns, so q false). But both false is possible (not in sea and drowns? Actually if not in sea and drowns, that's possible if he drowns elsewhere; but the example works if we assume drowning implies not in sea is false? The key is that the incompatibility is only in truth.)
4
Distinguish essential vs. accidental
True disjunction is essential (bi-dhāt) when the incompatibility arises from a logical relationship between the parts, like stone and tree (both are ground minerals? Actually the relationship is that they are different species of the same genus? Not necessarily; the point is that the disjunction is not accidental). Accidental (ittifāqiyya) occurs by coincidence, e.g., 'this is black or this is writing' which can both be true.
The passage states that true disjunction requires a relationship (ʿalāqa). Accidental disjunction has no such relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتسمى بالمنفصلة الحقيقية الموجبة. فتسمى بالمنفصلة الحقيقية السالبة. إنما سميت بذلك لكون الجمع بين جزئيها ممنوعاً. وإنما سميت مانعة الجمع لاشتمالها على منع الجمع بين جزئيها في الصدق فقط. وإنما سميت مانعة الخلو لاشتمالها على منع الخلو بين جزئيها في الكذب فقط. فالمعنى على التقدير الأول: أن مانعة الجمع ما حكم فيه على التنافي الذي هو في الصدق فقط لا في الكذب. وعلى التقدير الثاني: أنه ما حكم فيه من حيث الصدق فقط على التنافي، أعم من أن يكون التنافي أيضاً في الصدق فقط أو يكون فيه وفي الكذب. وهذا مثال الإيجاب للمنفصلة الحقيقية: إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حجراً أو شجراً. ومثال السلب: ليس البتة إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حجراً وإما أن لا يكون. ومثال مانعة الجمع: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وأما أن لا يغرق. ومثال مانعة الخلو: إما أن لا يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن يغرق. والمنفصلة الحقيقية هي التي يكون التنافي بين جزئيها بالذات لعلاقة بينهما، والاتفاقية هي التي لا يكون الانفصال لعلاقة بل بالاتفاق.
So it is called the true affirmative disjunction. So it is called the true negative disjunction. It is named that because the conjunction of its two parts is prevented. And it is called man'at al-jam' because it includes the prevention of conjunction between its two parts in truth only. And it is called man'at al-khuluw because it includes the prevention of disjunction between its two parts in falsehood only. So the meaning on the first supposition: that man'at al-jam' is that in which the incompatibility is judged only in truth, not in falsehood. And on the second supposition: that it is judged only with respect to truth regarding the incompatibility, regardless of whether the incompatibility is only in truth or also in falsehood. This is an example of the affirmative true disjunction: either this thing is a stone or a tree. And an example of the negative: it is not the case that either this thing is a stone or it is not. And an example of man'at al-jam': either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown. And an example of man'at al-khuluw: either Zayd is not in the sea or he drowns. The true disjunction is that in which the incompatibility between its two parts is essential due to a relationship between them, while the accidental is that in which the disjunction is not due to a relationship but by accident.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of disjunctive conditional propositions (al-munfasila) in Islamic logic. The author begins by naming the affirmative true disjunction and the negative true disjunction, explaining that the true disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya) is so called because it prevents both parts from being true together (man' al-jam') and also prevents both from being false together (man' al-khuluw). Then two other types are introduced: man'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction) which only prevents simultaneous truth but allows both to be false, and man'at al-khuluw (exhaustive disjunction) which only prevents simultaneous falsehood but allows both to be true. The author clarifies a subtle point about whether the judgment of incompatibility is restricted to truth alone or to falsehood alone, and whether the incompatibility itself is also restricted. Examples are given: for true disjunction, 'either this is a stone or a tree' (cannot be both, cannot be neither); for man'at al-jam', 'either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' (if he is in the sea, he drowns? Actually the example is: it cannot be that he is in the sea and does not drown, but it could be that he is not in the sea and drowns or does not drown); for man'at al-khuluw, 'either Zayd is not in the sea or he drowns' (cannot be that he is not in the sea and does not drown, but could be that he is in the sea and drowns or does not). Finally, the passage distinguishes true disjunction (which is based on an essential relationship between the parts) from accidental disjunction (ittiāqiyya), which occurs by coincidence, like the disjunction between blackness and writing in a particular person.

The Basis of Quantification in Conditionals: Judgment, Not Parts
Critique of the view that quantification of conditionals depends on parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the quantification of a conditional proposition is determined by its judgment or its parts, and critique the opposing view.

Core Idea
Universality of a conditional comes from the judgment, not the antecedent or consequent.

In this passage, the author refutes a common misconception that the universality or particularity of a conditional proposition is due to the quantification of its parts. Instead, he argues, it is the judgment about the connection or opposition that determines its scope. This refinement has important implications for how we analyze logical propositions in Islamic sciences, particularly in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the statement where the author asserts what determines quantification of conditionals. It says: 'the universality, particularity, and singularity of the conditional are not due to the parts; rather, they are based on the judgment.'
2
Understand the opposing view
Some scholars held that if the parts are universal, the conditional is universal. Example given: 'if every human is an animal, then every writer is an animal.' Note how they infer based on parts.
3
See the analogy to categoricals
The author analogizes: in categoricals, universality is due to the judgment of predication, not the subject/predicate. Similarly, in conditionals, quantification depends on the judgment about the connection or opposition.
4
Recognize the secondary benefit
The same principle applies to categoricals: quantification is based on judgment, not subject/predicate. This extends the correction to a broader domain.
5
Evaluate the critique
Consider the claim that the opposing view is based on a coincidence. The author suggests it is a mistake to think that because universality of parts often coincides with universality of the proposition, it is the cause.

(27) Let it not escape you: the universality, particularity, and singularity of the conditional are not due to the universality, particularity, and singularity of the parts; rather, they are based on the judgment, as the author clarified. A group has held that they are due to the parts: if it is universal like our saying 'if every human is an animal, then every writer is an animal', then the conditional is universal; if it is singular like our saying 'whenever Zayd writes, he moves his hand', then it is singular; if it is indefinite like our saying 'whenever a human is a writer, he moves his fingers', then it is indefinite. Thus said some verifying commentators in Sharh al-Matali'. But if they looked with the eye of verification, they would find the matter to be otherwise. For the categorical is not universal because of the universality of the subject and predicate, but because of the universality of the judgment which is predication therein; and its counterpart here is connection and opposition. So just as in categoricals one must look to the judgment and not to the parts, similarly in conditionals with respect to the relation of those states to the judgment. End. Another benefit is derived from this: that universality and particularity in categoricals are also based on the judgment, not the subject and predicate; and the commentator's words also allude to that. Some have differed on this as well, as is apparent from the words of some verifying commentators among the explainers of the text, who said: the basis of the mentioned divisions in conditionals is the judgment, not the subject, just as in categoricals. I think what led them into confusion is that they saw that whenever the subject is universal, the proposition is universal; or particular, it is particular; or singular, it is singular; and so on. So they ruled that universality and particularity are due to the universality and particularity of the subject. And they did not know that that is only by coincidence. However it may be, that view is far from verification. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (28) That is because according to what was established earlier, the division of the conditional into the mentioned divisions is based on the judgment regarding the suppositions of the antecedent as a whole, a part, or otherwise. So it is impossible to conceive of a natural (tabi‘iyya) proposition, as is clear to the ponderer. And it is not hidden that the impossibility of conceiving a natural conditional is according to the correct doctrine; but according to what the group claimed, what is required by the apparent meaning of their words is its permissibility, as in our saying 'whenever a thing is a human, it is a species' and 'whenever it is an animal, it is a genus'. So ponder. (Muhammad Ali) (29) That is, meaning one of these three from any language. (Abd al-Rahim) (30) It would be better for him to say: 'and in the affirmative disjunction', as in the coordinated phrase, so that he would not need to say 'this in the affirmative', meaning that 'abadan' (ever) and 'da'iman' (always) are quantifiers for the disjunction only in the affirmative. (Abd al-Rahim) (31) The difference between the conditional and the disjunctive in the quantifier is only in the affirmative, as indicated by his statement afterwards: 'and as for the free (sa'iba) disjunction absolutely, whether it is conditional or disjunctive'. And it does not mean that 'da'iman' and 'abadan' are quantifiers for the disjunction only in the affirmative, as is the seemingly understood meaning, such that it is said: it would be better for him to say 'and in the affirmative disjunction' instead of his statement 'and in the disjunction', as he said 'and in the affirmative conditional', so that he would not need this, or he could omit the word 'affirmative' here as well. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (32) An indication that the absoluteness therein is with respect to the restriction of specification, not absolutely, so that the intended meaning is 'or on some part absolutely' without any specification or its absence being considered, by the context of his statement 'or specified'. (Muhammad Ali) (33) For the judgment of the affirmation of humanity is not upon all suppositions of the affirmation of animality for a thing, for that may be in that it is a horse, a donkey, or other types of animal, nor upon some specified part, as is apparent; for specification...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage critically examines the basis for the quantification (hasr), particularity (ihmāl), and singularity (shakhsiyya) of conditional propositions. It begins by stating the correct view: these properties are not due to the quantification of the parts (i.e., the antecedent and consequent) but rather due to the judgment (hukm) itself, as the author of the original text stated. Some scholars have opposed this, arguing that if the parts are universal, the conditional is universal, giving examples like 'if every human is an animal, then every writer is an animal'. The passage argues that if they had investigated properly, they would see that in categorical propositions, universality is not because of the subject and predicate but because of the judgment of predication. Similarly, in conditionals, the relation (connection or opposition) between the antecedent and consequent is what determines the quantification. 

A secondary benefit: even in categoricals, quantification depends on the judgment, not the subject/predicate. The commentator alludes to this. The passage then suggests that the confusion arises from noticing that in categoricals, whenever the subject is universal, the proposition is universal, etc., leading them to think that the subject's quantification is the cause, whereas it is merely coincidental. The view that quantification is due to the parts is rejected as far from verification, though a tempered view that subject quantification may be a remote cause is allowed.

Next, the passage explains why a 'natural' conditional (tabi‘iyya) is impossible on the correct view: since the division is based on the judgment regarding all, some, or a single supposition of the antecedent, there is no room for a natural proposition that is unrestricted. However, on the opposing view, it would be permissible, e.g., 'whenever a thing is a human, it is a species; whenever it is an animal, it is a genus'.

Finally, several glosses clarify details about the quantifiers for disjunction: 'abadan' and 'da'iman' are used only in affirmative disjunctions, and the difference between conditionals and disjunctions in quantifier usage is only in the affirmative. Also, a note on the scope of 'absoluteness' in the text, indicating it is with respect to the restriction of specification. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing a specific judgment about animality.

Quantifiers in Disjunctive Propositions and the Natural Conditional
Clarifications on quantifiers for disjunctives and the natural conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the quantifier rules for conditional vs. disjunctive propositions, and explain why a natural conditional is impossible on the correct view.

Core Idea
Only affirmative disjunctives take 'ever' and 'always'; the natural conditional is impossible because the judgment is always quantified.

The passage continues with clarifications on quantifiers for disjunctive propositions and the concept of the natural conditional. It explains that 'abadan' and 'da'iman' are used only in affirmative disjunctions, and that the natural conditional is only conceivable on the incorrect view that quantification depends on parts. These details refine our understanding of propositional quantification in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between conditional and disjunctive quantifiers
The passage notes that the difference in quantifier usage between conditionals and disjunctives is only in the affirmative. For conditionals, quantifiers like 'kullama' are used; for disjunctives, 'abadan' and 'da'iman' are used only when the disjunction is affirmative.
2
Note that only affirmative disjunctives take these quantifiers
The commentator suggests it would be clearer to explicitly say 'in the affirmative disjunction' to avoid the misunderstanding that 'ever' and 'always' apply to all disjunctives.
3
Grasp why the natural conditional is impossible on the correct view
Since the judgment is always quantified (universal, particular, or singular) based on the suppositions of the antecedent, there is no room for a natural (unrestricted) conditional. This follows from the main argument.
4
See the example of permissible natural conditional on the opposing view
On the incorrect view (quantification depends on parts), a natural conditional would be allowed, e.g., 'whenever a thing is a human, it is a species; whenever it is an animal, it is a genus.' Note that here the parts are not quantified, so the conditional is natural.
5
Recognize the practical implication
This distinction prevents errors in interpreting conditional statements in texts. For instance, in legal reasoning, misapplying quantifier rules could lead to incorrect conclusions about the scope of rulings.

(۲۷) لا يذهب عليك : ان حصر الشرطية و اهمالها و شخصیتها لیست بسبب حصر الاجزاء و إهمالها و شخصيتها، بل انما هى باعتبار الحكم كما صرح به المصنف وقد ذهب جماعة إلى أنها بسبب الاجزاء فإن كانت كلية كقولنا ان كان كل انسان حيواناً فكل كاتب حيوان، فالشرطية كلية، و إن كانت شخصية كقولنا كلما كان زيد يكتب فهو يحرك يده فهى شخصية و ان كانت مهملة كقولنا : كلما كان انسان كاتباً فيكون متحرك الاصابع، فهى مهملة وهكذا قال بعض المحققين في شرح المطالع. ولو نظروا بعين التحقيق لوجدوا الأمر بخلاف ذلك فان الحملية لم تكن كلية لاجل كلية الموضوع و المحمول بل لاجل كلية الحكم الذى هو هناك حمل و نظيره هيهنا اتصال وعناد فكما يجب في الحمليات ان ينظر الى الحكم لا الى الاجزاء كذلك في الشرطيات بحسب ارتباط تلك الاحوال بالحكم انتهى . و قد استفيد من هذا فايدة اخرى وهي: ان الحصر والاهمال في الحملية ايضاً باعتبار الحكم لا الموضوع و المحمول و كلام المحشى ايضاً لا يخلو عن ايماء الى ذلك و قد خالف في ذلك جماعة ايضاً و هو ظاهر كلام بعض المحققين من شراح المتن حيث قال: ان مدار الاقسام المذكورة فى الشرطيات انما كان على الحكم لا على الموضوع كما في الحمليات. و اظن ان الذي أوقعهم في الشبهة انهم رأوا انه كلما كان الموضوع كلياً، تكون القضية كلية او جزئياً، تكون جزئية او شخصياً، تكون شخصية وهكذا فحكموا ان الكلية والجزئية وغيرهما انما هي لاجل كلية الموضوع وجزئيته ، و لم يدروا ان ذلك انما هو بسبب الاتفاق.و كيف كان، فالقول بذلك بعيد من التحقيق.. اللهم إلا ان يريدوا ان كلية الموضوع و شخصيته مثلاً تكون باعثة لكلية الحكم وشخصيته وهى لكلية القضية و شخصيتها، لكن المتبادر من السبب انما هو القريب فتأمل. (27) Let it not escape you: the universality, particularity, and singularity of the conditional are not due to the universality, particularity, and singularity of the parts; rather, they are based on the judgment, as the author clarified. A group has held that they are due to the parts: if it is universal like our saying 'if every human is an animal, then every writer is an animal', then the conditional is universal; if it is singular like our saying 'whenever Zayd writes, he moves his hand', then it is singular; if it is indefinite like our saying 'whenever a human is a writer, he moves his fingers', then it is indefinite. Thus said some verifying commentators in Sharh al-Matali'. But if they looked with the eye of verification, they would find the matter to be otherwise. For the categorical is not universal because of the universality of the subject and predicate, but because of the universality of the judgment which is predication therein; and its counterpart here is connection and opposition. So just as in categoricals one must look to the judgment and not to the parts, similarly in conditionals with respect to the relation of those states to the judgment. End. Another benefit is derived from this: that universality and particularity in categoricals are also based on the judgment, not the subject and predicate; and the commentator's words also allude to that. Some have differed on this as well, as is apparent from the words of some verifying commentators among the explainers of the text, who said: the basis of the mentioned divisions in conditionals is the judgment, not the subject, just as in categoricals. I think what led them into confusion is that they saw that whenever the subject is universal, the proposition is universal; or particular, it is particular; or singular, it is singular; and so on. So they ruled that universality and particularity are due to the universality and particularity of the subject. And they did not know that that is only by coincidence. However it may be, that view is far from verification. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (28) That is because according to what was established earlier, the division of the conditional into the mentioned divisions is based on the judgment regarding the suppositions of the antecedent as a whole, a part, or otherwise. So it is impossible to conceive of a natural (tabi‘iyya) proposition, as is clear to the ponderer. And it is not hidden that the impossibility of conceiving a natural conditional is according to the correct doctrine; but according to what the group claimed, what is required by the apparent meaning of their words is its permissibility, as in our saying 'whenever a thing is a human, it is a species' and 'whenever it is an animal, it is a genus'. So ponder. (Muhammad Ali) (29) That is, meaning one of these three from any language. (Abd al-Rahim) (30) It would be better for him to say: 'and in the affirmative disjunction', as in the coordinated phrase, so that he would not need to say 'this in the affirmative', meaning that 'abadan' (ever) and 'da'iman' (always) are quantifiers for the disjunction only in the affirmative. (Abd al-Rahim) (31) The difference between the conditional and the disjunctive in the quantifier is only in the affirmative, as indicated by his statement afterwards: 'and as for the free (sa'iba) disjunction absolutely, whether it is conditional or disjunctive'. And it does not mean that 'da'iman' and 'abadan' are quantifiers for the disjunction only in the affirmative, as is the seemingly understood meaning, such that it is said: it would be better for him to say 'and in the affirmative disjunction' instead of his statement 'and in the disjunction', as he said 'and in the affirmative conditional', so that he would not need this, or he could omit the word 'affirmative' here as well. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (32) An indication that the absoluteness therein is with respect to the restriction of specification, not absolutely, so that the intended meaning is 'or on some part absolutely' without any specification or its absence being considered, by the context of his statement 'or specified'. (Muhammad Ali) (33) For the judgment of the affirmation of humanity is not upon all suppositions of the affirmation of animality for a thing, for that may be in that it is a horse, a donkey, or other types of animal, nor upon some specified part, as is apparent; for specification...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage critically examines the basis for the quantification (hasr), particularity (ihmāl), and singularity (shakhsiyya) of conditional propositions. It begins by stating the correct view: these properties are not due to the quantification of the parts (i.e., the antecedent and consequent) but rather due to the judgment (hukm) itself, as the author of the original text stated. Some scholars have opposed this, arguing that if the parts are universal, the conditional is universal, giving examples like 'if every human is an animal, then every writer is an animal'. The passage argues that if they had investigated properly, they would see that in categorical propositions, universality is not because of the subject and predicate but because of the judgment of predication. Similarly, in conditionals, the relation (connection or opposition) between the antecedent and consequent is what determines the quantification. 

A secondary benefit: even in categoricals, quantification depends on the judgment, not the subject/predicate. The commentator alludes to this. The passage then suggests that the confusion arises from noticing that in categoricals, whenever the subject is universal, the proposition is universal, etc., leading them to think that the subject's quantification is the cause, whereas it is merely coincidental. The view that quantification is due to the parts is rejected as far from verification, though a tempered view that subject quantification may be a remote cause is allowed.

Next, the passage explains why a 'natural' conditional (tabi‘iyya) is impossible on the correct view: since the division is based on the judgment regarding all, some, or a single supposition of the antecedent, there is no room for a natural proposition that is unrestricted. However, on the opposing view, it would be permissible, e.g., 'whenever a thing is a human, it is a species; whenever it is an animal, it is a genus'.

Finally, several glosses clarify details about the quantifiers for disjunction: 'abadan' and 'da'iman' are used only in affirmative disjunctions, and the difference between conditionals and disjunctions in quantifier usage is only in the affirmative. Also, a note on the scope of 'absoluteness' in the text, indicating it is with respect to the restriction of specification. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing a specific judgment about animality.

On the correct view, every conditional is either universal, particular, or singular; a natural (unrestricted) conditional is inconceivable.

The Real Basis of Quantification in Conditionals
Author refutes that conditional quantification follows the subject and asserts it follows the judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition's quantification is determined by its antecedent term or by the judgment, and correctly classify examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Quantification in conditionals depends on the judgment's scope, not the antecedent's universality.

In this passage, Mirza Muhammad Ali corrects a common error among logicians: thinking that a conditional's universality comes from the antecedent term. He argues that just as in categoricals the judgment's scope determines quantification, in conditionals it is the connection or disjunction covering all or some antecedent suppositions. This lesson will help you apply this principle to avoid misclassification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mistaken view
The mistaken logicians saw a correlation: universal antecedent leads to universal conditional. They concluded the antecedent's universality is the cause. Note that they confused correlation with causation.
2
Identify the correct view from the author
The author says quantification follows the judgment (hukm). In conditionals, the judgment is the connection or disjunction covering all, some, or one antecedent supposition. So the judgment's scope is the real basis.
3
Test with examples from the passage
Example: 'Whenever Zayd writes, he moves his hand' – this is personal because the antecedent supposition is a specific person (Zayd), not because 'Zayd' is a singular term per se. The judgment covers that specific supposition.
4
Apply to new examples from Islamic sciences
Consider: 'Whenever a person prays with concentration, their heart is humble.' Is this universal, particular, or personal? The antecedent's term 'person' is universal, but the correct classification depends on whether the connection holds for every instance of 'prayer with concentration' or only some. The judgment's scope decides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(میرزا محمد علی)
Regarding the divisions of the conditional: it may be personal, like our saying 'Whenever Zayd writes, he moves his hand' — this is personal. And if it is unrestricted, like our saying 'Whenever a human is a writer, he moves his fingers' — this is unrestricted. This is what some investigators said in Sharh al-Matali'. But if they looked with the eye of verification, they would find the matter contrary to this. For the categorical is not universal because of the universality of the subject and predicate, but because of the universality of the judgment, which is predication there, and its analogue here is connection (ittisal) and opposition ('inad). So just as in categoricals one must look at the judgment, not the parts, likewise in conditionals with respect to the relation of those states to the judgment. End. And from this another benefit is derived: that universality and particularity in the categorical also are by consideration of the judgment, not the subject and predicate. And the words of the glossator also allude to that. And a group have also opposed this, and it is apparent in the words of some investigators among the commentators of the text where he said: 'The pivot of the mentioned divisions in conditionals is only the judgment, not the subject, as in categoricals.' And I think what caused them to fall into confusion is that they saw that whenever the subject is universal, the proposition is universal; or particular; it is particular; or personal; it is personal; and so on. So they judged that universality and particularity and others are only the universality and particularity of the subject. And they did not know that this is only coincidental. And however it may be, this saying is far from verification. Unless they mean that the universality of the subject and its personality, for example, are the cause for the universality of the judgment and its personality, which is for the universality of the proposition and its personality. But what is understood from 'cause' is the proximate cause, so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the glossator (Mirza Muhammad Ali) correcting a common misunderstanding about the quantification of conditional propositions. The author begins by presenting a view from some investigators in Sharh al-Matali' that classified conditionals as personal (shakhsiyya) and unrestricted (muhmala) based on examples like 'Whenever Zayd writes, he moves his hand' (personal) and 'Whenever a human is a writer, he moves his fingers' (unrestricted). But the author then refutes this, arguing that if they had investigated properly they would find the truth is the opposite: the universality or particularity of a conditional is not based on the antecedent or consequent being universal, but on the judgment (hukm) which in conditionals is the relation of connection (ittisal) or opposition ('inad) covering all or some of the antecedent's suppositions. Just as in categorical propositions the universality is about the judgment (i.e., the predication applies to all individuals of the subject), here the judgment applies to all or some of the possibilities of the antecedent. The author then brings a general benefit: the same principle applies to categorical propositions — their quantification is also based on judgment, not on the subject/predicate terms, and the glossator's own words allude to this. Then the author notes that a group of scholars opposed this correct view, citing a statement from some commentators of the matn who explicitly said the division in conditionals is based on judgment, not subject. The author speculates that the source of confusion for those who oppose is that they observed a correlation: when subject is universal, the proposition is usually universal; when particular, particular; etc. They mistakenly concluded that universality comes from the subject's universality, not realizing this is mere coincidence (ittifaq). The author concludes that their view is far from verification (ba'id min al-tahqiq), unless they mean that the subject's universality is a remote cause (ba'itha) for the judgment's universality, but the apparent meaning in the term 'cause' is the proximate cause, so their phrasing is imprecise.

The quantification of a conditional proposition is determined by the judgment's coverage of antecedent suppositions, not by the antecedent term's universality.

Impossibility of the Natural Conditional
Author argues the natural conditional is impossible on the correct view of conditional quantification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the natural conditional is impossible on the correct view of conditional quantification and distinguish it from the opposing view that permits it.

Core Idea
Every conditional must quantify over antecedent suppositions; an unquantified natural conditional is inconceivable.

In earlier lessons, we learned that conditional propositions are divided based on whether the judgment (connection or disjunction) covers all, some, or none of the antecedent's suppositions. This division leaves no room for a 'natural' conditional that lacks any such quantification. In this passage, the author refines the classification by showing that on the correct view, the natural conditional is impossible, while a rival school mistakenly allows it. We will unpack this critical point and see how it affects our understanding of conditionals in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the division of conditionals
Remember that conditionals are divided into universal (covering all antecedent suppositions), particular (covering some), personal (single), and unrestricted (general/partitive). This division is based on the judgment's scope over the antecedent's suppositions, not on the subject term.
2
Identify what a natural conditional would be
A natural conditional (tabi'iyya) is one where the judgment is about the nature of the antecedent itself, without any quantification. For example, 'If it is human, it is a species' — but this does not specify whether it covers all humans or only some.
3
Understand why it is impossible on the correct view
Since every conditional must fall under one of the quantified divisions (universal, particular, etc.), there is no separate category for an unquantified natural conditional. The very definition of a conditional entails a quantified judgment over antecedent suppositions.
The correct view (madhhab haqq) is that quantification is on the judgment, not the antecedent term.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
The opposing group (al-jama'a) thought quantification attaches to the antecedent term itself. On their view, a natural conditional like 'Whenever a thing is human, it is a species' would be permissible because it does not specify quantification. The author shows this example but warns to reflect on its flaws.
5
Reflect on the example
Consider the example: 'Whenever a thing is human, it is a species; whenever it is an animal, it is a genus.' This appears natural because it does not say 'all' or 'some'. On the correct view, such a statement would be incomplete as a conditional; it must be specified as universal or particular.
The author says 'fa ta'ammal' (so reflect), inviting the reader to see the error in the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لما تقرر آنفاً ان انقسام الشرطية بالاقسام المذكورة انما هو باعتبار الحكم على من تقادير المقدم كلاً أو بعضاً أو غير ذلك فح لا يمكن تعقل الطبيعية كما هو ظاهر للمتأمل . ولا يخفى ان عدم تعقل الطبيعية في الشرطية انما هو على المذهب الحق و اما على ما زعمه الجماعة فالذي يقتضيه ظاهر كلماتهم انما هو جوازه كقولنا كلما كان الشيء انساناً كان نوعاً وكلما كان حيواناً كان جنساً فتأمل. (محمد على)
And that is because it was established earlier that the division of the conditional into the mentioned divisions is only by consideration of the judgment covering the antecedent's suppositions, all or some or otherwise. Therefore, it is not possible to conceive of the natural (tabi'iyya) conditional, as is apparent to the reflective person. And it is not hidden that the inconceivability of the natural conditional is only according to the correct position. But according to what the group (al-jama'a) claimed, what the apparent meaning of their words necessitates is its permissibility, like our saying: 'Whenever a thing is a human, it is a species; and whenever it is an animal, it is a genus.' So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Muhammad Ali (the glossator) explaining a logical consequence of the correct view on conditional quantification. The author recaps that the division of conditionals (into universal, particular, personal, unrestricted) depends on whether the judgment (the relation of connection or disjunction) covers all (kull), some (ba'd), or none of the antecedent's suppositions (taqadir al-muqaddam). Since every conditional must be quantified in this way, there is no room for a 'natural' (tabi'iyya) conditional, which would be a conditional where no quantification is specified and the judgment is simply about the nature of the antecedent itself. On the correct view, such a conditional is impossible because the very nature of a conditional proposition requires a judgment that either covers all, some, or a specific supposition. The author then notes that this impossibility is only on the correct view. According to the opposing group (those who mistakenly base quantification on the subject term), the natural conditional would be permissible. The author gives an example of what such a natural conditional would look like according to their view: 'Whenever a thing is a human, it is a species; and whenever it is an animal, it is a genus' — these would be natural because they do not specify whether the judgment covers all or some humans/animals. But the author refrains from outright rejection and says 'so reflect' (fa ta'ammal), inviting the reader to consider the flaws in the opposing view.

Key Terms
طبيعية (tabi'iyya): A conditional proposition without explicit quantification, where the judgment is about the nature (tab') of the antecedent. Considered impossible on the correct view.
مذهب حق (madhhab haqq): The correct position that conditional quantification is based on the judgment covering antecedent suppositions, not on the antecedent term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim the natural conditional (tabi'iyya) is impossible on the correct view?
A) Because conditionals must have antecedent and consequent terms.
B) Because the division of conditionals is based on the judgment covering all, some, or none of the antecedent's suppositions, leaving no room for unquantified natural conditionals.
C) Because natural conditionals are only possible in categorical propositions.
D) Because the group (al-jama'a) allowed them, so they are impossible.

Key Takeaway
On the correct view, every conditional must be quantified over antecedent suppositions, making the natural conditional impossible; the opposing view's permissibility stems from a different understanding of quantification.

Temporal Quantifiers in Affirmative Disjunctives
Author clarifies that 'abadan' and 'da'iman' are quantifiers only for affirmative disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct application of temporal quantifiers (abadan/da'iman) to disjunctive conditionals and distinguish between affirmative and negative cases.

Core Idea
Temporal quantifiers 'abadan' and 'da'iman' apply only to affirmative disjunctive conditionals.

In this passage, two glossators clarify a subtle but crucial point about quantifiers in disjunctive conditionals. Understanding that the words 'always' and 'forever' are only used as quantifiers for affirmative disjunctives prevents misapplications in logical reasoning. This refinement shows how precision in phrasing can avoid ambiguity in Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the original claim about quantifiers
The original author (of the matn) likely said 'wa fi al-munfasila' (and in the disjunctive) regarding quantifiers. Your task is to identify what general claim is being made about quantifiers and disjunctives.
The original phrasing is not in the source chunk, but the glossators refer to it.
2
Identify Abd al-Rahim's proposed refinement
Abd al-Rahim suggests replacing 'wa fi al-munfasila' with 'wa fi al-munfasila al-mujiba' (in the affirmative disjunctive). Why? Because the quantifiers 'abadan' and 'da'iman' are only used for affirmative disjunctives. Note the logical reason: these quantifiers indicate that the disjunction holds at all times, which aligns with the affirmative mode.
3
Trace Mirza Muhammad Ali's elaboration
He expands on the point, warning against a mistaken understanding: some might think the quantifiers apply to disjunctives only in the affirmative (in a restrictive sense). He clarifies that the difference between conjunctive and disjunctive quantifiers is only in the affirmative case, as supported by the author's later remark about 'sa'iba' (unconditional).
This step requires careful reading of the second gloss.
4
Apply the rule to a theological example
Construct an affirmative disjunctive proposition with 'always' and test its validity. For instance: 'Either the world is created or it is eternal, always.' This is an affirmative disjunctive because it asserts that at all times, one of the two holds. Contrast with a negative disjunctive like 'It is not the case that either the world is created or it is eternal, always' — which would misuse the quantifier.
This step solidifies the rule.

This passage is a critical refinement by two glossators (Abd al-Rahim and Mirza Muhammad Ali) discussing the phrasing of a previous commentator regarding quantifiers for disjunctive conditionals. The first note (by Abd al-Rahim) suggests that it would be better for the original author to say 'And in the affirmative disjunctive' instead of just 'And in the disjunctive,' because the quantifiers 'abadan' (forever) and 'da'iman' (always) are specific to affirmative disjunctive propositions. The second note (by Mirza Muhammad Ali) elaborates: the difference between conjunctive and disjunctive in terms of quantifiers is only in the affirmative case. He points to a later statement about 'sa'iba' (unconcerned, i.e., unconditional) to show that the quantifiers are only for affirmative. The Six Divisions of Conditional Propositions
Gloss expands on the six divisions of conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six divisions of conditional propositions when the two sides are of different types, and distinguish them from the total fifteen divisions.

Core Idea
Six divisions arise only when the two sides differ; the total is fifteen.

This gloss clarifies a key classification of conditional propositions (shartiyya) in Islamic logic. The original text mentions six divisions, but these apply only when the two components (sides) are of different types. The gloss expands to show the full combinatorial picture: 15 types in total—9 for connected conditionals (muttasila) and 6 for disconnected (munfasila). Mastering this taxonomy is essential for correctly analysing compound propositions that appear in theological and jurisprudential arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of conditionals
Conditional propositions are divided into connected (muttasila, 'if...then') and disjunctive (munfasila, 'either...or'). The gloss refers to both.
2
Recognize the three types of components
Each side of a conditional can itself be a categorical (hamliyya), a connected conditional (muttasila), or a disjunctive conditional (munfasila). These three types combine to produce the classifications.
3
Understand the restriction to 'different types'
The six divisions mentioned in the text apply only when the two sides are of different types (mukhtalifatayn). Identical pairs are excluded. For example, a conditional whose both sides are categorical is not among the six.
4
Compute the total number of conditional divisions
For a connected conditional, the number of possible combinations of the two sides is 3×3 = 9. Subtracting the three identical cases leaves 6. These six, plus the six for disconnected conditionals (excluding identical pairs), total 15 divisions for conditionals absolutely.
The identical cases for disconnected conditionals are similarly excluded.
5
Use the table to see concrete examples
The glossator provides a table (on the following page) with examples of each division. Study that table to connect the abstract divisions to real logical statements.
If the table is not available, generate your own examples using fiqh or kalam statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a gloss (hashiya) explaining the classification of conditional propositions (shartiyya). It first defines the disjunctive particles 'ama' and 'aw' and notes the ambiguity of the conjunction particle. Then it clarifies a statement from the original text: 'So the divisions are six' refers to the divisions arising when the two components of a conditional are of different types (mukhtalifatayn). Why the Three Additional Divisions Exclude Munfasila
Explains why three subdivisions apply only to muttasila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the three additional subdivisions of conditional propositions apply only to muttasila and not to munfasila, using the concepts of malzum, lazim, and mu'anada.

Core Idea
In disjunctive conditionals, antecedent and consequent are symmetric; only connected conditionals have fixed natural roles.

You have learned that conditional propositions are divided into six types. However, the last three subdivisions—which depend on the nature of the antecedent (whether it is categorical, connected, or disconnected)—only apply to muttasila (connected conditionals), not to munfasila (disconnected conditionals). This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya explains why: the relationship between antecedent and consequent in muttasila is one of necessary implication (luzum), with fixed roles, while in munfasila it is mutual opposition (mu'anada), making the parts interchangeable. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key concepts
Locate the terms malzum (implier) and lazim (necessary) in the passage. Note how they describe the roles in muttasila. Then find mu'anad (opponent) for munfasila. Understand that malzum is the sufficient condition, lazim is the necessary consequence, while mu'anad indicates mutual exclusivity.
2
Analyze the natural roles in muttasila
In muttasila, the antecedent is naturally the malzum and the consequent the lazim. This asymmetry is inherent (tab'). Swapping them changes the logical meaning. For example, 'If the sun rises, then day exists' is not equivalent to 'If day exists, then the sun rises'.
The passage emphasizes that roles are fixed by nature, not arbitrary.
3
Examine the symmetric relationship in munfasila
In munfasila, each part is mu'anad to the other. The opposition is mutual: if A is opposite B, then B is opposite A. Therefore, the concepts of 'antecedent' and 'consequent' are merely notational; swapping them yields no logical difference. For example, 'Either it is day or it is night' is the same as 'Either it is night or it is day'.
The passage states that the assignment of antecedent/consequent is by mere placement (bi-mujarrad wad'), not by nature.
4
Apply the distinction to the three subdivisions
The three additional subdivisions classify muttasila based on whether the antecedent is categorical, connected, or disconnected. In muttasila, the antecedent's type matters because its role is fixed. In munfasila, since parts are symmetric, the same classification would be redundant or meaningless. Hence the subdivisions do not apply to munfasila.
The passage also notes that conversion (aks) does not apply to munfasila for the same reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كانت أقسام المنفصلة ستة ولم يجر فيها الأقسام الثلاثة الأخيرة كما جرت في المتصلة لأن مقدم المتصلة متميز عن تاليها بحسب الطبع والمفهوم فإن مفهوم المقدم فيها الملزوم ومفهوم التالي اللازم ويحتمل أن يكون الشيء ملزوماً للآخر ولا يكون لازماً له، فالمقدم في المتصلة متعين أن يكون مقدماً والتالي متعين أن يكون تالياً بخلاف المنفصلة فإن مفهوم التالي فيها المعاند ومفهوم المقدم المعاند والمعاند لابد أن يكون معانداً أيضاً لأن عناد أحد الشيئين للآخر في قوة عناد الآخر إياه فحال كل واحد من جزئيها عند الآخر حال واحد وإنما عرض لأحدهما أن يكون مقدماً والآخر أن يكون تالياً بمجرد وضع لا طبع، ففرق ما بين المتصلة المركبة من الحملية والمتصلة والمقدم فيها الحملية وبينها والمقدم فيها المتصلة بخلاف المنفصلة المركبة منها فلا فرق بين ما كان المقدم فيهما الحملية أو المتصلة وكذلك الحال في المركبة من الحملية ومن المتصلة والمنفصلة ولذا لا يجري فيها العكس كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then, if the divisions of the disconnected are six, the last three divisions did not apply to it as they applied to the connected, because the antecedent of the connected is distinguished from its consequent by nature and concept: the concept of the antecedent in it is the 'implied' (malzum) and the concept of the consequent is the 'necessary' (lazim), and it is possible that a thing is implied by another without being necessary for it. Thus, in the connected, the antecedent is necessarily the antecedent and the consequent is necessarily the consequent, unlike the disconnected, for the concept of the consequent in it is the 'opposing' (mu'anid) and the concept of the antecedent is also opposing, and the opposing must also be opposing because the opposition of one of two things to the other is equivalent to the opposition of the other to it. So the state of each of its two parts with respect to the other is one state; it is merely accidental that one becomes antecedent and the other consequent by mere placement, not by nature. So there is a difference between the connected composed of a categorical and a connected, where the antecedent is the categorical, and between it where the antecedent is the connected, unlike the disconnected composed of them, for there is no difference whether the antecedent in them is the categorical or the connected; and likewise in the case of the composite of a categorical and of a connected and a disconnected. Therefore, conversion (aks) does not apply to it, as will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage explains why the last three subdivisions of conditional propositions (listed in the previous table) apply only to muttasila (connected conditionals) and not to munfasila (disconnected conditionals). The reason is rooted in the nature of the relationship between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). In muttasila, the antecedent is the 'implied' (malzum) — the sufficient condition — and the consequent is the 'necessary' (lazim) — what follows from it. They have fixed roles by nature (tab'): you cannot swap them without changing the meaning. For example, 'If the sun rises, then day exists' is different from 'If day exists, then the sun rises'; the latter might be false while the former is true. In contrast, in munfasila (disjunctive), the relationship is mutual opposition (mu'anada): 'either A or B' is the same as 'either B or A'. The two parts are logically symmetric; which one is placed as antecedent and which as consequent is arbitrary (bi-mujarrad wad'), with no natural priority. Therefore, the three subtypes that depend on the nature of the antecedent (whether it is categorical, connected, or disconnected) create distinctions in muttasila but collapse into sameness in munfasila. For instance, a muttasila composed of a categorical and a connected proposition differs depending on which part is the antecedent; but in munfasila, swapping the parts yields no difference. Consequently, the operation of conversion (aks) — which is valid for muttasila under certain conditions — does not apply to munfasila, as will be explained later. This passage underscores the importance of recognizing the inherent asymmetry in conditional relationships for correct logical analysis.

Key Terms
ملزوم
لازم
معاند
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because muttasila is always true, while munfasila is always false.
C) Because the antecedent and consequent in muttasila are both categorical propositions.
D) Because the three additional subdivisions apply only to munfasila.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between muttasila's asymmetric implication and munfasila's symmetric opposition determines which logical operations and classifications apply.

The Consequent as Insha'i: A Critical Refinement
Critique of the claim that both parts of a conditional are originally propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a performative (insha'i) consequent and explain why it challenges the logician's requirement that both parts of a conditional be propositions.

Core Idea
Not every part of a conditional is a proposition; performative consequents lack truth-value.

The author of the matn claimed that both parts of a conditional are originally propositions. Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim refines this claim: the antecedent is conceded, but the consequent may be insha'i (performative), which does not admit truth-value. This lesson explores the refinement and its implications for distinguishing logic from grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's original claim
Find in the commentary the statement: 'the two parts of the conditional are originally propositions.' This is the base claim being refined.
The claim is attributed to al-muṣannif (the author of the matn).
2
Locate the objection
Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim says: 'This is conceded regarding the antecedent, but as for the consequent, no.' Mark where he denies the claim for the consequent.
3
Understand the reason: insha'i
The consequent may be insha'i (performative), which cannot be true or false, so it is not a proposition in the logical sense.
Insha'i includes commands, prohibitions, wishes, etc.
4
Examine the proposed solution
Some scholars estimate (bi-l-taqdīr) the insha'i as a declarative statement (maqul) to preserve the claim that both parts are propositions.
This is a way to reconcile the objection with the original claim.
5
Distinguish logical vs. grammatical perspectives
Logicians exclude insha'i from truth-bearing propositions; Arabic linguists treat it as a complete utterance. Confusing these leads to errors.
The commentator warns against mixing terminologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شيخ عبدالرحيم (ه) (٤١) هذا في المقدم مسلم وأما في التالي فلا لأن الجزاء قد تكون إنشائياً، فقول المصنف «وطرفا الشرطية في الأصل قضيتان» ممنوع. ويمكن أن نقول: بأن نقدر ونجعل الجزاء مقولاً كما هو رأي بعضهم فيا كان الإنشاء جزاء. ثم لا يخفى أن خروج الجزاء عن الخبرية واحتمال الصدق والكذب إنما هو عند المنطقيين وأما عند أهل العربية فهو باق بحاله فاحفظ هذا حتى لا تختلط كلامهم بكلامهم كما وقع عليه بعض الأكابر. (عبدالرحيم)
Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim (may God have mercy on him) (41): This is conceded regarding the antecedent, but as for the consequent, no, because the consequent may be insha'i (performative). So the author's statement 'the two parts of the conditional are originally propositions' is denied. And it is possible to say: we estimate and make the consequent a [declarative] statement (maqul), as is the opinion of some, so that the insha'i is a consequent. Then it is not hidden that the departure of the consequent from declarative status and from the possibility of truth and falsehood is only according to the logicians; as for the Arabic linguists, it remains in its original state. So preserve this so that you do not confuse their speech with each other's, as happened to some of the greats. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) by Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim on the author's claim that 'the two parts of a conditional are originally propositions (qadiyatān)'. 'Abd al-Rahim concedes that the antecedent (muqaddam) is indeed a proposition, but he denies that the consequent (tālī) necessarily is, because the consequent may be an inshā'ī (performative) utterance — such as a command, prohibition, or wish — which does not have truth-value (sidq or kidhb). In standard logic, only declarative (khabariyya) propositions are bearers of truth and falsehood. To salvage the author's view, one could reinterpret the inshā'ī consequent as a declarative statement implicitly (bi-l-taqdīr), as some scholars do. Then 'Abd al-Rahim adds a cautionary note: the exclusion of inshā'ī from truth-value is strictly a logical (mantiqī) perspective; Arabic linguists (ahl al-'arabiyya) still treat inshā'ī as a complete utterance with its own grammatical status. Confusing these two domains has led some great scholars into error. The student should be careful not to mix the terminologies of logicians and grammarians. This passage highlights an important boundary between logic and Arabic grammar.

Key Terms
إنشائي
قضية
جزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it may be an insha'i (performative) utterance without truth-value.
C) Because the antecedent already supplies the truth-value.
D) Because conditional particles remove the propositional status.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between logical and grammatical perspectives is crucial: logicians require truth-value for propositions, while grammarians accept performative sentences as valid, so one must not conflate the two.
