When Does Removing a Conditional Particle Produce Independent Propositions?
Debates whether removing the conditional particle alone suffices to make the parts independent propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate al-Jurjani's objection against the view that removing the conditional particle suffices for propositionhood, and apply the distinction between intelligible and uttered propositions to resolve the debate.

Core Idea
Removing the conditional particle does not automatically restore judgment; a basis for truth is also required.

In this passage, we explore a key debate in conditional logic: does deleting the conditional particle (e.g., 'if') from a conditional statement like 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray' automatically turn its parts into independent true-or-false propositions? Al-Jurjani argues it does not—using a striking counterexample. We'll walk through his reasoning and a proposed resolution based on whether we consider mental or spoken propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The author (al-Muhaqqiq?) held that removing the conditional particle alone makes the parts propositions. Al-Jurjani disagreed, demanding a positive basis (muqtada) for the judgment.
The passage states 'ظاهر عبارة المصنف في بعض كتبه هو الأول' – the apparent wording of the author supports the first view.
2
Examine al-Jurjani's counterexample
Consider 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray.' Both parts are false (Zaid is not a donkey, he does not bray), yet the conditional is true. Removing the particle yields two false statements, showing that truth does not automatically follow.
Arabic: 'إن كان زيد حماراً كان ناهقاً' – both parts known false, yet the conditional holds.
3
Understand the principle at stake
Al-Jurjani argues that removal of an impediment (the conditional particle) is insufficient; the existence of a basis (muqtada) for the judgment is necessary. The particle was not preventing an existing judgment but suspending it.
The text says 'زوال المانع لا يكفي في وجود الشيء بل لابد من وجود المقتضى'.
4
Apply the resolution: intelligible vs. uttered proposition
If the debate is about the intelligible proposition (mental content), al-Jurjani's objection holds. If it is about the uttered proposition (spoken sentence), the wording after removal still contains the judgment, so the objection fails.
The passage concludes: if 'القضية المعقولة' then objection valid; if 'القضية الملفوظة' then it is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤٢) ينبغي أن يعلم أنهما أي المقدم والتالي هل يصيران قضيتين بمجرد حذف الأداة أم لا؟ ظاهر عبارة المصنف في بعض كتبه هو الأول وخالفه المحقق الشريف فقال: إن مجرد حذف الأداة لا يكون في عود الحكم حتى يصيرا قضيتين بل لابد من وجود المقتضى أيضاً. وقال في حاشيته على الشرح المشهور للرسالة الشمسية إن من زعم أنه إذا حذف الأداة فقد وجد الحكم، فقد أخطأ، فكيف ذلك في مثل قولك: إن كان زيد حماراً كان ناهقاً مع العلم بكذب الطرفين وصدق الشرطية؟ لا يقال: الأدوات كانت مانعة عن الحكم فإذا زالت عاد الحكم. لأن زوال المانع لا يكفي في وجود الشيء بل لابد من وجود المقتضى وزوال المانع لا يستلزمه كما في المثال المذكور، انتهى. وقد يقال: إن كان النزاع في القضية المعقولة، فالاعتراض حق وإن كان في القضية الملفوظة كما هو الظاهر فالاعتراض ساقط لظهور أن طرفي الشرطية بعد حذف الأدوات موافقان في اللفظ المشتمل على الحكم قبل ذلك، فصح القول بأن طرفي الشرطية قضيتان بعد حذف الأدوات انتهى فتأمل. (عبد الرحيم)
(42) It should be known that the two, i.e., the antecedent and consequent, do they become propositions merely by removing the particle or not? The apparent wording of the author in some of his books is the first opinion, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif disagreed and said: Mere removal of the particle does not result in the return of the judgment so that they become two propositions; rather, the existence of a basis (muqtada) is also necessary. And he said in his gloss on the famous commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya that whoever claims that when the particle is removed, the judgment exists, has erred. How can that be in a statement like: 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray,' given the known falsehood of both parts and the truth of the conditional? It cannot be said: The particles were preventing the judgment, so when they are removed, the judgment returns. Because the removal of an impediment is not sufficient for the existence of a thing; rather, the existence of the basis is necessary, and the removal of the impediment does not entail it, as in the mentioned example. End quote. And it may be said: If the dispute is about the intelligible proposition (ma'qula), then the objection is valid; but if it is about the uttered proposition (malguza) — as is apparent — then the objection is invalid, because it is clear that the two parts of the conditional after the removal of the particles agree in wording containing the judgment as before. So the statement that the two parts of the conditional are two propositions after the removal of the particles is correct. End quote, so reflect. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents a debate about whether the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) of a conditional proposition become independent propositions (qadiyatān) simply by removing the conditional particle (adaāt al-sharṭ). The author (al-Muhaqqiq?) apparently held the positive view in some works. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif (al-Jurjani) disagreed, arguing that removing the particle does not automatically restore the judgment (hukm) that the parts are truth-bearing; rather, a positive basis (muqtada) for that judgment is also required. He uses a classic counterexample: 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray' (in kāna Zaydun ḥimāran kāna nāhiqan). Here, both parts are known to be false (Zaid is not a donkey, and he does not bray), yet the conditional statement is considered true (since it does not assert the reality of the antecedent). If we remove the conditional particle, we get two separate statements: 'Zaid is a donkey' and 'Zaid is brays' — both false, but the conditional remains true. This shows that the conditional particle suspends the claim of actuality, and simply removing it does not reinstate that claim. The objection that 'the particle was preventing the judgment, so when it is removed the judgment returns' is refuted: removal of an impediment (mane') is not enough; the basis (muqtada) must also be present. Following this, a possible reconciliation is offered: if the debate is about the 'intelligible proposition' (al-qadiyya al-ma'qula) — the abstract mental proposition — then al-Sharif's objection stands; but if it is about the 'uttered proposition' (al-qadiyya al-malguza) — the actual spoken sentence — the wording after removal still contains the judgment (e.g., 'Zaid is a donkey' is a full declarative sentence), so the objection fails. 'Abd al-Rahim ends with 'fa-ta'amul' (so reflect), indicating that the issue is subtle.

Key Terms
أداة الشرط
الحكم
المقتضى
قضية معقولة
قضية ملفوظة

Key Takeaway
Removing the conditional particle does not automatically produce independent true-or-false propositions; the distinction between intelligible and uttered propositions reconciles the opposing views.

Conditional Particles Strip Propositions of Independence
Explains that adding a conditional particle removes the proposition's self-sufficiency and truth-aptness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a conditional particle transforms an independent proposition into a dependent clause, losing its own truth-value.

Core Idea
Adding 'if' to a proposition kills its independence.

In logic, a complete proposition can stand alone and be true or false. But when you attach a conditional particle like 'if' or 'when', that proposition becomes a mere part of a larger conditional statement. This lesson unpacks the exact mechanism from the sharh: the proposition must be stripped of its independent judgment to serve as a component. Understanding this is key to analysing conditional verses in the Quran and fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify an independent proposition
Recall a simple declarative sentence that can be uttered alone and judged true or false, e.g., 'the sun is rising' (طلوع الشمس). This has tamam and sihhat al-sukut.
2
Add a conditional particle
Prefix 'if' (إن أو إذا) to that proposition: 'if the sun is rising'. Observe that the sentence no longer feels complete; we expect a consequence.
3
Note the loss of self-sufficiency
The text says the proposition is removed from sihhat al-sukut and the possibility of truth/falsehood. It cannot stand alone because it contains a suspended judgment.
4
Understand the reason
The proposition, as long as it retains ḥukm (judgment) and idh'ān (assent), cannot be made a part of another proposition. Adding the conditional suspends the judgment, turning it into a mere antecedent or consequent.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
In the Quran, 'If you turn away, He will replace you with another people' (47:38). The clause 'you turn away' is not independently asserted; it is part of a conditional. Recognizing this prevents misreading it as a categorical statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This brief note (comment 23) explains the effect of inserting a conditional particle into a proposition. It refers back to a previous statement by the author (mugannif): 'When you insert upon it a particle of connection...' The gloss clarifies that the addition of the particle removes the proposition from its original state of being complete (tāmm) and independently capable of truth or falsehood (ṣiḥḥat al-sukūt, 'self-sufficiency'). The reason is that a proposition, as long as it contains a judgment (ḥukm) and assent (idh'ān), cannot be made a part of another proposition. By adding the conditional particle, the judgment is suspended or modified, turning the independent proposition into a mere constituent (e.g., antecedent or consequent) of a conditional. The example is cut off ('if you say 'the sun...'), but it likely continued: 'is shining' (an independent proposition) becomes 'if the sun is shining...' (a conditional part). This highlights that conditional particles transform propositions into components, stripping them of their independent truth-aptness. The student should note the distinction between an independent proposition (qadiyya mustaqilla) and a conditional part (juz' al-shartiyya).

The Controversy over Conditional Parts: Verbal vs Mental Propositions
Author discusses the controversy over whether removing the conditional operator alone renders the parts propositions, citing al-Mohaqqiq al-Sharif and others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and verbal propositions in evaluating whether removing the conditional operator turns the parts into independent propositions, and reconstruct al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif's objection.

Core Idea
Removing a barrier does not automatically restore judgment; a cause (muqtada) is needed for the parts of a conditional to become full propositions.

In this passage, the logician al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif challenges the view that simply deleting the conditional operator (like 'if') from a conditional statement turns the antecedent and consequent into independent propositions. He argues that a motivating factor must also be present. The discussion distinguishes between mental propositions (abstract judgments) and verbal propositions (uttered sentences). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly handling conditional statements in logic and Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Locate the first view (attributed to al-Musannif) that mere removal of the operator suffices, and the second view (al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif) that a motivating factor (muqtada) is also required. Notice that al-Sharif explicitly disagrees.
2
Extract al-Sharif's core argument
Al-Sharif uses the example 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' where both parts are false but the conditional is true. He argues that removing the operator does not bring back judgment because there is no muqtada. This shows that removal of a barrier (the operator) does not guarantee existence of the thing.
3
Analyze the counter-argument about barrier removal
One might object that the operator was preventing judgment, so its removal should restore it. Al-Sharif refutes this by stating that the removal of a barrier does not suffice; a positive motivating factor is needed. Check the reasoning: the example proves that even after removal, the parts are not true propositions.
4
Apply the distinction between mental and verbal propositions
The passage then offers a resolution: if the dispute concerns mental propositions (abstract logical content), then al-Sharif's objection is valid. If it concerns verbal propositions (the uttered sentence), the objection fails because the words still carry the judgment. Understand why this distinction matters.
5
Determine the status of the parts after removal
Based on the resolution, decide: verbally, the parts are propositions because the linguistic form suggests judgment. Mentally, they are not unless there is a muqtada. This aligns with classical logic teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۴۲) ينبغي أن يعلم انهما اى المقدم والتالى هل يصيران قضيتين بمجرد حذف الاداة ام لا؟ ظاهر عبارة المصنف في بعض كتبه هو الأول وخالفه المحقق الشريف فقال: ان مجرد حذف الاداة لا يكون في عود الحكم حتى يصيرا قضيتين بل لابد من وجود المقتضى ايضاً. وقال في حاشيته على الشرح المشهور للرسالة الشمسية ان من زعم انه اذا حذف الاداة فقد وجد الحكم، فقد اخطاء، فكيف ذلك في مثل قولك: إن كان زيد حماراً كان ناهقاً مع العلم بكذب الطرفين و صدق الشرطية ؟ لا يقال : الادوات كانت مانعة عن الحكم فاذا زالت عاد الحكم. لان زوال المانع لا يكفى فى وجود الشيء بل لابد من وجود المتقضى وزوال المانع لا يستلزمه كما في المثال المذكور، انتهى . وقد يقال: إن كان النزاع فى القضية المعقولة، فالاعتراض حق وان كان في القضية الملفوظة كما هو الظاهر فالاعتراض ساقط لظهور ان طرفي الشرطية بعد حذف الادوات موافقان في اللفظ المشتمل على الحكم قبل ذلك ، فصح القول بان طرفي الشرطية قضيتان بعد حذف الادوات انتهى فتأمل.
(42) It should be known: [as for] both of them, i.e., the antecedent and the consequent, do they become two propositions merely by removing the operator or not? The apparent meaning of the author's words in some of his books is the first view. But al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif disagreed, saying: mere removal of the operator does not effect the return of judgment so that they become two propositions; rather, a motivating factor (muqtada) must also exist. He said in his gloss on the famous commentary of the Shamsiyya: Whoever claims that if the operator is removed then judgment is found, has erred. How about [the example of] your statement: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' when you know both parts are false yet the conditional is true? It cannot be said: the operators were preventing judgment, so when they are removed the judgment returns. For the removal of the barrier alone does not suffice for the existence of the thing; rather, a motivating factor must exist, and removal of the barrier does not necessitate it, as in the mentioned example. End. And it may be said: If the dispute is about the mental proposition, then the objection is valid; but if it is about the verbal proposition, as is apparent, then the objection is invalid, because it is obvious that the two parts of the conditional after removal of the operators agree in wording with the judgment [they had] before that. So the statement that the two parts of the conditional are two propositions after removal of the operators is correct. End. So reflect.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle point in logic: when we have a conditional statement like 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray,' the parts 'Zayd is a donkey' and 'he brays' are not independent judgments; they are governed by the conditional operator. The question is: if we simply remove the operator, do those phrases become full propositions? The apparent view of the author (al-musannif) in some works is yes. However, al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif opposed this, arguing that removing the operator alone does not restore the judgment (hukm). There must be a motivating factor (muqtada) for the judgment to exist. He gives the example of a conditional with false parts but a true conditional: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray.' Both parts are false, but the conditional is true. If we remove the operator, we get 'Zayd is a donkey' and 'he brays' — but these are not asserted as true judgments; they are merely considered within the conditional. Therefore, mere removal of the barrier (the operator) does not suffice to return the judgment. One might object that the operator was preventing the judgment, so its removal should bring it back. But al-Sharif refutes this by stating that removal of a barrier does not guarantee existence; a positive motivating factor is needed. Then a possible resolution is presented: if the dispute concerns the mental proposition (al-qadiyya al-ma'qula), the objection is valid; but if it concerns the verbal proposition (al-qadiyya al-malfuza), as seems to be the case, then the objection fails, because the words after removal still contain the judgment in their linguistic form. Hence the statement that the parts become propositions after removal is considered correct. The commentator ends by urging reflection (ta'ammul).

Key Terms
أداة (ada): The conditional operator (like 'in' or 'law') that connects the antecedent and consequent.
قضية معقولة (qadiyya ma'qula): A mental proposition, an abstract logical content.
قضية ملفوظة (qadiyya malfuza): A verbal proposition, the actual uttered sentence.
حكم (hukm): Judgment; the act of affirming or denying a predicate of a subject.
مقتضى (muqtada): A motivating factor or requisite that necessitates the judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the parts are false.
B) Because there is no motivating factor (muqtada) for the judgment.
C) Because the conditional is false.
D) Because the operator cannot be removed.

Key Takeaway
The mere removal of the conditional operator does not restore independent judgment to the parts unless a motivating factor (muqtada) exists, and this applies to mental propositions but not necessarily to verbal ones.

Why Disjunctive Conditionals Lack Three Divisions
Author explains why the disjunctive conditional does not have the three later divisions present in the connected conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the disjunctive conditional (al-munfasila) does not have the three final divisions that apply to the connected conditional (al-muttasila), and distinguish natural from conventional assignment of antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
In disjunctives, antecedent and consequent are opposites by nature, so their roles are merely conventional.

We already know that conditionals are divided into connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The connected conditional has a natural priority: the antecedent is the implier (malzum) and the consequent is the implied (lazim). This natural distinction enables further subdivisions, such as whether the antecedent itself is a categorical or a conditional. But the disjunctive conditional is different. Here, both parts are opposites (mu'anid), and opposition is mutual. The assignment of one as antecedent and the other as consequent is purely conventional. This fundamental difference explains why the three later divisions that apply to muttasila do not apply to munfasila. Let us explore this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core distinction between muttasila and munfasila
In muttasila, the antecedent is naturally the implier (malzum) and the consequent is the implied (lazim). In munfasila, both parts are opposites (mu'anid), and opposition is symmetric.
This is the key to understanding why their properties differ.
2
Understand why the assignment in munfasila is conventional
Because opposition is mutual, each part has the same status relative to the other. There is no natural priority; we arbitrarily label one as antecedent and the other as consequent.
This contrasts with muttasila, where the antecedent must be the implier and cannot be the implied.
3
Apply to compound conditionals
In compound conditionals, muttasila shows a difference based on whether the antecedent is categorical or itself a conditional. Munfasila shows no such difference because the parts are symmetric.
This is why the three further subdivisions that apply to muttasila do not apply to munfasila.
4
Check the consequence regarding inversion (al-'aks)
Since the roles are conventional, inversion (swapping antecedent and consequent) in munfasila does not change the logical meaning, unlike in muttasila.
The passage mentions that inversion will be discussed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ان كانت اقسام المنفصلة سنة ولم يجر فيها الاقسام الثلاثة الاخيرة كما جرت في المتصلة لان مقدم المتصلة متميز عن تاليها بحسب الطبع والمفهوم فان مفهوم المقدم فيها الملزوم و مفهوم التالى اللازم ويحتمل ان يكون الشيء ملزوماً للآخر ولا يكون لازماً له، فالمقدم فى المتصلة متعين ان يكون مقدماً و التالي متعين ان يكون تالياً بخلاف المنفصلة فأن مفهوم التالى فيها المعاند ومفهوم المقدم المعاند والمعاند لابد ان يكون معانداً ايضاً لأن عناد احد الشيئين للاخر في قوة عناد الاخراياه فحال كل واحد من جزئيها عند الآخر حال واحد وانما عرض لاحدهما ان يكون مقدماً و الاخر ان يكون تالياً بمجرد وضع لا طبع، ففرق ما بين المتصلة المركبة من الحملية و المتصلة والمقدم فيها الحملية وبينها والمقدم فيها المتصلة بخلاف المنفصلة المركبة منها فلا فرق بين ما كان المقدم فيهما الحملية أو المتصلة وكذلك الحال في المركبة من الحملية و من المتصلة والمنفصلة ولذا لا يجري فيها العكس كما سيجيء انشاء الله تعالى.
Then [it is said]: The divisions of the disjunctive are six, but the three final divisions do not apply to it as they apply to the connected, because the antecedent of the connected is distinct from its consequent by nature and concept. For the concept of the antecedent in it is the implicated (malzum) and the concept of the consequent is the implication (lazim). And it is possible for something to be implicated for another without being its implication. So the antecedent in the connected is determinate to be antecedent and the consequent determinate to be consequent, contrary to the disjunctive. For the concept of the consequent in it is the opposite (mu'anid) and the concept of the antecedent is also the opposite. And the opposite must necessarily also be opposite, because the opposition of one of the two things to the other is as strong as the opposition of the other to it. So the state of each of its two parts relative to the other is one state, and being antecedent or consequent for one of them occurs merely by convention not by nature. Thus there is a difference between the connected [conditional] composed of a categorical and a connected [conditional] where the antecedent is categorical, and [the connected conditional] where the antecedent is connected, contrary to the disjunctive composed of them. So there is no difference whether the antecedent in them is categorical or connected. Similarly for what is composed of a categorical and from a connected and a disjunctive. Hence inversion does not apply to it, as will come later, God willing.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why the disjunctive conditional (al-munfasila) does not have the three final divisions that the connected conditional (al-muttasila) has. The author begins by stating that the divisions of the disjunctive are six, but the three later ones do not apply because in the connected conditional, the antecedent (muqaddam) is naturally distinct from the consequent (tali). The concept of antecedent is that which implies (malzum) and the consequent is that which is implied (lazim). Importantly, something can be an implier without being implied. Therefore, in the connected conditional, the antecedent is fixed as antecedent by nature, and the consequent as consequent. In contrast, in the disjunctive conditional, the consequent is the opposite (mu'anid) and the antecedent is also the opposite. Because opposition is mutual, each part has the same status relative to the other. The designation of one as antecedent and the other as consequent is merely conventional (wad'), not natural. Consequently, there is no difference in the disjunctive when the parts are themselves compound conditionals, unlike in the connected where it matters whether the antecedent is categorical or connected. Therefore, inversion (al-'aks) does not apply to the disjunctive, as will be explained later. This distinction is crucial for understanding the properties of different types of conditionals in logic.

The disjunctive conditional lacks the three later divisions because its parts are opposites by nature, making the assignment of antecedent and consequent conventional rather than natural.

How Embedding a Proposition Strips It of Completeness
Author explains that when a proposition is made part of a conditional, it loses its independent truth-value and completeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a proposition used as a part of a conditional loses its independent truth-value and identify the logical operation of stripping judgment.

Core Idea
A proposition embedded in a conditional loses its truth-value because it must be stripped of judgment to become a part.

A simple proposition like 'The sun is rising' can stand alone and be evaluated as true or false. But when you embed it into a conditional sentence, such as 'If the sun is rising, then it is daytime,' the embedded proposition no longer functions as an independent assertion. This lesson explores why this transformation strips the proposition of its completeness and truth-value, and how logicians and grammarians have analyzed it.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the independent proposition's features
An independent proposition (qadiyya) carries judgment (hukm) and assent (idh'an), allowing it to be true or false. It has 'sihhat al-sukut' – the ability to stand alone and be completed by silence.
This is the default state of a simple assertion like 'The sun is rising.'
2
Understand the embedding process
When a connector like 'if' (in or law) is added, the proposition becomes a part of a conditional compound. For example, 'The sun is rising' becomes the antecedent of 'If the sun is rising, then it is daytime.'
The embedding is not just grammatical; it changes the logical status.
3
Identify the stripping requirement
To be a component, the original proposition must be stripped of its judgment and assent. Otherwise, it would retain its independent assertive force and could not be subordinated within the conditional's structure.
This stripping is necessary because a condition cannot be simultaneously asserted as an independent fact.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objection arises: in 'Zayd, his father stood' (Zaydun qama abuhu), the clause 'his father stood' seems to retain its judgment while serving as a predicate. The commentator responds that such clauses are treated grammatically as a single word (mufrad) or are reinterpreted as a single expression.
This shows that the stripping principle is consistent when viewed through the lens of logical composition.
5
Conclude the logical principle
Any proposition used as a part of another compound loses its independent completeness and truth-value. It becomes a mere component, stripped of its assertoric force.
This principle is fundamental for understanding conditional statements in logic and Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(23) His saying: 'When the operator of connection is inserted into it...' [means]: The proposition only exits from being capable of independent utterance and from the possibility of truth and falsity by the addition of the operators, and it does not remain as it was initially of completeness and capability of independent utterance. Because if a proposition is not stripped of judgment and assent, it cannot be made a part of another proposition. For if you say, for example, 'The sun is rising' and you place the relation between its two terms, it is not conceivable to connect it with a specific connection existing between the condition and the result. So it is not replied that the proposition may be made connected to a subject and predicated of it while remaining as it was of judgment and assent and without being stripped of them, as in our saying: 'Zayd, his father stood.' However, we can say: the proposition, come, remains also as it was, necessarily because it is in the status of a single word (mufrad) as stated by more than one of the grammarians. Or we say: the meaning here is 'Zayd's father stood'. So understand and analogize, and do not be deficient in another matter, by making it as subject of predication or predicate. When you want to make it a part of another [proposition], you must strip it of judgment. When it is stripped of judgment, it exits from completeness and the possibility of truth and falsity. So reflect. Mirza Muhammad Ali.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's statement: 'When the operator of connection is inserted into it...' The commentator explains that when a proposition is made part of a conditional, it loses its independent assertoric force (sihhat al-sukut) and its truth/falsity value. This is because, to be a component of another proposition, the original proposition must be stripped of its judgment (hukm) and assent (idh'an). For example, if you take the simple proposition 'The sun is rising' and want to use it as a part of a conditional like 'If the sun is rising, then it is day,' you cannot directly connect it as an independent claim. The objection might be raised that in some grammatical constructions, like 'Zayd, his father stood' (Zaydun qama abuhu), the clause 'his father stood' is used as a predicate while retaining its judgment. The commentator responds that in such cases, the clause is treated like a single word (mufrad) grammatically. Alternatively, the meaning is something like 'Zayd's father stood' which is a single expression. Therefore, whenever you want to embed a proposition into another, you must strip it of its judgment. Once stripped, it loses completeness and truth-value. This is a fundamental principle in logic about the nature of propositions and their use in compound statements.

Contradiction Between Singular Terms: A Critical Refinement
Author argues that contradiction applies to singular terms, refuting those who restrict it to propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute contradiction from propositional contradiction and identify evidence for the former in singular terms.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not limited to propositions; singular terms like 'existent' and 'non-existent' can be contradictory absolutely.

In this passage, the author tackles a debated issue in logic: does contradiction occur only between propositions, or can it also apply to singular terms (mufradāt)? The author first explains how a proposition can be treated as a singular when it becomes part of another, then critiques the view that restricts contradiction to propositions. By distinguishing absolute contradiction from propositional contradiction, the author defends the position that singulars can be contradictory, using the definition of ʿaks al-naqīḍ as key evidence. This lesson will guide you through the argument and help you apply this distinction in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Recognize that some logicians restrict contradiction to propositions only, while others (including the author) extend it to singular terms. The dispute hinges on whether negation and affirmation are required for contradiction.
The author says the dispute is verbal, depending on the sense of 'contradiction' used.
2
Understand the objection against singulars
The objector argues: if contradiction occurs between singulars, either judgment (truth/falsity) is considered or not. If considered, they are no longer singular; if not, there is no negation/affirmation, which seems essential to contradiction.
The author rejects this by noting that negation/affirmation are only required for propositional contradiction, not absolute contradiction.
3
Analyze the author's distinction
The author introduces 'absolute contradiction' (al-tanāquḍ al-muṭlaq) which does not require truth/falsity or explicit negation/affirmation. This applies to singulars like 'existent' and 'non-existent'.
Absolute contradiction simply means two terms cannot be true together or false together, but singulars don't have truth values; it's about conceptual incompatibility.
4
Examine the evidence from ʿaks al-naqīḍ
The definition of conversion by contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) involves 'replacing the two contradictories of the two terms' of a proposition. Since the terms of a proposition are singulars (even when originally propositions, they become like singulars), this shows that singulars have contradictories, hence absolute contradiction applies.
The author uses this as a strong indirect proof from a standard logical operation.
5
Apply to concrete examples
Consider pairs like 'this' and 'not this', 'existent' and 'non-existent'. These are singular terms that are absolute contradictories. In Islamic theology, 'eternal' and 'non-eternal' (al-qadīm wa al-ḥādith) are another example.
These examples show that the concept of contradiction is broader than propositional logic.

In the table of divisions of the conditional proposition, for example, and when the relation is placed between its two sides, its connector is not conceived—i.e., a specific connector existing between the condition and the consequent. So it is not objected that the proposition may be made connected to the subject and judged upon it while remaining as it was in terms of judgment and assent, without being stripped of them, as in our statement: 'Zaid, his father stood up,' even though we can say: the proposition did not remain as it was, necessarily because it is like a singular term, as stated by more than one of the grammarians. Marginal notes on 'Contradiction' (1) An indication of the weakness of the support for this statement. The evidence for that is: if there is contradiction between singulars, then either judgment is considered with them or not. If it is considered, they are not singular; if not, then negation and affirmation do not occur, even though they are considered in the concept of contradiction. This is open to objection, necessarily because negation and affirmation are only considered in the contradiction of two propositions, not absolutely. This is it. The verification is that the dispute is verbal. For whoever says it does not occur in singulars intends by it the contradiction in which negation and affirmation are considered; and whoever says it occurs in them intends only absolute contradiction. However it may be, the truth is that absolute contradiction occurs in singulars as well. This is indicated by the author's definition of conversion by contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) that will come: 'Replacement of the two contradictories of the two terms while preserving truth and quality, or making the contradictory of the second first while opposing the quality.' For the terms of a proposition are not a proposition. As for the conditional, it is apparent, for its terms, though originally propositions, have exited by the addition of the conditional and disjunctive particle from completeness, the absence of which entails the absence of the proposition, as is not hidden from the contemplator. As for the categorical, its first term is only singular, which is apparent. As for its other term, it may be a sentence and a proposition, but it is, as the grammarians have stated, in the status of a singular, just as is the status of every sentence and proposition that has no place in iʿrāb, for iʿrāb occurs only in the noun which is a part of the singular word. Even if it is granted, it suffices for indication that the first term in the categorical is singular, let alone that the other term in it is also singular in some cases, let alone it being more or equal inevitably. So reflect, especially since they take the contradictory of the second term by agreement, and of the first also according to the method of the ancients. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (Shaykh Muhammad al-Karamī said: His statement: 'It does not occur between singulars according to what was said' means: it does not occur such that benefit is obtained from it. Otherwise, there is no doubt that 'This is its contradictory, not this' and 'existent', 'non-existent', and analogize to that other than that.) (al-Taqrīb p. 55) (2) His statement: 'But because the discussion is about the contradiction of propositions': For it is said: This is based on what has been established by them from...
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the topic of contradiction (tanāquḍ). It addresses a debated issue: does contradiction occur between singular terms (mufradāt) or only between propositions (qaḍāyā)? The author first explains how a proposition can be treated as a singular term when it becomes part of another proposition—for example, in 'Zaid: his father stood up,' the phrase 'his father stood up' is originally a proposition but is used here as a predicate, losing its truth-value status and becoming like a singular. This sets the stage for the discussion.

The main argument begins with a critique of those who deny contradiction between singulars. They argue that if contradiction were to occur between singulars, either judgment (ḥukm) is considered or not. If judgment is considered, then the terms are no longer singulars because they carry truth/falsity. If not, then affirmation and negation (components of contradiction) do not occur. The author rejects this, noting that affirmation and negation are only required for the contradiction of propositions, not for absolute contradiction.

The author then clarifies that the dispute is verbal: those who deny it use contradiction in a specific sense requiring affirmation and negation, while those who affirm it use contradiction in the absolute sense. The truth, according to the author, is that absolute contradiction does apply to singulars. Evidence comes from the definition of ʿaks al-naqīḍ (conversion by contraposition), which involves 'replacing the two contradictories of the two terms.' Since 'the two terms' of a proposition are not themselves propositions—they are singular (in categoricals, the subject is always singular, and the predicate, even if a sentence, is grammatically like a singular; in conditionals, the parts are originally propositions but become like singulars due to connecting particles)—the fact that their contradictories are taken in the definition shows that contradiction exists between singulars.

The marginal note from Shaykh Muhammad al-Karamī provides concrete examples: 'This is its contradictory, not this' (a singular term like 'this' and 'not this') and 'existent' with 'non-existent' are clear cases of absolute contradiction between singulars. Thus, the passage defends the view that contradiction is not limited to propositions but also applies to singular terms in a meaningful sense.

عكس النقيض

D) The statement that 'this is its contradictory, not this' is a hypothetical example.

Key Takeaway
Absolute contradiction applies to singular terms, as evidenced by the definition of ʿaks al-naqīḍ, which requires taking contradictories of singular terms.

Quantity Condition for Contradiction: Objection and Answer
Addresses objection that difference in quantity is not always required; clarifies scope of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why difference in quantity is required for contradiction only when both propositions are quantified, and identify the role of subject unity in the objection.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires quantity difference only for quantified propositions, not for indefinite or singular ones.

This passage presents a common objection to the rule that two contradictory quantified propositions must differ in quantity. The objection claims that the real issue is unity of subject, not quantity. The commentator clarifies the scope of the rule: the quantity condition applies only to quantified propositions, while contradictions among indefinite or singular propositions do not require such a condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the established rule
The passage begins by stating that if two propositions are quantified (universal or particular), they must differ in quantity to be contradictory; otherwise, two particulars can both be true and two universals both false.
This rule is about the four types of quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
2
Locate the objection's core claim
The objector argues that the truth of two particular propositions in the example is not due to lack of quantity difference but due to lack of unity of subject. The subject's 'some' in one proposition is not the same 'some' in the other.
The objection challenges the necessity of quantity difference by pointing to an alternative explanation.
3
Follow the commentator's answer
The answer explains that judgments are based on the concept of the proposition, not the specific individuals. The concept of 'some S is P' and 'some S is not P' are not contradictory regardless of which individuals are intended. The specification of the subject is external to the concept.
This preserves the rule by separating logical form from material interpretation.
4
Recognize the scope limitation
The commentator notes that the author's phrase 'if they are quantified' indicates that the quantity condition is not absolute. Contradiction can occur in indefinite and singular propositions without differing in quantity.
Thus the rule applies only to quantified propositions, not to all propositions.

(میرزا محمد علی)
(4) i.e., when it is established that two particulars and likewise two universals can both be true together, it is known that if two propositions are quantified, they must differ in quantity, otherwise they would not be contradictory due to the simultaneous truth of the two particulars and the two universals. For if it is said: the truth of the two particulars in the mentioned example is not due to lack of difference in quantity but due to lack of unity of subject, for the some that is judged with humanity is not the some that is judged with non-humanity, and it will come that if the subject is not united they are not contradictory, so the necessity of difference in quantity is not proven absolutely. It is answered: the consideration in all judgments is only of the concept of the proposition, and when the concept of the two particulars is considered, which is affirmation of some individuals and negation of some individuals, they are not contradictory. As for the specification of the subject, it is external to the concept. How [could it be otherwise]? The essence of the subject in the universal is all individuals, and in the particular it is some of them, while contradiction exists between them. Then his saying 'if they are quantified' is an indication that contradiction is not exclusive to the four quantified [propositions] as might be understood from the apparent meaning of the author's statement, but rather as it occurs in them, it occurs in indefinite and singular propositions. So the author's condition of difference in quantity is not absolute but restricted to the case of two quantified [propositions]. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. It first establishes that two particular propositions (e.g., 'Some human is an animal' and 'Some human is not an animal') can both be true, and similarly two universal propositions can both be false. Therefore, if two propositions are quantified (i.e., have universal or particular quantifiers), they must differ in quantity to be contradictory; otherwise they would not be contradictory. An objection is raised: the truth of the two particulars in the example is not due to lack of quantity difference but due to lack of unity of subject (the 'some' in one is not the same 'some' as in the other). The answer is that the judgment is made on the basis of the concept of the proposition, not the specific individuals. The concept of 'some human is animal' and 'some human is not animal' are not contradictory, while the specification of the subject is external. Then the commentator clarifies that the author's phrase 'if they are quantified' indicates that contradiction is not limited to the four quantified propositions but also applies to indefinite (muhmala) and singular (shakhsiyya) propositions. Hence the condition of difference in quantity is restricted to the case of quantified propositions.

Conditions for Contradiction in Singular and Quantified Propositions
Examples and conditions for contradictory propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conditions for contradiction in singular vs quantified propositions, and apply them to judge whether two propositions are genuinely contradictory.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires difference in quality, modality, and, for quantified propositions, quantity.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the conditions for two propositions to be contradictory. Using examples and commentary, we learn that the conditions differ based on whether the propositions are singular or quantified. Understanding these precise conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic disciplines like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, where contradiction determines the validity of a proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
Recognize whether the two propositions are singular (مخصوصة) or quantified (محصورة). Singular propositions have a single subject (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'), while quantified propositions have universal or particular quantity (e.g., 'Every human is mortal').
Check if the subject is a single term or quantified by 'every', 'some', 'no', etc.
2
Check quality and modality for singular propositions
If both propositions are singular, ensure they differ in quality (affirmative vs negative) and in modality (necessity, possibility, actuality, etc.). For example, 'Zayd is standing' (affirmative, actual) and 'Zayd is not standing' (negative, actual) are contradictory because they differ in quality and have the same modality.
If they agree in quality or modality, they are not contradictory.
3
Add quantity check for quantified propositions
If both propositions are quantified, they must also differ in quantity (universal vs particular). For example, 'Every human is mortal' (universal affirmative) and 'Some human is not mortal' (particular negative) differ in quality and quantity. But 'Every human is mortal' and 'Some human is mortal' are both affirmative, so they are not contradictory.
Quantity is an additional condition only when both propositions are quantified.
4
Test with the given examples
Examine the examples provided: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is an animal' are both affirmative (same quality) but differ in quantity, so they agree in truth. Similarly, two negatives like 'No human is a stone' and 'Not some human is a stone' agree. This shows that quality must differ for contradiction.
Use these to verify the rule: difference in quality is necessary but not sufficient for quantified propositions.
5
Apply to new Islamic science cases
Given a pair of propositions from Kalam or Usul al-Fiqh, determine if they are contradictory. For example, 'The world is created' (singular, affirmative) and 'The world is not created' (singular, negative) are contradictory. But 'Every atom is created' and 'Some atom is created' are not, because they agree in quality.
Always check quality, modality, and quantity as per the conditions.

And as for the agreement of two negatives in them, it is like our saying 'Nothing of human is a stone' and 'Not some human is a stone', and our saying 'Nothing of human is an animal' and 'Some human is not an animal'. (6) i.e., the two contradictory propositions, whether they are singular or quantified: if they are singular, they must differ in quality and modality; if they are quantified, they must differ in these and in quantity. (Abd al-Rahim)
(7) The reason he delayed mentioning difference in quantity after difference in quality, although it is prior in the text, is because it is not a condition in every place but is specific to the case of their being quantified, unlike difference in quality which has been established earlier to the contrary. So it would have been better not to mention it here at all, but he mentioned it as a digression and as an indication that it is not a permanent condition but only in some states and times. (8) i.e., the two propositions absolutely, whether quantified or not. (Muhammad Ali)

Commentary
This passage provides illustrative examples and summarizes the conditions for contradiction. First, examples show that two affirmative propositions can agree in truth (both true or both false) when one is universal and the other particular; similarly two negatives can agree. This demonstrates that difference in quality (affirmative vs. negative) is necessary for contradiction, but not sufficient if quantity differs. The commentator then states the general conditions: for singular propositions, only difference in quality and modality is required; for quantified propositions, difference in quantity is also required. A note explains why the author mentioned quantity after quality: quantity is not a universal condition but specific to quantified propositions, whereas quality is always required. The commentator remarks that the author might have omitted quantity altogether but mentioned it as a digression to indicate its conditional nature. Finally, a gloss clarifies that 'the two propositions' in the condition refers to them absolutely, encompassing both quantified and non-quantified.

Refining the Condition of Modality Difference for Contradiction
Resolves an objection about modality difference condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when the condition of modality difference applies to contradictory propositions, and apply this refinement to resolve apparent conflicts in modal logic.

Core Idea
Modality difference is required only when both contradictories are modal.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential inconsistency: the rule that contradictory propositions must differ in modality seems to conflict with the later claim that the contradictory of a perpetual absolute proposition is the absolute general, which is not considered modal. The resolution clarifies that the condition is not absolute but applies only when both propositions carry a modality. This refinement is crucial for correctly handling modal contradictory pairs and avoiding misinterpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition under attack
Locate the condition that 'they must differ in modality' (يجب اختلافهما في الجهة). Note that this is a standard rule for contradictory modal propositions.
The condition appears in the first line of the Arabic.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that this condition contradicts the later statement that the contradictory of the perpetual absolute (دائمة مطلقة) is the absolute general (مطلقة عامة). The absolute general is usually not considered a modal proposition, so the pair would not differ in modality.
The perpetual absolute is modal (it includes a kind of perpetuity), while the absolute general is non-modal according to some.
3
Follow the resolution layers
The commentator gives three points: (1) According to the author's own view, actuality (فعلية) is also a modality, so there can be a difference; (2) The condition does not require both to be modal—it suffices that one has a modality; (3) The condition is only required when both propositions are modal, not universally.
The key refinement is that the condition is restricted: it applies only when both are modal.
4
Apply the refined condition
Test a given pair of propositions: if both are modal (e.g., necessary and possible), then modality difference is required; if one is modal and the other non-modal (e.g., perpetual absolute vs. absolute general), the condition does not apply and contradiction can still hold.
This prevents false conflicts like the one raised in the objection.

(۹) قوله: «يجب اختلافهما في الجهة ايضاً »: فان قيل : ان هذا ينافي ماسيأتي من ان نقيض الدائمة المطلقة المطلقة العامة لما سبق من ان المطلقة العامة ليست من الموجهات بل انما ذكروها في عدادها على التجوز.
قلنا: قد تقدم ايضاً ان هذا على مذهب غير المصنف و اما على مذهبه فالفعلية ايضاً جهة للقضية و ايضاً الاختلاف في الجهة لا يقتضى ان يكون كل واحدة من المتناقضتين مشتملة على جهة بل يكفى اشتمال احديهما للجهة والاخرى غير مشتملة لجهة اصلاً مع انا نقول : ان الاختلاف في الجهة ليس شرط التناقض مطلقا حتى يرد ما ذكر بل اذا كان كل واحدة من القضيتين موجهة كما اشار اليه المحشى فان قلنا بان المطلقة العامة داخلة فى الموجهات والفعلية داخلة في الجهات على ما هو الحق فلانسلم ان ليس بينها وبين الدائمة المطلقة اختلاف في الجهة والا فلا نسلم ان الاختلاف في الجهة شرط هنا اذ القدر المسلم كونه شرطاً اذا كانت كلتا القضيتين موجهتين لا اذا كانت واحدة منها موجهة دون الأخرى فتأمل (میرزا محمد علی)
(9) His saying 'They must also differ in modality': For if it is said: This contradicts what will come that the contradictory of the perpetual absolute is the absolute general, because the absolute general is not among the modal propositions but they mention it among them figuratively.
We say: It has also preceded that this is according to the view of other than the author, but according to his view actuality is also a modality for the proposition. Also, difference in modality does not require that each of the two contradictories include a modality; rather it suffices that one of them includes a modality and the other includes no modality at all. Moreover, we say: difference in modality is not a condition for contradiction absolutely such that what was mentioned would be problematic, but rather when each of the two propositions is modal as the commentator indicated. For if we say that the absolute general is included among modals and actuality is included among modalities as is the truth, then we do not accept that there is no difference in modality between it and the perpetual absolute. Otherwise we do not accept that difference in modality is a condition here, because what is conceded is that it is a condition when both propositions are modal, not when one is modal and the other is not. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential inconsistency: the condition that contradictory propositions must differ in modality seems to conflict with a later claim that the contradictory of a perpetual absolute proposition (dāʾima muṭlaqa) is the absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma), which is not considered a modal proposition. The answer is multi-layered: first, according to the author's own position, actuality (fiʿliyya) is also a modality, so there can be a difference. Second, the condition does not require that both propositions be modal; it suffices that one has a modality and the other does not. Third, the condition of modality difference applies only when both propositions are modal, not universally. Thus, when one is modal and the other non-modal, the condition is not required. The commentator advises careful reflection.

The Eight Unities Required for Contradiction
Author enumerates the eight conditions (unities) for contradiction as given by the ancients.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the eight unities is violated in a given pair of propositions and determine whether they can be contradictory.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not just opposite truth values; it requires unity across eight dimensions.

The ancient logicians recognized that for two propositions to truly contradict one another, they must be identical in several respects. If any of these eight unities is missing, the propositions could both be true or both false, and thus no genuine contradiction occurs. This lesson walks through each unity with clear examples so you can apply them in your own reasoning about Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement of unity of subject
Both propositions must refer to exactly the same subject (e.g., 'every human' vs. 'some stone' differ in subject, so they can both be true).
The subject is the thing about which something is said.
2
Understand the requirement of unity of predicate
The predicate must be the same in both propositions (e.g., 'animal' vs. 'stone' differ, allowing both to be true).
The predicate is what is affirmed or denied of the subject.
3
Understand the requirement of unity of place
If the place is different, the propositions can both be true (e.g., 'pearl in the sea' vs. 'pearl in a box').
Place refers to the location or context referred to.
4
Understand the remaining five unities: condition, relation, whole/part, potentiality/actuality, time
Each unity specifies a dimension that must be identical: e.g., condition (white vs. black body), relation (father of own son vs. father of others' sons), whole vs. part (all men are private parts vs. some men are not), potential vs. actual (wine intoxicating potentially vs. actually), time (strong in youth vs. weak in old age).
These examples show how context can avoid contradiction.
5
Apply the check to a new pair of propositions
Given two propositions, examine each unity: are the subjects identical? Predicates? Place? Condition? Relation? Whole/part? Potentiality/actuality? Time? If all eight are identical, then they can be contradictory; otherwise, they are not.
Practice with examples from fiqh or kalam to internalize the method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا ان التناقض لا يتحقق ولا يتصور الا بعد تحقق هذه الامور الثمانية المذكورة في البيتين: الاول وحدة الموضوع فانه لو اختلف الموضوع فى القضيتين لم يتناقضا الجواز ان تصدقا معاً نحو: كل انسان حيوان وليس بعض الحجر بحيوان وان تكذبا كعكس ذلك . الثانى وحدة المحمول، اذ لا تتناقضان عند اختلافه الجواز صدقهما معاً نحو كل انسان حیوان و بعض الانسان ليس بحجر و كذبها معاً كعكس ذلك . الثالث: وحدة المكان اذلو اختلف المكان لم تتناقضا لانها قد تصدقان معاً نحو: كل لؤلؤ محاط بالماء فى البحر و ليس بعض اللؤلؤ محاطاً بالماء فى الصندوق و قد تكذبان معاً كعكس الرابع: وحدة الشرط : اذ لا تناقض عند اختلافه تصدقهما في قولنا كل جسم مفرق للبصر اى بشرط كونه ابيض وبعض الجسم ليس بمفرق:ای بشرط كونه اسود فكذبهما معاً في عكس ذلك. الخامس: وحدة الاضافة اذ لو لم تتحد الاضافة لم تتناقضا لصدقهما معاً كقولنا: كل والد ما معا تقولنا كل والد اب اى بالنسبة الى ابنه وبعض الوالد ليس بأب أى بالنسبة الى ابناء الغير و كذبهما في عكس ذلك. السادس: وحدة الكل والجزء لعدم التناقض عند اختلاف الكل والجزء الصدقهما معاً كقولنا : كل الرجال عورة اى بعضه، و بعض الرجال ليس بصورة اى كله وكذبهما معاً في عكس ذلك. السابع وحدة القوة و الفعل اذلو اختلفا فيهما لم تتناقضا لجواز ان تصدقا معاً كقولنا كل خمر مسكر فى الدن اى بالقوة وليس بعضه بمسكر فيه اى بالفعل و كذبها معاً في عكس ذلك . الثامن: وحدة الزمان لعدم التناقض عند اختلافه لانها قد تصدقان معاً كقولنا كل انسان قوى اى فى زمن الشباب وبعض الانسان ليس بقوى اى فى زمن الشيخوخة وقد تكذبان معاً كعكس ذلك.
They said: contradiction is neither realized nor conceivable except after the realization of these eight matters mentioned in two verses: First: unity of subject, for if the subject differs in the two propositions, they do not contradict, because it is possible that they be both true, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Not some stone is an animal', and both false similarly. Second: unity of predicate, for they do not contradict when it differs, because they can both be true together, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is not a stone', and both false similarly. Third: unity of place, for if the place differs, they do not contradict because they can both be true, e.g., 'Every pearl is surrounded by water in the sea' and 'Not some pearl is surrounded by water in a box', and both false similarly. Fourth: unity of condition, for there is no contradiction when it differs, as in our saying 'Every body is light-dispersing' i.e., on condition of being white, and 'Some body is not light-dispersing' i.e., on condition of being black, and both false similarly. Fifth: unity of relation, for if the relation is not united, they do not contradict, because they can both be true, e.g., 'Every parent' (?) ... 'Every parent is a father' i.e., in relation to his son, and 'Some parent is not a father' i.e., in relation to others' sons, and both false similarly. Sixth: unity of whole and part, for there is no contradiction when whole and part differ, as in our saying 'All men are private parts' i.e., some of them, and 'Some men are not private parts' i.e., all of them, and both false similarly. Seventh: unity of potentiality and actuality, for if they differ in them, they do not contradict because they can both be true, e.g., 'Every wine is intoxicating in the jar' i.e., potentially, and 'Some wine is not intoxicating in it' i.e., actually, and both false similarly. Eighth: unity of time, for there is no contradiction when it differs, because they can both be true, e.g., 'Every human is strong' i.e., in youth, and 'Some human is not strong' i.e., in old age, and both false similarly.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage enumerates the eight unities (al-waḥdāt al-thamāniya) that the ancient logicians established as necessary conditions for contradiction. Each unity is explained with an example showing that if that aspect differs, the two propositions can both be true or both false, thus avoiding contradiction. The eight unities are: (1) unity of subject, (2) unity of predicate, (3) unity of place, (4) unity of condition, (5) unity of relation, (6) unity of whole and part, (7) unity of potentiality and actuality, (8) unity of time. The examples are from everyday objects and natural phenomena, demonstrating practical application. This is a core definitional passage listing the safeguards against false contradiction.

وحدة الشرط
وحدة الاضافة
وحدة الكل والجزء
وحدة القوة والفعل
وحدة الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the eight unities required for contradiction?
A) Unity of subject
B) Unity of predicate
C) Unity of mode (wajh)
D) Unity of time

Key Takeaway
Two propositions are truly contradictory only if they are identical in subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, whole/part, potentiality/actuality, and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following two propositions from fiqh: 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden' and 'Some intoxicating drink is not forbidden.' Identify which of the eight unities would have to differ for these to be both true (e.g., unity of condition: if the first refers to wine made from grapes and the second to nabidh under a different condition).
?
Take a pair of propositions from the Ash'ari position on kasb: 'All human actions are created by Allah' and 'Some human actions are not created by Allah.' Determine whether they satisfy the unity of subject and unity of relation. If not, why is there no contradiction?

Propositions

10 min
Contradictory of the General Conditioned Proposition
Author states the contradictory of the general conditioned proposition is the temporal possible and defends it against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory of a general conditioned proposition and distinguish between the two interpretations (mā dāma al-waṣf vs. bi-sharṭ al-waṣf) that affect its logical behavior.

Core Idea
The contradictory of the general conditioned hangs on 'as long as' vs. 'on condition of'.

In modal logic, propositions can be conditioned on a description of the subject. The general conditioned proposition can be read in two subtly different ways: one where the judgment holds 'as long as' the description exists, and another where the description is a necessary condition for the judgment. The correct contradictory differs based on this reading, and confusing them leads to error. This lesson unpacks the author's doctrine and the objection it faces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
The general conditioned proposition can be interpreted as 'as long as the description exists' (mā dāma al-waṣf) or 'on condition of the description' (bi-sharṭ al-waṣf). These differ in whether the description is merely a temporal marker or a causal condition for the predicate.
The author (al-musannif) explicitly adopts the 'mā dāma' interpretation.
2
State the claimed contradictory
The author claims that the contradictory of the general conditioned is the temporal possible (al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina): a proposition asserting that the subject is possibly described by the predicate at the time the description holds. This mirrors how the general possible contradicts the absolute necessary.
This only works if the general conditioned is taken as 'mā dāma al-waṣf'.
3
Examine the objection
An objector argues that under the 'bi-sharṭ al-waṣf' reading, both the general conditioned and the temporal possible can be false together, violating the law of non-contradiction. Example: 'Every writer is necessarily an animal on condition of writing' and its supposed contradictory 'Not some writer is possibly an animal while writing' – both false since writing is irrelevant to animality.
The objector's example relies on the 'bi-sharṭ al-waṣf' interpretation.
4
Resolve with the author's interpretation
The author responds that his definition uses 'mā dāma al-waṣf', so the objection's example is irrelevant: under 'mā dāma', the first proposition is true (since every writer is necessarily an animal at all times he is writing). The objection only applies to those who mix the two interpretations.
Thus the contradictory relationship is preserved under the correct interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فافهم. (میرزا محمد علی) حقيقة. (محمد على)
Understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) Reality. (17) That is, the contradictory of the general conditioned proposition is truly the temporal possible, just as the general possible is the contradictory of the absolute necessary. For if it is said: This is only valid if the general conditioned proposition is interpreted as that which judges with necessity of the relation as long as the description (madaam al-wasf), i.e., in all times of its being described by the attributive description. But if it is interpreted as that which judges with necessity of the relation on condition of the description (bi-shart al-wasf), i.e., the description has an entry in the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, then it is not valid because they would both be false in a matter where the description of the subject has no entry, like our saying 'Every writer is necessarily an animal on condition of the description' and 'Not some writer is possibly an animal while he is a writer'; both are false. The latter is obvious, the former because writing has no entry in the affirmation of animality for the essence of the subject, and their both being false is evidence of non-contradiction as previously mentioned. We say: It has preceded at the beginning of the discussion of modalities that the author's intent is only that the general conditioned proposition is that which judges with necessity of the relation as long as the description, as is explicit in his expression. So he judges here that the contradictory of the general conditioned proposition is the temporal possible based on his interpretation of the general conditioned proposition. Thus we do not accept that they both are false in the mentioned example, for the first proposition is true according to this interpretation, as is apparent. Yes, this objection applies to one who combines this with taking the general conditioned proposition on condition of the description. Understand. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the general conditioned proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma). The author asserts that its true contradictory is the temporal possible (al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina), analogous to how the general possible contradicts the absolute necessary. An objection is raised: this works only if the general conditioned is interpreted as 'as long as the description' (mā dāma al-waṣf). If interpreted as 'on condition of the description' (bi-sharṭ al-waṣf), then both could be false, as in the example of a writer being necessarily an animal on condition of writing versus not possibly an animal while writing – both are false because writing is irrelevant to animality. The answer clarifies that the author's definition uses the 'as long as' interpretation, so the example is not problematic; the first proposition is true under that reading. The objection only applies to those who mix both interpretations. Thus the lesson guards against confusing the two interpretations of the general conditioned proposition.

Propositions

The Contradictory of the General Customary Proposition
Author defines the contradictory of the general customary proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of the general customary proposition ('urfiyya 'āmma) and explain why it is the absolute temporal (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa).

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'always' is 'at some time'.

In modal logic, we need precise contradictories for arguments. The general customary proposition judges a relation as perpetual as long as the subject exists. Its negation is not a simple 'not perpetual', so logicians defined a technical contradictory: the absolute temporal. This lesson unpacks that relationship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general customary proposition
The 'urfiyya 'āmma means a judgment of perpetuity of a relation as long as the subject exists in reality. For example, 'Every human is breathing' (as long as they exist).
It is like a perpetual but conditioned on existence.
2
Recognize its negation
The contradictory must deny that perpetuity. The negation is 'not perpetual' (salb al-dawām). However, this negation lacks a determinate meaning in common propositions.
3
Interpret the negation as absolute temporal
Logicians interpret 'not perpetual' as 'true at some time' – an absolute temporal (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa). Thus the contradictory of the general customary is the absolute temporal.
This makes the contradictory a well-defined modal proposition.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'Every fasting person is hungry as long as they fast' (general customary). Its contradictory is 'At some time, a fasting person is not hungry' (absolute temporal).
This is used in fiqh for temporal exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ميرزا محمد علی) (۱۹) اى النقيض الصريح لدوام النسبة هو سلب الدوام ولم يكن لسلب الدوام ايضاً مفهوم محصل من القضاياء المتعارفة . فقال :المنطقيون ان سلب الدوام اشارة الى حينية مطلقة فعلى هذا يكون نقيض العرفية العامة الحينية المطلقة. (شرح الشمسية) (۲۰) ای ببیان نقیض الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتين. وفيه انه لو لم يتعلق ببيان نقيضهما غرض لماعدهما المصنف من القضاياء المعتبرة ولم يبين عكسهما مع انه صرح بان الوقتيتين تنعكسان مطلقة عامة ونقيض المركبة المفهوم المردد بين نقيضى جزئيها فلابد من ذكر نقيضها حتى يتم دليل الخلف. والنكتة فى عدم ذكر هما هى انه لما ذكر ان نقيض الضرورة الذاتية هو الامكان الذاتي ونقيض الضرورة الوصفية الامكان الوصفى فيعلم منه ان نقيضى الضرورية الوقتية والضرورية المنتشرة الامكان في وقت معين والامكان في وقت ما فيكون نقيضى الوقتيتين المطلقتين ، الممكنة الوقتية و الممكنة المنتشرة. (شيخ عبد الرحيم) (۲۱) و لذا لم يذكر هما الكاتبى فى الرسالة فى مبحث الموجهات ايضاً و انما ذكر هما المصنف هنا مقدمة لذكر الوقتية و المنتشرة فانهما كما تقدم هما الوقتية و المنتشرة المطلقتان المقيدتان باللادوام الذاتي و اما في مبحث العكس المستوى فانما ذكر الوقتيتين وهما الوقتية والمنتشرة لا الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتان كما يظهر من بعض المحققين من المحشين ميرزا محمد علی) (۲۲) قوله: «فتأمل»: كانه اشارة الى انه كان ينبغي ان يذكر نقيضيها كما ذكر عينيها ... (میرزا محمد علی)
(18) That is, it is not truly the contradictory of the general customary proposition, just as the absolute general is not truly the contradictory of the perpetual. Then it is not hidden that the author did not address in the chapter on modalities the temporal possible and absolute, while mentioning them in the chapter on contradiction, as a hint that they are not among the well-known propositions unlike the remaining simple ones. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (19) That is, the explicit contradictory of the perpetuity of the relation is the negation of perpetuity, and the negation of perpetuity also has no determinate concept from the customary propositions. So the logicians said: The negation of perpetuity indicates an absolute temporal. Thus the contradictory of the general customary is the absolute temporal. (Sharh al-Shamsiyya) (20) That is, an explanation of the contradiction of the time-limited and spread absolute propositions. In it: if there were no purpose in explaining their contradiction, the author would not have counted them among the considered propositions nor explained their converse, while he stated that the two time-limiteds convert to absolute general, and the contradictory of the composite is a concept wavering between the contradictories of its two parts. So it is necessary to mention their contradiction so that the proof by contradiction is completed. The point in not mentioning them is that when he mentioned that the contradictory of essential necessity is essential possibility, and the contradictory of descriptive necessity is descriptive possibility, it is known from that that the contradictories of time-limited necessity and spread necessity are possibility at a specific time and possibility at some time. Thus the contradictories of the two absolute time-limiteds are temporal possible and spread possible. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (21) Therefore the author (al-Katibi) did not mention them in the treatise in the chapter on modalities either. Rather, the author (here) mentions them as an introduction to mentioning time-limited and spread, for they are as previously: the two absolute time-limiteds restricted by essential non-perpetuity. But in the chapter on simple conversion, he only mentioned the two time-limiteds (waqtiyya and muntashira), not the two absolute time-limiteds, as appears from some verifying commentaries. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (22) His saying 'think' is perhaps an indication that he should have mentioned their contradictories just as he mentioned their identities. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of contradictions for other modal propositions. Point (18) clarifies that the general customary proposition ('urfiyya 'āmma) does not have the general absolute as its contradictory, just as the absolute general is not the contradictory of the perpetual. The author notes that the temporal possible and absolute were omitted from the modalities chapter deliberately to indicate they are not standard propositions. Point (19) explains that the contradictory of perpetuity (dawām) is the negation of perpetuity, which logicians interpret as an absolute temporal (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa), making that the contradictory of the general customary. Point (20) addresses the time-limited (waqtiyya) and spread (muntashira) absolute propositions. The author argues they must have contradictories for the proof by contradiction to work. The key insight is that since essential necessity contradicts essential possibility, and descriptive necessity contradicts descriptive possibility, then time-limited necessity (waqtiyya ḍarūriyya) contradicts possibility at a specific time, and spread necessity (muntashira ḍarūriyya) contradicts possibility at some time. Thus the contradictories of the absolute time-limited propositions are the temporal possible and spread possible. Point (21) explains that al-Katibi omitted them in the modalities chapter but mentions them here as a prelude. Point (22) hints that the author should have stated these contradictories explicitly. The lesson clarifies the logical relationships and the reason for omissions.

الدائمة المطلقة

The contradictory of 'perpetual as long as subject exists' is 'true at some time' – the absolute temporal.
